The First Proof: Time Specificity Indicates Createdness
First proof: past time specificity implies createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the word 'idh' (when) in the Quran indicates that the speech is specific to a past time, and why that entails createdness.

Core Idea
What is bound to time is temporal — even divine speech.

This lesson examines the first of three scriptural proofs for the createdness of the Quran. By analyzing the verse 'When your Lord said to the angels,' Sanusi argues that the word 'idh' pins the speech to a past moment, and anything so tied to time must be temporal. Let's see how this reasoning works.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English together, noting how the proof is constructed.
2
Identify the key term: 'idh'
Focus on the word 'idh' as a past time particle. Understand why that implies specificity.
3
Follow the logical steps
The proof moves from time specificity to createdness. See how the commentary elaborates on past, present, and future.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider: If Allah's speech is tied to a time, can it be eternal? Why or why not?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : ( قوله تعالى : ( وإذ قال ربك للملائكة ﴾ [ الحجر : ۲۸] وإذ ظرف زمان ماض فيكون قوله الواقع في هذا الظرف مختصا بزمان معین ( ولمختص بزمان معين محدث. الرابع: كتاب أحكمت آياته ثم فصلت فإنه يدل على أن القرآن مركب من الآيات التي هي أجزاء متعاقبة فيكون حادثاً، وكذا قوله تعالى : لو إنا أنزلناه قرآناً عربياً ﴾ [الشعراء : ٢ ] ، يدل على أن كلام الله تعالى قد يكون عربياً تارة وعبرياً أخرى، فيكون متغيراً وذلك دليل حدوثه. الخامس: حتى يسمع كلام الله فإنه يدل على أن كلامه مسموع فيكون حادثاً لأن المسموع يكون حرفاً وصوتاً. قوله: (والمختص بزمان معين (محدث) أما المختص بالحال والاستقبال فظاهر وأما المختص بالماضي فلان الانتفاء في الحال أو الاستقبال ينافي القدم لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه. قوله: (الرابع) كتاب أحكمت آياته ثم فصلت قال ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنه أحكمت أي لم ينسخ بكتاب كما نسخت الكتب والشرائع به ثم فصلت تثبت بالأحكام والحلال والحرام وفيه أقوال أخر. قوله: (وعبرياً أخرى دلالة الآية الكريمة على أن كلام الله تعالى قد لا يكون عربياً ظاهر فإن الذوق السليم يفهم من التخصيص ذلك، وأما دلالته على أنه قد يكون عبرياً أخرى فبضم أن التورية أيضاً كلامه بالاتفاق على أن المراد قد يكون عبرياً مثلاً فإن المقصود هاهنا مجرد الدلالة على التغير.
The third: His saying, may He be exalted, 'And when your Lord said to the angels' [al-Hijr 28]; 'when' is a past time particle, so His speech occurring in this time particle is specific to a particular time, and what is specific to a particular time is temporal (muhdath). The fourth: 'A book whose verses were perfected and then detailed' – it indicates that the Quran is composed of verses that are successive parts, so it is temporal; similarly His saying, 'Indeed, We have sent it down as an Arabic Quran' [al-Shu'ara 2] indicates that the speech of Allah may sometimes be Arabic and sometimes Hebrew, so it is changeable, and that is evidence of its temporality. The fifth: 'Until he hears the speech of Allah' – it indicates that His speech is heard, so it is temporal, because what is heard consists of letters and sounds. The saying: 'What is specific to a particular time is temporal; as for what is specific to the present or future, it is apparent, and as for what is specific to the past, because non-existence in the present or future contradicts eternity, since what is proven eternal is impossible to cease.' The saying: 'The fourth: A book whose verses were perfected and then detailed' – Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Perfected' meaning it was not abrogated by another book as scriptures and laws were abrogated by it, then 'detailed' meaning it was confirmed with rulings, halal, and haram; there are other sayings. The saying: 'and Hebrew another' – the indication of the noble verse that the speech of Allah may not be Arabic is apparent, for sound taste understands that from the specification; as for its indication that it may be Hebrew another time, it is by adding that the Torah is also His speech by agreement, meaning it may be Hebrew, for example, for the purpose here is merely to indicate change.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents three scriptural proofs for the createdness of the Quran. The first proof (third) uses the verse 'When your Lord said to the angels' to argue that the word 'when' (idh) indicates a past time, and since the speech is placed in that time, it is specific to a particular moment, and anything specific to a particular moment is temporal. The commentary clarifies that specificity to the past, present, or future all imply contingency. The second proof (fourth) argues from the description of the Quran as 'perfected and detailed,' implying composition of successive parts, which indicates createdness. It also cites the verse 'We sent it down as an Arabic Quran' to show that Allah's speech can change between languages, which is evidence of contingency. The commentary explains that Ibn Abbas interpreted 'perfected' as not abrogated, and 'detailed' as containing rulings. The third proof (fifth) uses the phrase 'until he hears the speech of Allah' to argue that because speech is heard, it consists of letters and sounds, which are temporal. The commentary on the fourth proof adds that the possibility of the Quran being in another language (Hebrew) indicates change, which implies createdness.

Key Terms
ظرف الزمان
المختص

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why does the word 'idh' indicate createdness?
A) Because it is a past time particle, so the speech is specific to a past time, and anything specific to a time is temporal.
B) Because the word 'idh' is itself created.
C) Because the angels were created.

Key Takeaway
The first proof establishes that if divine speech is tied to a specific time, it cannot be eternal and must be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Quran is created and time-bound, does that diminish its authority in your heart, or does it make the revelation more intimate — a specific message from God to humanity at a precise moment? How will this change the way you approach its commands today?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you remember that its words entered time to speak directly to your situation? Will you now listen to each verse as if it were revealed just for you, this very moment?
?
If the Quran were eternal, it might feel distant and abstract. But this proof shows it is temporal — it entered history to shake you. Have you been treating the Quran as a living address from God, or as a historical document? What will you do differently starting now?

The Second Proof: Composition and Linguistic Change
Second proof: composition and linguistic change indicate temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran's composition of successive parts and its changeability between languages demonstrate its createdness.

Core Idea
Composition and change are signs of contingency.

This lesson presents the second proof, which draws from two characteristics of the Quran: its composition of verses in sequence, and its ability to be expressed in different languages. Both features are hallmarks of created things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read both the Arabic and translation, noticing the two subproofs: composition and linguistic change.
2
Understand the composition argument
The Quran is described as 'a book whose verses were perfected and then detailed,' implying it is composed of parts, thus temporal.
3
Understand the language change argument
The verse 'We sent it down as an Arabic Quran' implies it could also be Hebrew, showing changeability.
4
Review the commentary
The commentary by Ibn Abbas and the explanation of change reinforce the point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third: His saying, may He be exalted, 'And when your Lord said to the angels' [al-Hijr 28]; 'when' is a past time particle, so His speech occurring in this time particle is specific to a particular time, and what is specific to a particular time is temporal (muhdath). The fifth: 'Until he hears the speech of Allah' – it indicates that His speech is heard, so it is temporal, because what is heard consists of letters and sounds. The saying: 'The fourth: A book whose verses were perfected and then detailed' – Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'Perfected' meaning it was not abrogated by another book as scriptures and laws were abrogated by it, then 'detailed' meaning it was confirmed with rulings, halal, and haram; there are other sayings. The saying: 'and Hebrew another' – the indication of the noble verse that the speech of Allah may not be Arabic is apparent, for sound taste understands that from the specification; as for its indication that it may be Hebrew another time, it is by adding that the Torah is also His speech by agreement, meaning it may be Hebrew, for example, for the purpose here is merely to indicate change.

Commentary
This passage presents three scriptural proofs for the createdness of the Quran. The first proof (third) uses the verse 'When your Lord said to the angels' to argue that the word 'when' (idh) indicates a past time, and since the speech is placed in that time, it is specific to a particular moment, and anything specific to a particular moment is temporal. The commentary clarifies that specificity to the past, present, or future all imply contingency. The second proof (fourth) argues from the description of the Quran as 'perfected and detailed,' implying composition of successive parts, which indicates createdness. It also cites the verse 'We sent it down as an Arabic Quran' to show that Allah's speech can change between languages, which is evidence of contingency. The commentary explains that Ibn Abbas interpreted 'perfected' as not abrogated, and 'detailed' as containing rulings. The third proof (fifth) uses the phrase 'until he hears the speech of Allah' to argue that because speech is heard, it consists of letters and sounds, which are temporal. The commentary on the fourth proof adds that the possibility of the Quran being in another language (Hebrew) indicates change, which implies createdness.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is used as evidence in the second proof?
A) The Quran's miraculous nature
B) The Quran is composed of successive parts
C) The Quran was revealed over time
D) The Quran is only in Arabic

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that the Quran's structure of parts and its linguistic variability are signs of its createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Knowing that the Quran's very structure of verses and its translatability point to its createdness, how does this affect the way you approach its verses? Are you reading it as the temporal speech of God that addresses you right now, or as an abstract eternal text? What would change in your recitation if you truly internalized that these words came into existence in time?
?
If the Quran's ability to be translated into other languages is a sign of its createdness, does that humble you when you encounter translations? Does it make you more grateful that God chose to communicate in a form that can adapt to human languages, or do you cling solely to the Arabic as if the meaning cannot survive translation? Search your heart: which reflects belief in its createdness?
?
The proof argues that anything composed of parts cannot be eternal—it must have a beginning. Look at your own life: what are you treating as 'eternal' in your priorities or attachments that is actually composite and temporal? How does this lesson's logic expose the folly of relying on created things as if they will never pass away?

The Third Proof: The Quran is Heard
Third proof: audibility implies letters and sounds, hence createdness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the Quran being audible (heard) implies it consists of letters and sounds, which are temporal, thus proving its createdness.

Core Idea
What is heard cannot be eternal — letters and sounds are created.

The third proof focuses on the sensory experience of the Quran. Since the Quran is described as something that can be heard, it must be composed of letters and sounds, which are attributes of created speech. This lesson unpacks that concise argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the brief passage
Read the single sentence of the proof and its translation.
2
Unpack the logic
The proof moves from 'heard' to 'letters and sounds' to 'temporal,' because letters and sounds are physical and thus created.
3
Consider the implications
If the Quran is heard, it must be in the form of sound, which is created. Therefore, the Quran is created.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear the Quran recited, do you ever pause to consider that the very letters and sounds you are hearing are not eternal but created? How does that shift your focus from the recitation to the One who sent the message? Commit to listening to one verse today with this awareness.
?

Because if the sounds are created, then the miracle lies in the meaning, not the medium. Which part of the Quran do you really worship—the words themselves or the message they convey? Examine your heart: are you more attached to the specific Arabic words as they are recited, or to the command they carry?

The Sixth Proof: Quran's Miraculous Nature and Concurrency
Sixth proof with Hanbali objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sixth proof for the createdness of the Quran: that its miraculous nature must be concurrent with the claim of prophethood, and how the Hanbali response challenges this reasoning.

Core Idea
A miracle must accompany the claim to verify it; if eternal, it cannot be specific.

The Quran is universally acknowledged as miraculous, but what does that mean for its temporality? This lesson examines the argument that a miracle must occur at the same time as the prophetic claim to serve as verification. The Hanbali school offers a counterview that the miraculous nature itself suffices.

Guided Path
1
Understand the premise
The Quran is a miracle by consensus. This is the starting point.
The term 'معجز' means miracle, something that cannot be matched.
2
Analyze the requirement of concurrency
The proof argues that a miracle must be concurrent with the claim; otherwise, it could belong to anyone.
3
Draw the conclusion
If the Quran is a miracle that validates Muhammad's claim, it must be temporal, arising at the time of his claim.
This leads to the createdness of the Quran.
4
Examine the Hanbali response
The Hanbalis argue that the miraculousness is in its appearance at the Prophet's hand; concurrency with the claim is fulfilled simply because it appeared then, not that its essence is temporal.
The objector does not demand the Quran's essence to be temporal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أنه أي القرآن (معجز) إجماعاً ( ويجب مقارنته أي مقارنة المعجز ( للدعوى ) حتى يكون تصديقاً للمدعي في دعواه فيكون حادثا حدوثها ) وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن مقارناً لها حادثاً معها بل يكون قديماً سابقاً عليها فلا اختصاص له ،به أي بذلك المدعى وتصديقه. السابع : أنه ( أعني القرآن موصوف بأنه ( منزل وتنزيل وذلك يوجب حدوثه لاستحالة الانتقال بالإنزال والتنزيل على صفاته القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى. الثامن : ( قوله عليه السلام في دعائه ( يا رب القرآن العظيم ويا رب طه ويس) فالقرآن مربوب كلاً وبعضاً والمربوب (محدث) اتفاقاً. قوله: (السادس أنه معجزة إلخ) للحنابلة أن يقولوا معنى إعجازه أنه ظهر في يد النبي عليه السلام ولم يظهر في يد غيره فيكفي مقارنة ظهوره فلا يلزم حدوث ذاته. قوله: (على صفاته القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى) إذ لا خفاء في امتناع نزول المعنى القديم القائم بذاته تعالى بخلاف اللفظ فإنه وإن كان عرضاً لا يزول عن محله، لكن قدينزل الجسم الحامل له فيوصف اللفظ لذلك بالنزول ولو مجازاً.
The sixth: That is, the Quran is a miracle (mu'jiz) by consensus, and it must be concurrent with the claim, so that it is a verification of the claimant in his claim; thus it becomes temporal with the temporality of the claim. Otherwise, that is, if it is not concurrent with it, being temporal with it, but rather is eternal prior to it, then there is no specificity to that claimant and his verification. The seventh: That is, the Quran is described as 'sent down' (munzal) and 'revelation' (tanzil), and that necessitates its temporality because it is impossible for descent and revelation to occur upon His eternal attributes subsisting in His essence, exalted is He. The eighth: His saying, peace be upon him, in his supplication: 'O Lord of the Great Quran, and O Lord of Ta-Ha and Ya-Sin' – thus the Quran is a thing possessed of a lord (marbub) in its entirety and in its parts, and the thing possessed of a lord is temporal by agreement. The saying: 'The sixth: that it is a miracle, etc.' – The Hanbalis may say: The meaning of its miraculousness is that it appeared at the hand of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and did not appear at the hand of another, so the concurrence of its appearance suffices, and thus the temporality of its essence is not necessitated. The saying: 'Upon His eternal attributes subsisting in His essence, exalted is He' – for there is no concealment of the impossibility of the descent of the eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, as opposed to the word (lafz), for even though it is an accident that does not depart from its locus, the body that carries it may descend, and thus the word is described as descending, even figuratively.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents three further proofs for the createdness of the Quran. The sixth proof: The Quran is a miracle by consensus, and a miracle must be contemporaneous with the claim of prophethood in order to verify the claimant. If the Quran were eternal and preceded the claim, it would not be specific to that claimant. Therefore, it must be temporal, arising at the time of the claim. The commentary objects that the Hanbalis hold that the miraculousness lies in its appearance at the hand of the Prophet, which is sufficient, and does not require the Quran itself to be temporal. The seventh proof: The Quran is described as 'sent down' (munzal) and 'revelation' (tanzil), which imply descent and change, impossible for an eternal attribute of Allah. The commentary clarifies that the eternal meaning cannot descend, but the verbal utterance (lafz) can be described as descending if the body carrying it descends, even metaphorically. The eighth proof: In a prophetic supplication, the Quran is addressed as 'marbub' (something having a lord), which implies it is a created object, since everything having a lord is created by agreement.

Key Terms
معجز
مقارنة
الدعوى
تصديق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the sixth proof, why must a miracle be concurrent with the claim?
A) To show that the miracle is eternal
B) To ensure specificity to the claimant and verification
C) To prove the miracle is physical
D) To avoid consensus

Key Takeaway
The sixth proof argues that the Quran's miraculous nature requires concurrency with the prophetic claim, necessitating its createdness, but the Hanbalis reinterpret miraculousness as tied to appearance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The Hanbalis argued that the Quran's miraculousness lies in its appearance with the claim, not its essence. Are you more attached to the form of religious expressions or to the timeless truth they convey? Can you separate the two and still hold them together?
?
When theological debates like this arise, do you dismiss them as irrelevant or do you allow them to deepen your reliance on the Quran's divine origin? What would it cost you to take a side in this debate?

The Seventh Proof: Quran Described as 'Sent Down'
Seventh proof relying on 'sent down' description

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the seventh proof for the createdness of the Quran: that being described as 'sent down' implies temporality, because descent cannot apply to an eternal attribute of Allah.

Core Idea
Descent implies change; eternal attributes do not descend.

The Quran is frequently described as 'sent down' (munzal) and 'revelation' (tanzil). This lesson examines how this description logically entails temporality, since descent involves change and is impossible for Allah's eternal attributes. A clarification distinguishes between the eternal meaning and the verbal form.

Guided Path
1
Identify the description
The Quran is called 'sent down' (munzal) and 'revelation' (tanzil) in the Quran itself.
For example, 'And We have sent down to you the Book'.
2
Analyze the implication
Descent (inzal) implies movement or transition, which is impossible for an eternal attribute of Allah.
Eternal attributes are unchanging.
3
Draw the conclusion
Therefore, the Quran cannot be an eternal attribute; it must be temporal (created).
4
Examine the clarification
The commentary notes that the eternal meaning cannot descend, but the verbal utterance (lafz) can be described as descending when the body carrying it descends, even metaphorically.
This preserves the possibility that the eternal meaning is not affected.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادس : أنه أي القرآن (معجز) إجماعاً ( ويجب مقارنته أي مقارنة المعجز ( للدعوى ) حتى يكون تصديقاً للمدعي في دعواه فيكون حادثا حدوثها ) وإلا ) أي وإن لم يكن مقارناً لها حادثاً معها بل يكون قديماً سابقاً عليها فلا اختصاص له ،به أي بذلك المدعى وتصديقه. السابع : أنه ( أعني القرآن موصوف بأنه ( منزل وتنزيل وذلك يوجب حدوثه لاستحالة الانتقال بالإنزال والتنزيل على صفاته القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى. الثامن : ( قوله عليه السلام في دعائه ( يا رب القرآن العظيم ويا رب طه ويس) فالقرآن مربوب كلاً وبعضاً والمربوب (محدث) اتفاقاً. قوله: (السادس أنه معجزة إلخ) للحنابلة أن يقولوا معنى إعجازه أنه ظهر في يد النبي عليه السلام ولم يظهر في يد غيره فيكفي مقارنة ظهوره فلا يلزم حدوث ذاته. قوله: (على صفاته القديمة القائمة بذاته تعالى) إذ لا خفاء في امتناع نزول المعنى القديم القائم بذاته تعالى بخلاف اللفظ فإنه وإن كان عرضاً لا يزول عن محله، لكن قدينزل الجسم الحامل له فيوصف اللفظ لذلك بالنزول ولو مجازاً.
The sixth: That is, the Quran is a miracle (mu'jiz) by consensus, and it must be concurrent with the claim, so that it is a verification of the claimant in his claim; thus it becomes temporal with the temporality of the claim. Otherwise, that is, if it is not concurrent with it, being temporal with it, but rather is eternal prior to it, then there is no specificity to that claimant and his verification. The seventh: That is, the Quran is described as 'sent down' (munzal) and 'revelation' (tanzil), and that necessitates its temporality because it is impossible for descent and revelation to occur upon His eternal attributes subsisting in His essence, exalted is He. The eighth: His saying, peace be upon him, in his supplication: 'O Lord of the Great Quran, and O Lord of Ta-Ha and Ya-Sin' – thus the Quran is a thing possessed of a lord (marbub) in its entirety and in its parts, and the thing possessed of a lord is temporal by agreement. The saying: 'The sixth: that it is a miracle, etc.' – The Hanbalis may say: The meaning of its miraculousness is that it appeared at the hand of the Prophet, peace be upon him, and did not appear at the hand of another, so the concurrence of its appearance suffices, and thus the temporality of its essence is not necessitated. The saying: 'Upon His eternal attributes subsisting in His essence, exalted is He' – for there is no concealment of the impossibility of the descent of the eternal meaning subsisting in His essence, as opposed to the word (lafz), for even though it is an accident that does not depart from its locus, the body that carries it may descend, and thus the word is described as descending, even figuratively.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents three further proofs for the createdness of the Quran. The sixth proof: The Quran is a miracle by consensus, and a miracle must be contemporaneous with the claim of prophethood in order to verify the claimant. If the Quran were eternal and preceded the claim, it would not be specific to that claimant. Therefore, it must be temporal, arising at the time of the claim. The commentary objects that the Hanbalis hold that the miraculousness lies in its appearance at the hand of the Prophet, which is sufficient, and does not require the Quran itself to be temporal. The seventh proof: The Quran is described as 'sent down' (munzal) and 'revelation' (tanzil), which imply descent and change, impossible for an eternal attribute of Allah. The Eighth Proof: Quran Called 'Marbub' in Prayer
Eighth proof from Quran being addressed as 'marbub'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the eighth proof for the createdness of the Quran: that it is called 'marbub' (having a lord) in a prophetic supplication, which implies it is created by consensus.

Core Idea
Everything that has a lord must be created.

In a well-known prayer, the Prophet addressed the Quran as 'O Lord of the Quran', and similarly for Surahs Ta-Ha and Ya-Sin. This lesson explores the implication: if the Quran is something that has a Lord (marbub), then it must be a created entity, since everything other than Allah is created by consensus.

Guided Path
1
Understand the prophetic prayer
The Prophet said in supplication: 'O Lord of the Great Quran, O Lord of Ta-Ha and Ya-Sin.'
This prayer is authentic.
2
Analyze the term 'marbub'
The Quran is referred to as something having a Lord (marbub), meaning it is a possessed entity under Lordship.
'Marbub' is the passive participle of 'rabb' (Lord).
3
Draw the conclusion
Everything that has a lord (marbub) is created (muhdath) by agreement among Muslims.
This is a direct proof from the Prophet's words.

Third Argument: Prophetic Testimony and the Response to Circularity
Proof of God's truthfulness from prophetic consensus and answer to circularity objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for God's truthfulness based on the consensus of prophetic testimony and how the objection of circularity is answered by distinguishing practical confirmation from verbal confirmation.

Core Idea
The prophet's testimony is necessary knowledge; miracles provide immediate practical confirmation.

This passage presents the third proof of God's truthfulness: it relies on the unanimous prophetic testimony that God is truthful. An objector raises a circularity problem, but the response shows that the miracle is a practical confirmation that breaks the cycle by operating in the realm of customary indication, not verbal truth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of argument, objection, and response become clear.
Notice the key terms: خبر النبي, تصديق فعلي, دلالة عادية, الدور.
2
Identify the argument's structure
The passage has three parts: (1) the argument itself – the prophet's report that God is truthful is known by necessity from religion; (2) the objection – that this creates a circularity; (3) the response – the miracle is a practical confirmation, not a verbal statement, and its indication is customary.
Mark where each part begins and ends in the text.
3
Understand the response to circularity
The objector claims that establishing God's truthfulness through the prophet's truthfulness is circular because the prophet's truthfulness is known through God's confirmation. The response says that the confirmation by miracle is a practical (فعلي) act, not a verbal statement, and its indication is customary (عادية), not logical. This means the miracle directly indicates God's confirmation without requiring God's verbal truthfulness, thus breaking the circle.
Customary indication (دلالة عادية) means it is based on observable patterns, not necessary logical entailment.
4
Reflect on the concept of practical confirmation
Ask yourself: How does a miracle function as a practical confirmation? Why does it not require a verbal statement from God about the prophet's truthfulness? Reflect on examples of customary signs in daily life.
This connects to the broader kalam framework of miracles as signs of prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : وعليه الاعتماد ) لصحته ودلالته على الصدق في الكلام النفسي واللفظي معا ) خبر النبي عليه الصلاة والسلام). بكونه صادقاً في كلامه كله ( وذلك ) أي خبره عليه السلام بصدقه ( مما يعلم بالضرورة من الدين فلا حاجة إلى بيان إسناده ) وصحته ولا إلى تعيين ذلك الخبر بل نقول : تواتر عن الأنبياء عليهم السلام كونه تعالى صادقاً كما تواتر عنهم كونه تعالى متكلماً ( فإن قيل : صدق النبي إنما يعلم بتصديقه تعالى إياه و (إنما يدل تصديقه ) إياه، ( على الصدق ( أي صدق النبي ( إذا امتنع عليه ( تعالى ( الكذب ) ووجب يكون كلامه صدقاً، فصدق النبي إنما يعرف بصدق الله تعالى (فيلزم الدور) إذا ثبت صدقه تعالى بصدق النبي كما فعلتم ( قلنا : التصديق بالمعجزة ) تصديق فعلي لا قولي ودلالتها على التصديق دلالة عادية لا يتطرق إليها شبهة كما ستقف عليه
The third: and it is the reliance (for its soundness and its indication of truth in both psychological and verbal speech) on the report of the Prophet (peace be upon him) that he is truthful in all his speech. And that (i.e., his report of his truthfulness) is known by necessity from religion, so there is no need to clarify its chain or authenticity, nor to specify that report. Rather, we say: it is mutawatir from the prophets (peace be upon them) that God is truthful, just as it is mutawatir from them that God speaks. If it is said: the truthfulness of the prophet is only known by God's confirmation of him, and His confirmation indicates the truthfulness of the prophet only if lying is impossible for God and His speech must be true. So the truthfulness of the prophet is known only through God's truthfulness, so circularity follows if we establish God's truthfulness through the prophet's truthfulness, as you did. We say: the confirmation by miracle is a practical confirmation, not a verbal one, and its indication of confirmation is a customary indication that is not subject to any doubt, as you will come to know.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents the third argument in favor of God's truthfulness. The argument relies on the prophet's testimony that God is truthful. The commentator asserts that this is known by necessity from religion, being transmitted via multiple chains (tawatur) just like the fact that God speaks. An objection is raised: this creates circularity because the prophet's truthfulness is known through God's confirmation (miracles), and God's confirmation indicates the prophet's truthfulness only if God is truthful. Thus, establishing God's truthfulness through the prophet's truthfulness, and vice versa, is circular. The response is that the confirmation by miracle is a practical (act) confirmation, not a verbal statement. Its indication of the prophet's truthfulness is a customary (not logical) indication, and it is not subject to doubt. This breaks the circle because the miracle is an immediate sign that does not require God's verbal truthfulness.

Key Terms
خبر النبي
تصديق فعلي
دلالة عادية
الدور

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is irremediably circular because it uses the prophet's truthfulness to prove God's truthfulness and vice versa.
Correction: The response shows that the miracle is a practical confirmation that does not require God's verbal truthfulness, thus breaking the circle. The prophet's truthfulness is known through the miracle directly, and from that we infer God's truthfulness.

Checkpoint
How does the response to the circularity objection avoid the circle?
A) By denying that the prophet's truthfulness is needed
B) By stating that the miracle is a practical confirmation, not a verbal statement
C) By claiming that the prophet's report is self-authenticating
D) By relying on reason alone

Key Takeaway
The third argument uses prophetic consensus to prove God's truthfulness, and the circularity objection is resolved by distinguishing practical confirmation from verbal confirmation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly live as if the prophets' testimony about God's truthfulness is 'necessary knowledge' in your heart, or do you sometimes reduce it to an academic belief that doesn't shape your actions? Commit today to recite one prophetic report about God's truthfulness and ask yourself how it changes your trust in Him this week.
?
When you face a trial that shakes your certainty in God's promises, do you look for the 'practical confirmation' of His faithfulness in the consistency of His prophets and the natural order, or do you demand a verbal sign? Identify one area where you need to rely on the customary indication of God's truthfulness rather than a direct statement.
?
The response to circularity here breaks an intellectual impasse. Is there a circular doubt in your own life—something you keep questioning about God or His messengers—that you can resolve by seeing the practical signs He has already given? Write down one such doubt and the practical confirmation that addresses it.

Interpreting the Hadith of God's Hand
The author provides two interpretations of the hadith of God's hand to avoid anthropomorphism.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how classical Islamic theologians interpret anthropomorphic hadiths by either affirming without likeness or metaphorical interpretation.

Core Idea
God's hand is either affirmed as unlike any hand or understood as a metaphor for divine management.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif tackles a famous hadith about God placing His hand on the Prophet's shoulders during the Night Journey. To protect divine transcendence, the author presents two valid interpretations used by Sunni scholars. Let's explore them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author introduces the hadith and immediately rejects a literal limb.
The word 'كف' can mean palm or hand.
2
Identify the two interpretations
Look for the two sayings introduced by 'فقيل' and 'وقيل'. The first affirms a palm unlike palms, the second interprets it as management.
These represent the two main Sunni approaches: tafwid and ta'wil.
3
Understand the deeper meaning
Reflect on why the author mentions 'برد' (cold) as spirit and comfort. This shows the hadith is about divine gentleness, not a physical hand.
The Prophet's experience was spiritual, not sensory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ورد في حديث ليلة كفه المعراج وضع بين كتفي فوجدت بردها في كبدي ولا يجوز إثبات الجارحة كما ذهبت إليه المشبهة فقيل : هو موصوف بكف لا كالكفوف، وقيل: مؤول بالتدبير يقال : فلان في كف فلان أي في تدبيره فالمقصود من الحديث بيان طافه في تدبيره له وبيان أنه وجد روح طافه لأن البرد يطلق على كل روح وراحة
And it has been reported in the hadith of the night of the Mi'raj: 'He placed His palm between my shoulders, and I found its coolness in my liver.' It is not permissible to affirm a limb, as the anthropomorphists (al-mushabbiha) have held. So it is said: He is described with a palm not like palms, and it is said: it is interpreted metaphorically as management (al-tadbir); it is said: 'So-and-so is in the palm of so-and-so', i.e., in his management. So the purpose of the hadith is to show His gentleness in His management of him, and to show that he found the spirit of His gentleness, because 'cold' (al-bard) is applied to every spirit and comfort.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage addresses the interpretation of the hadith describing God placing His hand between the Prophet's shoulders during the Night Journey. The author states that it is not permissible to affirm a literal limb (jaraha) as the anthropomorphists (mushabbiha) do. Two interpretations are given: first, that God is described with a hand that is unlike any hand (affirmation of a reality without likeness), second, that it is interpreted metaphorically as 'management' (tadbir), based on the Arabic idiom 'so-and-so is in the hand of so-and-so' meaning under his control. The purpose of the hadith is to demonstrate God's gentle management (lutf) and the spiritual comfort the Prophet experienced, since 'cold' (bard) can mean peace and rest.

Key Terms
كف
معراج
مشبهة
تدبير
برد
لطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the hadith forces belief in a physical hand for God.
Correction: Sunni scholars have two valid ways to understand it: either affirm a hand unlike any hand, or interpret it as a metaphor for divine management.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is one interpretation of God's 'hand' (kaf) in the hadith?
A) It is a literal hand like human hands.
B) It refers to God's power.
C) It is interpreted metaphorically as management (tadbir).
D) It refers to God's knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The hadith of God's hand is interpreted either by affirming without likeness or as a metaphor for God's management, preserving transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read a hadith that describes God in physical terms, does your heart instinctively recoil in denial, or are you willing to pause and seek the deeper meaning? How does your response reveal your own assumptions about God?
?
This lesson gives two valid interpretations: affirming without likeness or understanding as a metaphor. Which approach feels more honest to you, and why? Are you willing to accept both as valid without picking a side?
?
The hadith's purpose is to show God's gentle management and the Prophet's spiritual comfort. Have you ever experienced a moment where an apparent 'physical' description of God in scripture became a source of spiritual rest for you? If not, what is holding you back from that trust?

The First Proof from Moses' Request
First aspect of the proof from Moses' request, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the request of Moses (AS) is used as a rational proof for the possibility of seeing Allah, and why the metaphorical interpretation of his request is rejected based on linguistic principles.

Core Idea
Moses' request for vision proves its possibility because a prophet would not ask for something impossible.

The verse in which Moses asks to see Allah is a key proof used by Sunni theologians. In this lesson, we examine the first logical aspect (wajh) derived from this verse: Moses' act of asking itself grounds the possibility of seeing Allah. We will also engage with a prominent objection that claims Moses was merely asking for knowledge, not physical sight. By the end, you will see why the literal meaning of the verse must be retained.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first aspect. Let the author's argument unfold without interruption.
2
Understand the logical argument
Notice the dichotomy: either Moses knew vision was impossible or he didn't. Both lead to contradictions if vision were impossible.
3
Examine the objection
The objection claims Moses used 'vision' metaphorically for knowledge. Identify the proponents and the reasoning behind this interpretation.
This is a common Ash'ari interpretation.
4
Analyze the response
The response focuses on the linguistic context: the preposition 'ila' and the parallel usage of 'nazar' make the literal meaning obligatory without evidence.
5
Conclude the lesson
Reflect on why the literal meaning must be upheld and how this strengthens the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والاحتجاج به من وجهين: الأول: أن موسى عليه السلام ( سأل الرؤية ولو امتنع كونه تعالى مرئياً (لما سأل لأنه حينئذ إما أن يعلم امتناعه أو يجهله فإن علمه فالعاقل لا يطلب المحال فإنه عبث، وإن جهله فالجاهل بما لا يجوز على الله ويمتنع لا يكون نبياً كليما، وقد وصفه الله تعالى بذلك في كتابه بل ينبغي أن لا يصلح للنبوة إذا المقصود من البعثة هو الدعوة إلى العقائد الحقة والأعمال الصالحة ( الثاني : أنه ( تعالى ( علق الرؤية على استقرار الجبل واستقرار الجبل أمر ممكن في نفسه وما علق على الممكن فهو ممكن، إذ لو كان ممتنعاً لأمكن صدق الملزوم بدون صدق اللازم ) الاعتراض أما على الأول فمن وجوه الأول : أن موسى عليه السلام لم يسأل الرؤية بل تجوز بها عن العلم ) : الضروري لأنه لازمها وإطلاق اسم الملزوم على اللازم شائع )، سيما استعمال رأى بمعنى علم وأرى بمعنى أعلم فكأنه قال : اجعلني عالماً بك علماً ضرورياً، وهذا تأويل أبي الهذيل العلاف وتبعه) فيه ( الجبائي وأكثر البصريين والجواب أن الرؤية وإن استعملت للعلم لكنها إذا وصلت بإلى فبعيد جدا، والصواب أن يقال : لو كانت الرؤية المطلوبة في أرني بمعنى العلم لكان النظر المترتب عليه بمعناه أيضاً، والنظر وإن استعمل بمعنى العلم إلا أن استعماله فيه موصولاً بإلى مستبعد مخالف للظاهر قطعاً ) ومخالفة الظاهر لا تجوز إلا (لدليل ولا دليل هاهنا فوجب. قوله: ( وما علق على الممكن فهو ممكن) أورد عليه أنه يصح أن يقال : إن انعدم المعلول انعدمت العلة، والعلة قد يمتنع عدمه والسر فيه أن الارتباط بين الشرط والجزاء بحسب الوقوع الإمكان وذلك لأن إمكان الشيء ذاتي غير معلق على شيء، وأجيب بأن انعدام العلة التامة على قاعدة الإسلام غير ممتنع إذ منها تعلق القدرة والإرادة ويجوز انقطاعه وفيه أنه يشكل بالنسبة إلى الصفات وقد يجاب بأن المراد بالممكن المعلق عليه هو الذي في مرتبة الإمكان الصرف بحيث لا يشوبه امتناع لا من الذات ولا من الغير ولا شك في أن إمكان استقرار الجبل كذلك ولا كذلك انعدام المعلول فيما امتنع عدم علته وأما ما ذكر من السر فستعرف جوابه وفيه نظر لأن ارادة الله تعالى تعلقت بعدم استقراره عقيب النظر فاستحال استقراره لذلك وإن كانت استمالة بالعرض، ويمكن أن يجاب عن أصل الاعتراض بمنع صحة ذلك القول لغة فتأمل ..
And the proof by it is from two aspects: First: That Moses, peace be upon him, asked for vision. If it were impossible for Him, exalted, to be seen, he would not have asked, because then either he knew its impossibility or he was ignorant of it. If he knew it, then the rational person does not seek the impossible, for it is futile. And if he was ignorant of it, then one who is ignorant of what is not permissible for Allah and is impossible would not be a prophet, let alone the one addressed by Allah, and indeed Allah described him as such in His Book. Rather, he would not be fit for prophethood, for the purpose of sending is the call to true beliefs and righteous deeds. Second: That He, exalted, conditioned vision upon the stability of the mountain, and the stability of the mountain is in itself possible. Whatever is conditioned upon the possible is itself possible, because were it impossible, it would be possible for the antecedent to be true without the consequent. (Objection to the first from aspects; first: That Moses, peace be upon him, did not ask for vision, but employed a trope (tajawwuz) for knowledge—necessary knowledge, because it is a concomitant of it, and applying the name of the antecedent to the consequent is common, especially the use of 'ra'a' in the sense of 'knowledge' and 'ara' in the sense of 'make known.' So it is as if he said: 'Make me know You with necessary knowledge.' This is the interpretation of Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf, followed by al-Jubbai and most of the Basrans. The answer: That vision, even if used for knowledge, when connected with 'ila' (to) is very far [from that meaning]. The correct thing is to say: If the vision requested in 'arini' meant knowledge, then the 'nazar' (looking) that follows it would also be in its meaning (of knowledge). And 'nazar,' even if used for knowledge, its use connected with 'ila' is far-fetched and definitely contrary to the apparent meaning. And violating the apparent meaning is not permitted except with evidence, and there is no evidence here, so the apparent meaning is obligatory. (Regarding his saying: 'Whatever is conditioned upon the possible is itself possible,' an objection was raised against it: It is correct to say: If the effect ceases, the cause ceases; but the cause may be such that its cessation is impossible. The secret of it is that the connection between the condition and the consequent is according to the occurrence of possibility, because the possibility of a thing is essential and not dependent on anything. And it was answered: That the cessation of the complete cause according to the principle of Islam is not impossible, because the attachment of power and will is part of it, and its interruption is permissible. But there is a problem regarding the attributes. And it may be answered: That what is meant by 'possible' conditioned upon it is that which is in the rank of pure possibility, such that it is not mixed with impossibility from itself or from another. There is no doubt that the possibility of the mountain's stability is likewise, and not so the cessation of the effect where the cessation of its cause is impossible. As for what was mentioned about the secret, you will come to know its answer. And there is consideration because the will of Allah Most High was attached to its non-stability after the look, so its stability became impossible because of that, even if it is a non-essential impossibility. And it may be answered regarding the original objection by denying the correctness of that saying linguistically, so reflect.)
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage extracts two aspects (wajh) from the verse of Moses as logical proofs for the possibility of seeing God, then deals with objections to both. First aspect: Moses asked to see God. If seeing God were impossible, Moses would not have asked because either he knew it was impossible (and a rational person doesn't ask for the impossible) or he was ignorant (which would disqualify him from prophethood). Therefore, it must be possible. Second aspect: God conditioned vision on the mountain staying in place; the mountain's stability is possible, so what is conditioned on the possible is itself possible. If it were impossible, the antecedent (the condition) could be true without the consequent (vision) being true, but this is not the case. Objection to the first aspect: Some scholars (like Abu al-Hudhayl al-Allaf) claim Moses was using 'vision' metaphorically for knowledge, so he was asking for certain knowledge, not physical sight. The answer: While 'ra'a' can mean knowledge, when used with the preposition 'ila' it almost always means physical seeing. Moreover, if it meant knowledge, then the word 'nazar' (looking) that follows would also have to be metaphorical, which is even more far-fetched. Without evidence for such a stretch, the apparent meaning (physical vision) must be retained. The second aspect's objection concerns the logic of conditionals: sometimes what is conditioned on something possible can itself be impossible if the condition is impossible in practice. For example, if a cause is necessary, then its effect cannot be decoupled. The answer: The stability of the mountain is purely possible, and God's power and will can attach to it. The mountain did not stay because God willed it not to, but that does not negate its intrinsic possibility. The objection from the linguistic angle is also briefly refuted. Overall, the passage defends the Sunni position using rigorous kalam reasoning.

النبوة
المحال
التجوز
الظاهر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Moses was asking for knowledge, not physical vision
Correction: The linguistic context (preposition 'ila') indicates physical sight; without evidence, the literal meaning must be accepted.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the metaphorical interpretation of Moses' request is rejected?
A) Because prophets never use metaphors
B) Because the word 'vision' with 'ila' means physical sight, and no evidence supports the metaphor
C) Because the verse is about the afterlife
D) Because Moses was perfect

Key Takeaway
The first proof from Moses' request shows that asking for something impossible is irrational for a prophet, and the literal sense of the verse cannot be abandoned without evidence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that a prophet would not ask for the impossible. When you read the verse about Moses asking for vision, do you feel a sense of awe that this is a real possibility? Or do you treat it as a distant theological discussion? How can you make the promise of seeing Allah a driving hope in your life?
?
The refutation of the metaphorical interpretation teaches us to take Allah's words literally unless there is clear evidence. Is there a verse about the afterlife that you tend to explain away metaphorically to avoid its weight? How will you commit to accepting its literal meaning and letting it transform your actions?

The Second Proof: Condition on the Possible
Second aspect of the proof from Moses' request, with objection and answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical principle of conditioning a possible outcome on a possible event, and how the objection from necessary cause-effect relationships is refuted.

Core Idea
If vision is conditioned on the possible stability of the mountain, then vision itself must be possible.

The second aspect from the Moses verse uses the conditional: Allah says 'You will not see Me until the mountain settles.' Since the mountain's stability is possible, vision must also be possible. This lesson delves into this logical principle and addresses an objection that argues from cause-effect necessity. By the end, you will grasp why the mountain's stability remains essentially possible even though it did not occur.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage starting from 'qawluhu: wa ma 'ulliqa 'ala al-mumkin' to the end.
2
Understand the principle
The principle: if B is conditioned on A, and A is possible, then B is possible. This is defended by the contrapositive: if B were impossible, the condition could be true without the consequent.
3
Examine the objection
The objection claims that if a cause is necessary, its effect cannot exist without the cause, so conditioning on a possible cause does not guarantee the effect's possibility.
4
Analyze the responses
Multiple responses: (1) In Islam, the complete cause includes God's will which can cease, so the cause is not necessary. (2) The possibility here is 'pure possibility' without any external hindrance.
5
Reflect on the final answer
The mountain's stability is essentially possible, even though God willed it not to occur temporarily. The objection confuses essential impossibility with accidental prevention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

?

?

Do you truly trust that God can do what seems impossible to you, or have you let your limited experience define what is possible? ?

Do you live as if God's will is subject to your logic, or do you submit to the fact that His will can interrupt what you think is necessary? Confess: where do you resist God's sovereignty over your plans? Will you repent and trust His will, even when it overturns your expectations?

The Third Objection: Moses' Request for His People
The author presents and refutes the third objection about Moses' request being on behalf of his people.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third objection that Moses asked to see Allah on behalf of his people and the author's two-part refutation showing why this interpretation is unsound.

Core Idea
Moses' request was only for his people, but the refutation proves this interpretation fails.

This lesson delves into the third objection against Moses' request for vision: that he asked not for himself but because his people demanded it. The author argues this goes against the apparent meaning of the Quranic narrative and provides two key reasons. Let's examine the passage to see how the refutation unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the objection claims Moses asked for vision only to prevent his people from asking, attributing the request to himself as a teaching method.
2
Understand the first refutation
The author argues that if Moses were believed among his people, he could simply say 'this is impossible' or rebuke them; if he were not believed, his report would not be accepted. The first refutation covers both scenarios.
Consider the example of the people asking for a god.
3
Understand the second refutation
The second refutation points out that the disbelievers were not present at the time of the request; only the seventy chosen ones were there. Thus, the people would not accept Moses' report if they already denied his miracles.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Think about why the author concludes that the interpretation of al-Jahiz is contrary to the apparent meaning and lacks evidence.

( الثالث : ( من تلك الوجوه ( إنما سألها بسبب قومه ) . لا لنفسه لأنه كان عالماً بامتناعها لكن قومه اقترحوا عليه وقالوا : أرنا الله جهرة وإنما نسبها إلى نفسه في قوله : أرني ( ليمنع ) عن الرؤية ) فيعلم قومه امتناعها بالنسبة إليهم بالطريق الأولى)، وفيه مبالغة لقطع دابر اقتراحهم وفي أخذ الصاعقة لهم دلالة على استحالة المسؤول، (وهذا تأويل الجاحظ ومتبعيه والجواب أنه خلاف الظاهر) فلا بد له من دليل (و) مع ذلك ( لا يستقيم أما أولاً فلأنه لو كان) موسى (مصدقاً بينهم لكفاه ) في دفعهم ( أن يقول : هذا ممتنع بل كان يجب عليه أن يردعهم عن ما لا يليق بجلال الله (كما زجرهم و قال إنكم قوم تجهلون عند قولهم : اجعل لنا ( إلهاً كما لهم آلهة (وإلا أي وإن لم يكن مصدقاً بينهم بل كان القوم كافرين منكرين ) لصدقه ( لم يصدقوه ) أيضاً ( في الجواب ) لن تراني إخباراً عن الله تعالى لأن الكفار لم يحضروا وقت السؤال ولم يسمعوا الجواب بل الحاضرون هم السبعون المختارون فكيف يقبلون مجرداً إخباره مع إنكارهم لمعجزاته الباهرة، ( وأما ثانياً فلأنهم) لما

(The third) of those ways (is that) he asked only because of his people, not for himself, because he knew its impossibility, but his people proposed to him and said 'Show us God openly' and he attributed it to himself in his saying 'Show me' to prevent them from vision, so that his people would know its impossibility with respect to them a fortiori. In that is an exaggeration to cut off the root of their proposal, and the taking of the thunderbolt is an indication of the impossibility of the requested. This is the interpretation of al-Jahiz and his followers. The answer is that it is contrary to the apparent meaning, so it must have evidence. Yet it is not sound: First, because if Moses were believed among them, it would suffice for him to say 'This is impossible'; rather, he should have rebuked them for what is not befitting the majesty of God, as he rebuked them and said 'You are ignorant people' when they said 'Make for us a god as they have gods'. Otherwise, if he were not believed among them but the people were disbelievers denying his truthfulness, they would not believe him either in the answer 'You will not see Me' as a report from God, because the disbelievers were not present at the time of the question and did not hear the answer; rather, the present ones were the seventy chosen ones. So how could they accept merely his report while they deny his manifest miracles? Second, because they... His statement (and in the taking of the thunderbolt etc.) – the benefit of this premise appears in his statement 'and there is no indication in the thunderbolt' etc. His statement (rather, he should have rebuked them) – and in al-Kashshaf, they were believers, and Moses rebuked them and taught them the error, but they persisted and said 'We will not believe you until we see God openly' (al-Baqara 55). So he requested vision for them to learn its impossibility and remove their doubt. It was objected that it is inconceivable for believers to say to their prophet 'We will not believe you', and it was answered that it is not unlikely for a people whose nature is stubbornness. Do you not see that after they believed, they refused to accept the rulings of the Torah until God raised the mountain over them, and it was said 'If you accept what is in it, otherwise it will fall upon you'? And they said to him after they saw manifest miracles like the splitting of the sea and raising the mountain and other things, 'Go, you and your Lord, and fight; we are sitting here' (al-Ma'ida 24). It is also possible that 'We will not believe you' means the removal of certainty, in which case there is no problem. His statement (how can they accept merely his report?) – if you say: It does not follow from what was mentioned their acceptance of merely the report of Moses.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage presents the third objection: that Moses asked for vision not for himself but on behalf of his people, who had demanded to see God. The author refutes this interpretation as contrary to the apparent meaning. Two refutations are given: first, if Moses were believed, he would simply say it is impossible; if he were not believed, his report would not be accepted. Second, the people present were the chosen seventy, not the disbelievers. Several marginal comments follow: one discusses the thunderbolt as a sign, another addresses the credibility of Moses' people and the report in al-Kashshaf that they were believers, and another questions how the people could accept Moses' report if they were disbelievers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that Moses asked to see Allah on behalf of his people is a valid interpretation supported by the text.
Correction: The author argues this interpretation is contrary to the apparent meaning and is unsound for two reasons: it does not fit the context of Moses being believed or disbelieved, and the disbelievers were not present.

Checkpoint
According to the author's first refutation, what would happen if Moses were believed among his people?
A) He would simply say 'This is impossible' or rebuke them.
B) He would perform a miracle to show them.
C) They would accept his report immediately.
D) He would ask again on another occasion.

Key Takeaway
The third objection that Moses asked on behalf of his people fails because it is not supported by the Quranic context and leads to logical inconsistencies.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever twisted the clear meaning of Allah's words to avoid a difficult command, just as the objection here twists the story of Moses? What would it cost you to submit to the apparent meaning today?
?
The seventy chosen ones were already believers, yet they needed to learn submission to Allah's decree that He cannot be seen in this world. In what area of your life are you demanding a sign from Allah when you already know the truth? Will you repent and submit without a sign?
?
Moses could have simply rebuked his people, but he chose to intercede for them. When you see others in error, do you intercede for them with Allah or rebuke them harshly? Examine your heart and commit to a more merciful approach.

Fifth Objection: Ignorance or Minor Sin of the Prophet
Fifth objection arguing Moses may not have known impossibility or it was a minor sin, and refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why a prophet cannot be ignorant of fundamental divine attributes, and why asking for the impossible is a major sin, not a minor one.

Core Idea
A prophet's knowledge of Allah's attributes is essential; asking for the impossible is a major sin.

The fifth objection tries to excuse Moses by suggesting he may not have known that seeing Allah is impossible, or if he knew, it was a minor mistake. This lesson shows why both possibilities are impossible for prophets. The refutation affirms that a prophet chosen for speech must have complete knowledge, and that requesting the impossible is an act of futility and a major sin.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two prongs of the objection
The objector proposes two excuses for Moses: either he was ignorant that vision of Allah is impossible, or he knew but his request was a minor sin.
These are two separate lines of defense.
2
Examine the first refutation: prophecy requires full knowledge
The answer denies ignorance, arguing that a prophet chosen for speech must know what is possible and impossible for Allah, unlike ordinary Mu'tazilites or partial students of kalam.
This refutation links prophethood to comprehensive theological knowledge.
3
Examine the second refutation: the request is a major sin
If Moses knew, his request would be an act of futility and audacity towards Allah, which is a major sin, not minor.
Such a request implies anthropomorphism and disrespect.
4
Note the future discussion on minor sins
Even if it were considered a minor sin, the permissibility of minor sins for prophets is contested and will be addressed later.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الخامس) من تلك الوجوه ( أنه قد لا يعلم امتناع الرؤية ولا يضر ذلك في نبوته (مع العلم بالوحدانية ، لأن المقصود من وجوب معرفته عدنا هو التوصل إلى العلم بحكمته وأنه لا يفعل قبيحاً والغرض من البعثة هو الدعوة إلى أنه تعالى واحداً وأنه كلف عباده بأوامر ونواة تعريضاً لهم إلى النعيم المقيم، وذلك لا يتوقف على العلم باستحالة رؤيته وأما من جعل الوجوب شرعيا فعنده يجوز أن لا تكون شريعة موسى آمرة بمعرفة أنه تعالى يستحيل رؤيته ( أو ) يعلم موسى امتناع الرؤية و ( السؤال ) بطلها ( صغيرة لا تمتنع على الأنبياء والجواب التزام أن النبي المصطفى ( المختار ( بالتكليم في معرفة الله تعالى وما يجوز عليه ويمتنع دون آحاد المعتزلة و دون ( من حصل طرفاً من علم الكلام هي البدعة الشنعاء) والطريقة العوجاء التي لا يسلكها واحد من العقلاء ( واحتجاجنا بلزوم العبث ) على تقدير العلم بالاستحالة ( وهو مما تنزه عنه من له أدنى تمييز فضلا عن الأنبياء كيف ومثل هذا التجاسر على الله تعالى ( بطلب ما لا يجوز عليه، ويشعر بالتجسم على رأيكم ( لا) يعد من الصغائر بل من الكبائر التي يمتنتع صدورها عنهم ( و) على تقدير كون السؤال من الصغائر نقول : ( في جوازها من الأنبياء ما سيأتي ) من المنع والتفصيل
The fifth of those aspects: that he might not know the impossibility of vision, and that does not harm his prophethood, given knowledge of unity, because the purpose of the obligation of knowing Him for us is to attain knowledge of His wisdom and that He does not do evil, and the purpose of sending messengers is the call to the fact that He is one and that He charged His servants with commands and prohibitions to expose them to eternal bliss. That does not depend on knowledge of the impossibility of seeing Him. But as for one who makes the obligation legal, in his view it is possible that the law of Moses did not command knowledge that it is impossible to see Him. Or that Moses knows the impossibility of vision, and the request [for it] is a minor sin that is not impossible for prophets. The answer is the commitment that the chosen prophet, distinguished by speech, is knowledgeable of God and what is possible and impossible for Him, unlike individuals of the Mu'tazila and unlike those who have obtained a portion of kalam—it is a heinous innovation and a crooked path that no rational person follows. And our argument is based on the necessity of futility, assuming knowledge of impossibility, and that is something from which even someone with the least discernment is free, let alone prophets. How is it that such audacity towards God, asking for what is not permissible for Him, and it suggests anthropomorphism in your view, is not considered a minor sin but rather a major sin whose occurrence from them is impossible. And assuming that the request is from the minor sins, we say: regarding its permissibility for prophets, what will come later regarding prohibition and detail.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection and its refutation. The objector offers two possibilities: either Moses did not know that vision is impossible, which does not harm his prophethood because the core of prophecy is calling to tawhid and obligation, not detailed knowledge of all attributes; or he knew but committed a minor sin, which is possible for prophets. The answer decisively rejects both. It affirms that a prophet chosen for speech (Moses) must have comprehensive knowledge of God's attributes, including impossibility of vision—unlike ordinary Mu'tazilites or those who dabble in kalam, which is condemned as innovated and misguided. The request, if made knowingly, is not a minor sin but a major one implying futility (عبث) and audacity towards God, suggesting anthropomorphism. Such a request is impossible for prophets. Even if it were considered minor, the permissibility of minor sins for prophets is contested and will be discussed later. The passage thus upholds the necessity of prophetic infallibility regarding such grave matters.

النبوة (prophethood)
الوجوب (obligation)
الكبائر (major sins)
الصغائر (minor sins)
العبث (futility)
التجسيم (anthropomorphism)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A prophet could be ignorant of fundamental divine attributes or that asking for the impossible from Allah is a minor sin.
Correction: Prophets must have comprehensive knowledge of Allah's attributes, and requesting the impossible implies futility and is a major sin, incompatible with prophethood.

Checkpoint
According to the answer to the fifth objection, what is the ruling on a prophet asking God for something impossible?
A) It is a minor sin that may be forgiven.
B) It is a major sin or an act of futility, impossible for prophets.
C) It is permissible if done to test the people.
D) It depends on the prophet's intention.

Key Takeaway
A prophet's infallibility includes complete knowledge of Allah's attributes and protection from major sins like requesting the impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever made a dua that implied you knew better than Allah what is good for you? How does this lesson's insistence on 'futility' challenge your approach to prayer, and what is one specific request you need to surrender to Allah's wisdom today?
?
The passage condemns 'those who have obtained a portion of kalam' as following a crooked path. Do you study theology to draw closer to Allah or to win arguments? What would change if you genuinely sought knowledge to increase your awe of Allah rather than your intellectual pride?
?
If even a prophet must have complete knowledge of Allah's attributes, how can you excuse your own ignorance about who Allah is? What is one attribute of Allah you have not taken seriously, and how has that led you to treat Him with less reverence than He deserves?

The Fourth Argument: The Paradox of Commanding Abu Lahab to Believe
Sanusi presents the fourth argument on Abu Lahab's impossible command to believe.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the core logic of the fourth argument: why commanding Abu Lahab to believe is impossible because it would require him to believe that he does not believe, creating a self-contradictory state where faith and its absence must coexist in his heart.

Core Idea
The command for Abu Lahab to believe would force him to affirm his own disbelief—an impossible combination.

This lesson examines a nuanced theological argument about divine command and human capability. The case of Abu Lahab serves as a test case: if God has decreed that someone will not believe, can that person still be commanded to believe? The argument demonstrates the inherent impossibility of such a command due to the self-referential paradox it creates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the argument. Notice how the passage defines faith and uses divine foreknowledge.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the paradox
Faith is belief in the messenger, and the messenger brought the news that Abu Lahab will not believe. So if Abu Lahab believes, he must believe that he does not believe.
This self-reference is key.
3
Understand why it is impossible
The commentary explains that affirming something contrary to one's own knowledge is not generally impossible. The impossibility here arises because it requires the simultaneous presence of faith and its absence in the heart.
This is the specific impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع : إيمان أبي لهب مأمور به ) أي أمر بأن يؤمن دائما ( وهو ممتنع لأنه تعالى أخبر بأنه لا يؤمن والإيمان تصديق الرسول فيما علم مجيئه به ) ، ومما جاء به أنه لا يؤمن ( فيكون ) هو حال إيمانه على الاستمرار ( مأموراً بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن ويصدق بأنه لا يصدق قوله : ( لأنه تعالى أخبر بأنه لا يؤمن ليس في قوله تعالى : سيصلي ناراً ذات لهب المسد :٣]، ما يدل على ذلك لجواز أن يكون صليها للفسق على أن يحتمل أن يحمل الإيمان المنفي على إيمان الموافاة، اللهم إلا أن يستفاد أنه لا يؤمن أصلا من خبر الرسول عليه السلام وإسناد الأخبار إلى الله تعالى لأن الرسول عليه السلام ما ينطق عن الهوى إن هو إلا وحي يوحى . قوله : (وهو تصديق بما علم من نفسه خلافه ضرورة وأنه (محال) لا يخفى أن مجرد التصديق بما علم من نفسه خلافه ليس بمحال ألا ترى أن من اعتقد أن العالم قديم مأمور بأن يصدق بأنه حادث ويبدل اعتقاده إليه ولا استحالة فيه مع أنه تصديق بما وجد من نفسه خلافه، بل الاستحالة فيما نحن فيه إنما نشأ من خصوصية المقام حيث لزم فيه الجمع بين وعدمه، وهو محال بالضرورة وذلك لأن الإيمان بأنه لا يؤمن يتوقف على ثبوت عدم الإيمان فيه لأن الإيمان وعدمه من أفعال القلب، والتصديق بفعل القلب يتوقف على ثبوته في القلب فيلزم من وقوعه المكلف به الجمع بين الإيمان وعدمه . قوله : ( ولا نسلم أن هذا الخبر مما علم أبو لهب (مجيئه قيل : لا شك أن سماع هذا الخبر ممكن له وأنه مكلف بالتصديق التفصيلي على تقدير سماعه فيلزم منه جواز التكليف بالجمع النقيضين وإن لم يقع وهو المطلوب ،هاهنا، ولعل هذا هو وجه التأمل ولك أن تقول : لا نسلم أنه مكلف بالتصديق التفصيلي على تقدير العلم بهذا الخبر ويؤيده ما قال أن الحاجب من أن المكلف إذا علم أن المكلف به لا يقع لا يجوز تكليفه به فإن قلت : إذا لم يكلف لزم أن لا يعاقب والقول بأن التكليف أولاً كاف لا جهة له لأن الساقط لا يعود قلت : المعلوم يقينا معاقب بالفعل لعدم إتيانه بالتصديق الإجمالي الذي أمر به وأما أنه كان يعاقب على ذلك التقدير أيضاً فيحتاج إلى البيان، فليتأمل .
The fourth point: The faith of Abu Lahab is commanded—that is, he was commanded to believe perpetually—and it is impossible because the Exalted has informed that he will not believe, and faith is belief in the messenger regarding what he knows has come with him. And among what he has come with is that he will not believe, so he, in the state of his continuing faith, is commanded to believe that he does not believe and to affirm that he does not affirm. He said: (Because the Exalted informed that he will not believe. There is not in the statement of the Exalted: 'He will roast in a fire of flame' [al-Masad 111:3] what indicates that, because it is possible that his roasting is for disobedience, and it is possible that the negated faith is interpreted as the faith of the time of death. Unless it is inferred that he will not believe at all from the report of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and the attribution of reports to God, because the Messenger does not speak from desire; it is only revelation revealed.) He said: (And it is a affirmation of what he knows from himself contrary to it necessarily, and that it is impossible.) It is not hidden that mere affirmation of what one knows from oneself to be contrary is not impossible. Do you not see that one who believes the world is eternal is commanded to affirm that it is originated and to change his belief to it, and there is no impossibility in that, even though it is an affirmation of what he finds from himself contrary to it? Rather, the impossibility in our case arises from the specificity of the situation, where it necessitates the combination of it and its absence, which is impossible by necessity. That is because the belief that he will not believe depends on the establishment of the absence of faith in him, since faith and its absence are actions of the heart, and affirmation about an action of the heart depends on its establishment in the heart, so it would necessitate, from its occurrence as that which is commanded, the combination of faith and its absence. He said: (And we do not concede that this report is of what Abu Lahab knew of its coming.) It is said: There is no doubt that hearing this report is possible for him and that he is commanded to give specific affirmation on the assumption of hearing it, and so it would necessitate the permissibility of commanding the combination of contradictories even if it does not occur, and that is the required conclusion here. Perhaps this is the aspect of reflection. But you may say: We do not concede that he is commanded to give specific affirmation on the assumption of knowledge of this report. This is supported by what al-Hajib said: that when a commanded person knows that what he is commanded will not occur, it is not permissible to command him with it. If you say: If he is not commanded, it would necessitate that he is not punished. And the statement that the command at first is sufficient has no basis because what has fallen does not return. I say: It is known with certainty that he is punished actually for not performing the general belief that he was commanded. As for whether he would be punished on that assumption as well, that requires explanation, so reflect.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument concerning the impossibility of commanding Abu Lahab to believe. The argument states that Abu Lahab was commanded to believe perpetually, but this is impossible because God informed (via the Quran and the Prophet) that he would not believe. Faith is belief in the messenger regarding what he knows has come with him. Among the things the messenger brought is the news that Abu Lahab will not believe. Therefore, if Abu Lahab were to have faith continuously, he would be commanded to believe that he does not believe, which is a paradoxical command to affirm a falsehood about himself. The commentary then addresses several objections and clarifications. First, it questions whether the Quranic verse 'He will roast in a fire of flame' (111:3) necessarily implies that Abu Lahab will never believe. It suggests alternative interpretations: the verse might refer to his punishment for disobedience, not eternal disbelief, or the negated faith might be 'faith at the time of death' (iman al-muwafah). However, if the Prophet's report is taken as definitive revelation, then it is established that he will never believe. Second, the commentary clarifies that affirming something contrary to one's own knowledge is not inherently impossible (e.g., someone who believes the world is eternal can be commanded to believe it is created and change his view). The impossibility here is specific: because faith and its absence are states of the heart, and affirming that one lacks faith requires that faith be absent in the heart. But if the act of faith (believing) is performed, then faith is present, which contradicts the affirmation that it is absent. Thus, the command would necessitate the simultaneous combination of faith and its absence, which is impossible. Third, an objection is raised: it is not established that Abu Lahab actually knew or acknowledged this specific prophecy. The response argues that hearing the report is possible for him, and on that assumption, he would be commanded to give specific belief in it, which would entail commanding the impossible combination. But another response denies that he is commanded to give specific belief upon knowing this report, supported by the principle that if one knows a commanded act will not occur, the command is invalid. The discussion then considers the consequences: if he is not commanded, he would not be punished for that specific command, but he still would be punished for failing to perform the general belief (al-tasdiq al-ijmali) which he was commanded. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect further.

Key Terms
إيمان
تصديق
محال
تكليف
خبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that commanding something impossible is always invalid, or that the impossibility is due to divine foreknowledge alone.
Correction: The impossibility here is not due to foreknowledge alone but because the specific command would require combining faith and its absence in the same heart, which is logically impossible.

Checkpoint
Why is the command for Abu Lahab to believe considered impossible according to the fourth argument?
A) Because he was already a believer
B) Because believing that he does not believe would require combining faith and its absence
C) Because God does not command disbelievers
D) Because the command was given after his death

Key Takeaway
The command for Abu Lahab to believe is impossible because it would require the simultaneous existence of faith and its absence in his heart.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself believing one thing about yourself while your actions prove the opposite? Are there areas in your life where your inner conviction contradicts what you claim to believe? What would it take for you to honestly face that contradiction today and repent?
?
This argument shows that God's command can expose the impossibility of a person's state. Is there a command of Allah that you find difficult to reconcile with your current state? Do you dismiss it as impossible, or do you let it drive you to seek the grace to change?
?
The paradox of Abu Lahab lies in the combination of faith and its absence. In your own heart, are there beliefs you hold that actually negate other beliefs you profess? Will you ask Allah to purify your heart from such contradictions and grant you a consistent faith?
