The Fourth Argument: Objections and Responses
Sanusi addresses objections to the fourth argument and discusses punishment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objections to the fourth argument and the responses involving knowledge of prophecy and the distinction between general and specific belief.

Core Idea
Even if the command is impossible, the discussion reveals deeper principles about divine commands, human knowledge, and punishment.

This lesson continues the exploration of the fourth argument by examining objections and responses. The discussion delves into whether the report of Abu Lahab's disbelief was known to him, whether he was commanded to believe in it specifically, and the implications for punishment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Understand the objection that Abu Lahab may not have known the prophecy. The text presents a debate about whether hearing the report is sufficient for command.
Pay attention to the dialectical structure.
2
Follow the exchange
Some say hearing the report makes him obligated to specific belief, which would entail commanding the impossible. Others argue he is not commanded to specific belief on this assumption, supported by al-Hajib's principle.
Al-Hajib's principle: if one knows a commanded act will not occur, the command is invalid.
3
Consider the conclusion on punishment
The passage concludes that Abu Lahab is punished for not performing the general belief he was commanded, not for failing the impossible specific command.
Reflect on the distinction between general and specific belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الرابع : إيمان أبي لهب مأمور به ) أي أمر بأن يؤمن دائما ( وهو ممتنع لأنه تعالى أخبر بأنه لا يؤمن والإيمان تصديق الرسول فيما علم مجيئه به ) ، ومما جاء به أنه لا يؤمن ( فيكون ) هو حال إيمانه على الاستمرار ( مأموراً بأن يؤمن بأنه لا يؤمن ويصدق بأنه لا يصدق قوله : ( لأنه تعالى أخبر بأنه لا يؤمن ليس في قوله تعالى : سيصلي ناراً ذات لهب المسد :٣]، ما يدل على ذلك لجواز أن يكون صليها للفسق على أن يحتمل أن يحمل الإيمان المنفي على إيمان الموافاة، اللهم إلا أن يستفاد أنه لا يؤمن أصلا من خبر الرسول عليه السلام وإسناد الأخبار إلى الله تعالى لأن الرسول عليه السلام ما ينطق عن الهوى إن هو إلا وحي يوحى . قوله : (وهو تصديق بما علم من نفسه خلافه ضرورة وأنه (محال) لا يخفى أن مجرد التصديق بما علم من نفسه خلافه ليس بمحال ألا ترى أن من اعتقد أن العالم قديم مأمور بأن يصدق بأنه حادث ويبدل اعتقاده إليه ولا استحالة فيه مع أنه تصديق بما وجد من نفسه خلافه، بل الاستحالة فيما نحن فيه إنما نشأ من خصوصية المقام حيث لزم فيه الجمع بين وعدمه، وهو محال بالضرورة وذلك لأن الإيمان بأنه لا يؤمن يتوقف على ثبوت عدم الإيمان فيه لأن الإيمان وعدمه من أفعال القلب، والتصديق بفعل القلب يتوقف على ثبوته في القلب فيلزم من وقوعه المكلف به الجمع بين الإيمان وعدمه . قوله : ( ولا نسلم أن هذا الخبر مما علم أبو لهب (مجيئه قيل : لا شك أن سماع هذا الخبر ممكن له وأنه مكلف بالتصديق التفصيلي على تقدير سماعه فيلزم منه جواز التكليف بالجمع النقيضين وإن لم يقع وهو المطلوب ،هاهنا، ولعل هذا هو وجه التأمل ولك أن تقول : لا نسلم أنه مكلف بالتصديق التفصيلي على تقدير العلم بهذا الخبر ويؤيده ما قال أن الحاجب من أن المكلف إذا علم أن المكلف به لا يقع لا يجوز تكليفه به فإن قلت : إذا لم يكلف لزم أن لا يعاقب والقول بأن التكليف أولاً كاف لا جهة له لأن الساقط لا يعود قلت : المعلوم يقينا معاقب بالفعل لعدم إتيانه بالتصديق الإجمالي الذي أمر به وأما أنه كان يعاقب على ذلك التقدير أيضاً فيحتاج إلى البيان، فليتأمل .
The fourth point: The faith of Abu Lahab is commanded—that is, he was commanded to believe perpetually—and it is impossible because the Exalted has informed that he will not believe, and faith is belief in the messenger regarding what he knows has come with him. And among what he has come with is that he will not believe, so he, in the state of his continuing faith, is commanded to believe that he does not believe and to affirm that he does not affirm. He said: (Because the Exalted informed that he will not believe. There is not in the statement of the Exalted: 'He will roast in a fire of flame' [al-Masad 111:3] what indicates that, because it is possible that his roasting is for disobedience, and it is possible that the negated faith is interpreted as the faith of the time of death. Unless it is inferred that he will not believe at all from the report of the Messenger, peace be upon him, and the attribution of reports to God, because the Messenger does not speak from desire; it is only revelation revealed.) He said: (And it is a affirmation of what he knows from himself contrary to it necessarily, and that it is impossible.) It is not hidden that mere affirmation of what one knows from oneself to be contrary is not impossible. Do you not see that one who believes the world is eternal is commanded to affirm that it is originated and to change his belief to it, and there is no impossibility in that, even though it is an affirmation of what he finds from himself contrary to it? Rather, the impossibility in our case arises from the specificity of the situation, where it necessitates the combination of it and its absence, which is impossible by necessity. That is because the belief that he will not believe depends on the establishment of the absence of faith in him, since faith and its absence are actions of the heart, and affirmation about an action of the heart depends on its establishment in the heart, so it would necessitate, from its occurrence as that which is commanded, the combination of faith and its absence. He said: (And we do not concede that this report is of what Abu Lahab knew of its coming.) It is said: There is no doubt that hearing this report is possible for him and that he is commanded to give specific affirmation on the assumption of hearing it, and so it would necessitate the permissibility of commanding the combination of contradictories even if it does not occur, and that is the required conclusion here. Perhaps this is the aspect of reflection. But you may say: We do not concede that he is commanded to give specific affirmation on the assumption of knowledge of this report. This is supported by what al-Hajib said: that when a commanded person knows that what he is commanded will not occur, it is not permissible to command him with it. If you say: If he is not commanded, it would necessitate that he is not punished. And the statement that the command at first is sufficient has no basis because what has fallen does not return. I say: It is known with certainty that he is punished actually for not performing the general belief that he was commanded. As for whether he would be punished on that assumption as well, that requires explanation, so reflect.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument concerning the impossibility of commanding Abu Lahab to believe. The argument states that Abu Lahab was commanded to believe perpetually, but this is impossible because God informed (via the Quran and the Prophet) that he would not believe. Faith is belief in the messenger regarding what he knows has come with him. Among the things the messenger brought is the news that Abu Lahab will not believe. Therefore, if Abu Lahab were to have faith continuously, he would be commanded to believe that he does not believe, which is a paradoxical command to affirm a falsehood about himself. The commentary then addresses several objections and clarifications. First, it questions whether the Quranic verse 'He will roast in a fire of flame' (111:3) necessarily implies that Abu Lahab will never believe. It suggests alternative interpretations: the verse might refer to his punishment for disobedience, not eternal disbelief, or the negated faith might be 'faith at the time of death' (iman al-muwafah). However, if the Prophet's report is taken as definitive revelation, then it is established that he will never believe. Second, the commentary clarifies that affirming something contrary to one's own knowledge is not inherently impossible (e.g., someone who believes the world is eternal can be commanded to believe it is created and change his view). The impossibility here is specific: because faith and its absence are states of the heart, and affirming that one lacks faith requires that faith be absent in the heart. But if the act of faith (believing) is performed, then faith is present, which contradicts the affirmation that it is absent. Thus, the command would necessitate the simultaneous combination of faith and its absence, which is impossible. Third, an objection is raised: it is not established that Abu Lahab actually knew or acknowledged this specific prophecy. The response argues that hearing the report is possible for him, and on that assumption, he would be commanded to give specific belief in it, which would entail commanding the impossible combination. But another response denies that he is commanded to give specific belief upon knowing this report, supported by the principle that if one knows a commanded act will not occur, the command is invalid. The discussion then considers the consequences: if he is not commanded, he would not be punished for that specific command, but he still would be punished for failing to perform the general belief (al-tasdiq al-ijmali) which he was commanded. The passage ends with an invitation to reflect further.

Key Terms
تصديق إجمالي
تصديق تفصيلي
تكليف
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the discussion, for what is Abu Lahab punished?
A) For not performing the specific belief in the prophecy
B) For not performing the general belief he was commanded
C) For his disbelief itself
D) For not obeying the impossible command

Key Takeaway
Abu Lahab is punishable for failing to perform the general belief he was commanded, not for the impossible specific command.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What 'general belief' (al-tasdiq al-ijmali) in Allah and His messenger have you neglected, focusing instead on specific details that seem difficult? How can you renew that general faith today?
?
Abu Lahab heard the truth but refused it. Is there any truth about Islam you have known but resisted accepting fully? What is keeping you from submitting to it completely?
?
You have been commanded to believe generally, yet you may have avoided that command by arguing over specifics. This very moment, what step can you take to act on the general belief you already have?

The First Obligatory Method: Permissibility and Prophethood
First ilzāmī argument and response on permissibility and prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first compulsory argument against the Ash'arites regarding permissibility for God and the response that miracles provide customary certainty.

Core Idea
Miracles give reliable certainty even if lying is possible for God.

This lesson examines a key Mu'tazilite objection: if everything is permissible for God, then even lying is possible, which would destroy trust in prophets and miracles. The Ash'arite response, as presented here, distinguishes between the ground for God's truthfulness (not intellectual ugliness) and the customary reliability of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the first ilzāmī argument
Read the section from 'وأما الطريقان' through 'سد باب النبوة'. Understand the consequentialist objection: if God could lie, then miracles cannot distinguish true prophets.
Notice the phrase 'لو حسن من الله كل شيء' — the hypothetical starting point.
2
Identify the core problem
The argument claims that without objective ugliness, lying would be possible for God, nullifying the benefit of sending messengers and invalidating religious laws by consensus.
The phrase 'وإنه باطل إجماعاً' shows the argument assumes consensus that such a state is absurd.
3
Read the response
Read from 'والجواب' to the end of the passage. The author rejects that the impossibility of lying rests on intellectual ugliness; there can be another proof. Then a second point: the miracle's indication is customary, not dependent on the metaphysical impossibility of lying.
The response does not concede the premise; it redefines the basis of certainty.
4
Reflect on customary certainty
The author analogizes miracles to ordinary customary knowledge (e.g., that fire burns) which is certain even though its contrary is possible. This shows that certainty about prophethood does not require the impossibility of lying.
This anticipates later discussions on causality and divine custom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الطريقان الإلزاميان فأحدهما: لو حسن من الله كل شيء كما اقتضاه مذهبكم من أن القبح إنما هو لأجل النهي الذي لا يتصور في أفعاله تعالى، حسن أي لم يمتنع منه الكذب، وفي ذلك إبطال الشرائع وبعثة الرسل بالكلية، لأنه قد يكون في تصديقه للنبي بالمعجزة كاذباً ولا يمكن حينئذ تمييز النبي عن المتنبي، فلا تثبت الأحكام الشرعية وتنتفي فائدة البعثة، وإنه باطل إجماعاً. ولحسن منه أيضاً خلق المعجزة على يد الكاذب وعاد المحذور الذي هو سد باب النبوة. والجواب: إن مدرك امتناع الكذب منه تعالى عندنا ليس هو قبحه العقلي حتى يلزم من انتفاء قبحه أن لا يعلم امتناعه منه، إذ يجوز أن يكون له مدرك آخر وقد تقدم هذا في مباحث كونه تعالى متكلماً. ودلالة المعجزة على صدق المدعي عادية، فلا تتوقف على امتناع الكذب كما في سائر العلوم العادية التي ليست نقائضها ممتنعة، فنحن نجزم بصدق من ظهرت المعجزة على يده مع أن كذبه ممكن في نفسه فلا يلزم التباس، وسيأتي. وثانيهما: الإجماع على تعليل الأحكام الشرعية بالمصالح والمفاسد، ولو توقف الحسن والقبح على ورود الشرع كما زعمتم لامتنع تعليل الأحكام بها، وفي منعه سد باب القياس وتعطل أكثر الوقائع من الأحكام، وأنتم لا تقولون به. قلنا: اهتداء العقل إلى المصالح والمفاسد ليس من المقصود في شيء، كما مر من أن المصلحة والمفسدة راجعة إلى ملاءمة الغرض.
As for the two obligatory methods: the first of them is: if everything were permissible for God, as your doctrine requires, namely that ugliness is solely due to the prohibition which is inconceivable in His actions, then permissible meaning lying would not be impossible for Him, and in that is the nullification of the revealed laws and the sending of messengers entirely, because He could be lying in verifying the prophet through the miracle, and then it would not be possible to distinguish the prophet from the false claimant, so the legal rulings would not be established and the benefit of the mission would be negated, and that is false by consensus. And it would also be permissible for Him to create the miracle at the hand of the liar, and the same problem of blocking the door of prophethood would return. The answer: The ground for the impossibility of lying from Him, exalted, according to us is not its intellectual ugliness, such that from the negation of its ugliness it would follow that its impossibility from Him is not known; for it is possible that there is another ground, and this has preceded in the discussions on His being a speaker. And the indication of the miracle concerning the truthfulness of the claimant is customary, so it is not dependent on the impossibility of lying, as in other customary sciences whose contradictories are not impossible; for we are certain of the truthfulness of the one at whose hand the miracle appears, even though his falsehood is possible in itself, so no confusion follows. And it will come later. And the second of them: consensus on attributing legal rulings to benefits and harms; if good and evil were dependent on the arrival of revelation, as you claim, then it would be impossible to attribute rulings to them, and in denying that is closing the door of analogy and suspending most of the events from rulings, and you do not affirm that. We said: The intellect's guidance to benefits and harms is not at all the intended point, as has passed that benefit and harm refer to compatibility with the purpose.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage presents two compulsory (ilzāmī) arguments used by the Mu'tazila against the Ash'arites, who deny objective good and evil. The first argument: if everything is permissible for God (as Ash'arites hold), then even lying would be possible, which would undermine the reliability of miracles and prophethood. The author responds by stating that the impossibility of lying from God is known from another source (not intellectual ugliness), and that the indication of miracles is customary, so miracles reliably indicate truthfulness even if lying is possible in itself. The second argument: there is consensus that legal rulings are based on benefits and harms, which would be impossible if good and evil were not known before revelation. The author responds that the intellect's guidance to benefits and harms is not the point; benefit and harm are about compatibility with purpose, not about the intrinsic moral value that the Mu'tazila claim.

Key Terms
الإلزاميان
المدرك
الامتناع
العادية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If there is no objective moral ugliness, then we can never be certain about prophethood because God could lie.
Correction: Certainty about prophethood comes from the customary nature of miracles and an independent proof for God's truthfulness, not from the absence of logical possibility of lying.

Checkpoint
According to the response in the passage, why does a miracle reliably indicate truthfulness?
A) Because lying is impossible for God due to its intellectual ugliness.
B) Because God has covenanted never to support a liar with a miracle.
C) Because the indication of a miracle is customary, like other regularities.
D) Because consensus affirms that a miracle cannot occur at the hand of a liar.

Key Takeaway
Miracles provide customary certainty about prophethood even if God could theoretically lie, because their reliability is based on divine habit, not metaphysical necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your certainty in the truthfulness of the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him) rests on the custom of God, not on absolute logical proof. Does this disturb you? Examine your heart: have you ever secretly wished for a more 'scientific' guarantee, something that could not possibly be otherwise? What does this reliance on divine habit demand of your trust in God's consistency? Will you live today as if His custom is enough, or will you seek an impossible certainty that He has not given?
?
Imagine a situation where your faith is challenged by doubt: 'What if miracles were not enough?' This passage answers that doubt by reminding you that God's way is reliable. But are you actually living with that reliability? Do you treat the signs of God in your life—answered prayers, guidance, the very existence of revelation—as customary proofs that demand your full obedience? Or do you dismiss them as mere possibilities? Commit to one area today where you will act on this customary certainty, not wait for absolute proof.

The Second Obligatory Method: Legal Rulings and Benefits
Second ilzāmī argument and response on legal rulings and benefits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second compulsory argument against the Ash'arites regarding the basis of legal rulings in benefits and harms, and the response that the intellect's recognition of benefits is not about intrinsic moral value.

Core Idea
Benefits and harms guide rulings, but not as intrinsic moral categories.

This lesson presents the Mu'tazilite claim that if good and evil were only known through revelation, then legal rulings could not be based on benefit and harm—a position they attribute to Ash'arism. The Ash'arite response stated here distinguishes between the intellect's grasp of worldly advantage and the moral categories that require revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the second ilzāmī argument
Read the section starting with 'وثانيهما' up to 'وأنتم لا تقولون به'. Note the appeal to consensus that rulings are based on benefits and harms.
The argument assumes that if good/evil depend on revelation, then 'attributing rulings to benefits' is impossible.
2
Identify the threatened consequence
The argument claims that denying pre-revelation knowledge closes the door to analogical reasoning (qiyās) and leaves most novel cases without rulings.
This is a practical objection: without objective values, legal reasoning becomes unmoored.
3
Read the response
Read the response: 'قلنا: اهتداء العقل إلى المصالح والمفاسد ليس من المقصود في شيء'. The author reinterprets benefit and harm as compatibility with divine purpose, not as intrinsic moral facts.
The response does not deny that intellect can recognize benefits, but denies that this recognition constitutes moral knowledge.
4
Understand the redefinition
The term 'مصلحة' here is not a moral quality but a description of how an action fits the divine purpose. This allows rulings to be based on benefits while maintaining the Ash'arite view that good and evil are revealed.
This is a subtle but crucial distinction: benefit/harm are factual, not evaluative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الطريقان الإلزاميان فأحدهما: لو حسن من الله كل شيء كما اقتضاه مذهبكم من أن القبح إنما هو لأجل النهي الذي لا يتصور في أفعاله تعالى، حسن أي لم يمتنع منه الكذب، وفي ذلك إبطال الشرائع وبعثة الرسل بالكلية، لأنه قد يكون في تصديقه للنبي بالمعجزة كاذباً ولا يمكن حينئذ تمييز النبي عن المتنبي، فلا تثبت الأحكام الشرعية وتنتفي فائدة البعثة، وإنه باطل إجماعاً. ولحسن منه أيضاً خلق المعجزة على يد الكاذب وعاد المحذور الذي هو سد باب النبوة. والجواب: إن مدرك امتناع الكذب منه تعالى عندنا ليس هو قبحه العقلي حتى يلزم من انتفاء قبحه أن لا يعلم امتناعه منه، إذ يجوز أن يكون له مدرك آخر وقد تقدم هذا في مباحث كونه تعالى متكلماً. ودلالة المعجزة على صدق المدعي عادية، فلا تتوقف على امتناع الكذب كما في سائر العلوم العادية التي ليست نقائضها ممتنعة، فنحن نجزم بصدق من ظهرت المعجزة على يده مع أن كذبه ممكن في نفسه فلا يلزم التباس، وسيأتي. وثانيهما: الإجماع على تعليل الأحكام الشرعية بالمصالح والمفاسد، ولو توقف الحسن والقبح على ورود الشرع كما زعمتم لامتنع تعليل الأحكام بها، وفي منعه سد باب القياس وتعطل أكثر الوقائع من الأحكام، وأنتم لا تقولون به. قلنا: اهتداء العقل إلى المصالح والمفاسد ليس من المقصود في شيء، كما مر من أن المصلحة والمفسدة راجعة إلى ملاءمة الغرض.
As for the two obligatory methods: the first of them is: if everything were permissible for God, as your doctrine requires, namely that ugliness is solely due to the prohibition which is inconceivable in His actions, then permissible meaning lying would not be impossible for Him, and in that is the nullification of the revealed laws and the sending of messengers entirely, because He could be lying in verifying the prophet through the miracle, and then it would not be possible to distinguish the prophet from the false claimant, so the legal rulings would not be established and the benefit of the mission would be negated, and that is false by consensus. And it would also be permissible for Him to create the miracle at the hand of the liar, and the same problem of blocking the door of prophethood would return. And it will come later. And the second of them: consensus on attributing legal rulings to benefits and harms; if good and evil were dependent on the arrival of revelation, as you claim, then it would be impossible to attribute rulings to them, and in denying that is closing the door of analogy and suspending most of the events from rulings, and you do not affirm that. Commentary
This passage presents two compulsory (ilzāmī) arguments used by the Mu'tazila against the Ash'arites, who deny objective good and evil. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ash'arites deny that benefits and harms can be used in legal reasoning at all.
Correction: Ash'arites affirm that benefits and harms are relevant, but they are not intrinsic moral categories; they are factual descriptions of suitability to divine purpose, and the intellect can recognize them.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the relationship between benefit (maṣlaḥa) and purpose (gharaḍ)?
A) Benefit is the same as moral good.
B) Benefit is a moral category revealed by God.
C) Benefit refers to compatibility with a purpose.
D) Benefit is unknowable to the intellect.

Key Takeaway
Benefits and harms are factual compatibility with divine purpose, not inherent moral values, so legal rulings can be based on them without requiring pre-revelation moral knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a moral dilemma, do you instinctively weigh benefits and harms from your own perspective, or do you seek out divine purpose from revelation? What does your default approach reveal about your trust in Allah's wisdom over your own calculation? Commit today to check every decision against explicit revelation before relying on your own judgment.
?
The passage warns that denying the basis of rulings on benefits/harms would leave most events without guidance. Are there areas of your life where you act as if no ruling applies—where you feel 'suspended' without clear direction? Could it be that you have ignored the tools Allah has given, like analogy and general principles? Repent of neglecting the comprehensiveness of His law.
?
The Ash'arite response says the intellect's guidance to benefits/harms is not the intended point—the point is compatibility with divine purpose. Is your ultimate intention in your actions to achieve worldly benefit or to align with Allah's purpose? How many of your daily actions are truly motivated by pleasing Him? Write down one specific intention you will purify before your next action.

Refutation of Circularity in Defining Prophethood
Answer to the objection of circularity in defining prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Abhari resolves the circularity objection by distinguishing between verbal and internal divine speech.

Core Idea
Defining prophethood by God's speech does not cause circularity when speech is understood as temporal verbal speech.

Here we face a logical objection: if prophethood is defined by divine speech, and divine speech is proven by prophets, there is circularity. Al-Abhari breaks the circle by distinguishing two kinds of speech. Let's read his argument and see how the distinction works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Objection
Read the objection carefully: the definition of prophethood depends on divine speech, yet divine speech is established by reports of prophets. This seems like clear circularity.
Notice the tension: X is defined by Y, but Y is known through X.
2
Read al-Abhari's Answer
Now read the answer in the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two types of speech: verbal (temporal) and internal (eternal).
The answer hinges on which kind of speech is involved in defining prophethood.
3
Reflect on the Resolution
Consider how the distinction breaks the circle: prophethood depends on temporal verbal speech, which does not require prior knowledge of prophethood. The circle is avoided.
Think about why temporal speech does not create circularity while eternal speech would.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( فهو عند أهل الحق من قال الله تعالى إلخ ) قال الأبهري : النبوة عبارة عن هذا القول كونه متعلقا بالمخاطب ولما كان التعلق والمتعلق به غير قديم لا يلزم قدم النبوة وإن كان قول الله تعالى قديماً لأنها ليست عبارة عن مجرد ذلك القول بل عنه وعن تعلقه بالمتعلق الحادث وأنت خبير بأن تفسير النبي بما ذكر ينبغي أن يبنى على حدوث الألفاظ إذ قدمها التعلق أو قدمه أيضاً مع حدوث المتعلق غير معقول. فإن قلت : ذلك التفسير يفضي إلى توقف النبوة على ثبوت الكلام وقد استدل عليه فيما سبق بما تواتر من الأنبياء وهل هذا إلا دور صريح، قلت : الذي توقف عليه النبوة هو الكلام اللفظي وهو من قبيل إيجاد الأصوات والحروف الغير المتوقف على الكلام النفسي القائم به تعالى والمأخوذ من الشرع هو هذا فلا دور .
His statement: 'So according to the people of truth, it is one to whom God says etc.' Al-Abhari said: Prophethood is an expression of this statement being related to the addressee. And since the relation and the object of relation are not eternal, the eternity of prophethood does not follow, even if the statement of God is eternal, because it is not an expression of merely that statement, but of it and of its relation to the temporal object. And you are aware that the interpretation of 'nabi' as mentioned should be based on the temporality of words, because if the relation is eternal, or if it is also eternal with the temporality of the object, it is inconceivable. For if you say: That interpretation leads to prophethood being dependent on the establishment of speech, and it has been argued earlier based on the widespread reports of prophets – is this not clear circularity? I say: That on which prophethood depends is verbal speech, which is of the type of creating sounds and letters, and does not depend on internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī) subsisting in Him. And what is taken from revelation is this, so there is no circularity.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection raised against the definition of prophethood given earlier. The objection is that if prophethood is defined by God's saying 'I have sent you', then prophethood depends on the existence of divine speech. But the existence of divine speech (as a proof of revelation) is itself established by the testimony of the prophets, which would create a circular argument. The answer, given by al-Abhari, distinguishes between two kinds of speech: verbal speech (al-kalām al-lafẓī), which is the creation of sounds and letters, and internal speech (al-kalām al-nafsī), which subsists eternally in God. The prophethood depends only on the temporal, created verbal speech, not on the eternal internal speech. Since verbal speech is a created event, and the prophethood is also a temporal event, there is no circularity because the verbal speech is established independently by revelation (which comes through the prophet), but the argument is that the definition uses the verbal speech as a marker, not as a cause that requires prior knowledge of eternal speech. The distinction breaks the circle.

Key Terms
الدور
الكلام اللفظي
الكلام النفسي
النبوة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that defining prophethood by God's speech implies that prophethood is eternal or that the definition requires prior knowledge of eternal speech.
Correction: The definition refers to temporal verbal speech, not the eternal internal speech, so prophethood is a temporal event and no circularity occurs.

Checkpoint
How does al-Abhari avoid the charge of circularity in defining prophethood?
A) By asserting that prophethood is eternal
B) By distinguishing between verbal speech and internal speech
C) By denying that prophethood depends on speech
D) By claiming that speech is not needed for the definition

Key Takeaway
The distinction between verbal and internal speech resolves the apparent circularity in defining prophethood through divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider how you know the Quran is from God, have you ever been caught in a circular argument (e.g., 'I trust the Prophet because the Quran says so, and I trust the Quran because it came from the Prophet')? How does this lesson's distinction between verbal and internal speech help you break that circle and ground your faith on a sound foundation?
?
If someone challenged you by saying, 'Your definition of prophethood is circular, so your whole belief system is illogical,' would you be able to respond using the argument of this passage? Take a moment to actually formulate that response in your own words right now.
?
The scholars debated such subtle distinctions to protect the logical coherence of their faith. Are you willing to invest the same intellectual effort to understand and defend what you believe, even when it requires careful reasoning rather than simple repetition?

The Philosophers' Proof of Prophetic Knowledge of the Unseen
Philosophers' first characteristic of prophethood with proof from soul's connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical argument that a prophet's knowledge of the unseen arises from the rational soul's spiritual connection to higher intellects.

Core Idea
Prophetic knowledge is explained by the soul's innate ability to connect to celestial intellects.

The philosophers (falāsifa) present a naturalistic account of prophethood, reducing it to three distinguishing qualities. The first quality – knowledge of the unseen – is defended through a sophisticated metaphysical theory. In this passage, the author explains how the human soul, being immaterial, can relate to higher intellects and thereby receive knowledge of events. This sets the stage for understanding the philosophical approach to prophethood, which contrasts with the orthodox view of direct revelation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first characteristic
Read the passage carefully and note what the philosophers consider the first special quality of a prophet.
It is knowledge of the unseen, encompassing past, present, and future.
2
Understand the metaphysical basis
Focus on the description of human souls as immaterial and their relation to higher intellects. Why does this make knowledge of the unseen possible?
Souls are not bound by matter; they have affinity with higher immaterial intellects.
3
Examine the mirror analogy
The author uses an analogy of two mirrors. Consider how this illustrates the transmission of knowledge from the celestial intellects to the prophet's soul.
Just as one mirror reflects another's images, the soul reflects forms from higher intellects.
4
Consider the supporting evidence
The passage ends with an observation about variation in human souls. How does this support the possibility of an exceptionally strong prophetic soul?
Variation shows some souls can attain higher levels, up to holy souls that grasp many truths by intuition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقالوا : هو ( أي النبي ) من اجتمع فيه خواص ثلاث يمتاز بها عن غيره إحداها أن يكون له اطلاع على المغيبات الكائنة والماضية والآتية ولا يستنكر هذا الاطلاع لأن النفوس الإنسانية مجردة في ذاتها عن المادة غير حالة فيها بل هي لامكانية ولها نسبة في التجرد إلى المجردات العالية والنفوس السماوية المنتقشة بصور ما يحدث في هذا العالم العنصري الكائن الفاسد لكونها مبادئ له فقد تتصل النفس الناطقة بها أي بتلك المجردات اتصالاً معنوياً وتنجذب إليها بواسطة الجنسية وتشاهد ما فيها من صور الحوادث فتحكيها أي يرتسم فيها تلك من الصور ما لا تستعد هي لارتسامه فيها كمرآة يحاذي بها مرآة أخرى فيها نقوش فينعكس منها إلى الأولى ما يقابلها ويؤيده أي يدل على جواز ما قلنا من أن تكون للنبي نفس قوية بهذه المرتبة ما ترى النفوس أي رؤية النفوس البشرية وما عليها من التفاوت في إدراك المعاني العقلية في طرفي الزيادة والنقصان تفاوتاً متصاعداً إلى النفوس القدسية التي تدرك النظريات الكثيرة بالحدس في أقرب
As for the philosophers, they said: He (i.e., the prophet) is one in whom three special qualities are gathered, by which he distinguishes himself from others. One of them is that he has knowledge of the unseen, past, present, and future. And this knowledge is not to be denied, because human souls are immaterial in their essence, not subsisting in matter, but rather potential. And they have a relation in their immateriality to the higher intellects and celestial souls which are impressed with the images of what occurs in this elemental, generated, and corruptible world, because they are their principles. So the rational soul may connect to those intellects spiritually and be attracted to them through generic affinity, and observe what is in them of the forms of events, and then depict them – i.e., there are imprinted in it from those forms what it itself was not prepared to have imprinted. This is like a mirror that is placed opposite another mirror that has engravings, and from the second to the first is reflected what corresponds to it. And what confirms this – i.e., indicates the possibility of what we said that the prophet has a strong soul of this rank – is what is seen of souls, i.e., the sight of human souls and the variation among them in the perception of intellectual meanings at the extremes of increase and decrease, a variation rising all the way to the holy souls which perceive many theoretical matters by intuition in the shortest
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophical view of prophethood. The philosophers (falāsifa) hold that a prophet possesses three unique characteristics. The passage explains the first characteristic: knowledge of the unseen (al-mughayyabāt), including past, present, and future. The possibility of such knowledge is defended by an argument from the nature of the soul. Human souls are immaterial (mujarrada) and have a relation to higher intellects and celestial souls. These higher intellects contain the forms of all events in the sublunary world. The rational soul can connect to these intellects via a spiritual connection and 'see' these forms, which are then reflected in the soul as knowledge. The analogy of two mirrors is used: when one mirror faces another that has images, the images are reflected. The argument is further supported by the observed variation in human intellectual capacities; just as some people are naturally more perceptive, it is possible for a prophet to have a soul so strong that it can easily connect to the higher intellects. This leads to the concept of holy souls (al-nufūs al-qudsiyya) that grasp many theoretical truths by intuition in a very short time.

Key Terms
الفلاسفة
المغيبات
النفوس المجردة
المجردات
النفوس السماوية
الحدس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Knowledge of the unseen is only possible through direct divine revelation.
Correction: The philosophers argue that such knowledge can be acquired naturally through the soul's connection to higher intellects, without special revelation.

Checkpoint

A) Ability to perform miracles
B) Knowledge of the unseen past, present, and future
C) Perfect moral character
D) Physical strength

Key Takeaway
Philosophers argue that prophetic knowledge of the unseen results from the soul's natural ability to connect to higher intellects and receive forms of events.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Are you feeding it with material distractions or spiritual truths? Wake up to the state of your soul—what one change will you make today to strengthen its affinity with the divine?
?
The philosophers say that the soul's connection to the higher intellects occurs through 'generic affinity'—likeness. Look at your own soul: does it bear any likeness to the celestial? Or have you allowed it to become coarse and earthly? You know the answer—what will you do about it before you meet your Lord?
?
The passage speaks of holy souls that perceive theoretical matters by intuition. You have been given the same rational soul. Are you satisfied with second-hand faith, or will you strive to taste direct certainty? This week, commit to memorizing and reflecting on one verse about the unseen every morning until your heart begins to see.

The Subtlety of the Verse on Prophethood's Placement
Commentary showing the verse subtly indicates suitability for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse 'He knows where to place His message' subtly indicates that prophethood is granted based on spiritual suitability, as shown in its occasion of revelation.

Core Idea
God chooses prophets based on spiritual virtues, not lineage.

This lesson examines the subtlety in the verse 'He knows where to place His message' (Quran 6:124). While it appears to support the Ash'ari view that prophethood is purely by God's will, the commentary reveals that the verse also indicates a condition of preparation, as seen in its revelation regarding Abu Jahl's objection. We'll explore how this subtle point is handled by the commentator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the commentator's initial statement about subtlety.
2
Identify the subtlety
The commentator says the verse seems to support mere will but actually indicates preparation. Understand why this is 'subtle'.
The verse speaks of knowledge, not will directly.
3
Examine the occasion of revelation
Read the story of Abu Jahl. How does it show that prophethood is about spiritual virtues?
The verse refutes the idea that prophethood is by lineage or wealth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (نوع خفاء كما لا يخفى لأن المطلوب أن المخصص مجرد المشيئة والآية إنما تدل على أنه تعالى يعلم المكان الذي يجعل فيه رسالته وهذا أمر آخر بل دلالة الآية الكريمة على اشتراط الاستعداد أظهر لما روى أن أبا جهل قال : تزاحمنا بنو عبد مناف حتى إذا صرنا كفرسي رهان قالوا منا نبي يوحى إليه والله لا نرضى به إلا أن يأتينا وحي كما يأتيه فنزلت الآية للرد عليهم بأن النبوة ليست بالنسب والمال وإنما هي بفضائل نفسانية يخص بها من يشاء من عباده فيجتبي لرسالته من علم أنه يصلح لها. كذا في تفسير القاضي ثم هذا لا يستلزم الإيجاب الذي يقوله الفلاسفة لأنه تعالى إن شاء أعطى النبوة وإن شاء أمسكه وإن استعد المحل، وقد يقال : إذا شاورك أحد فقلت له : أنت تعلم ما تصنع استفيد منه بحسب العرف تفويض أمره إلى مشيئته وفيه ما فيه .
His statement: 'There is a kind of subtlety, as is not hidden, because what is required is that the specific factor is mere will, and the verse only indicates that He knows the place where He places His message, and this is another matter. Rather, the indication of the noble verse for the condition of preparation is more apparent because of what is narrated that Abu Jahl said: 'The Banu Abd Manaf competed with us until we became like two horses in a race; they said: from among us a prophet receives revelation. By God, we will not be satisfied unless revelation comes to us as it comes to him.' So the verse was revealed to refute them, that prophethood is not by lineage or wealth, but by spiritual virtues with which He singles out whom He wills from His servants, and He chooses for His message whom He knows is suitable for it.' Thus in the commentary of al-Qadi. Then, this does not entail the necessitation that the philosophers speak of, because He, if He wills, gives prophethood, and if He wills, withholds it, even if the place is prepared. And it may be said: if someone consults you and you say to him: 'You know what you are doing,' it is understood according to custom as entrusting the matter to his will, and there is something in it.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage comments on the subtlety of the verse 'He is most knowing where to place His message' (Quran 6:124). The commentator notes that while the verse might seem to support the Ash'ari position that prophethood is purely by God's will, its indication is subtle because the verse speaks about knowledge of placement, not about the will as exclusive cause. He then points out that the verse actually appears to indicate the condition of preparation, citing the occasion of revelation: Abu Jahl objected to prophethood being given to someone from Banu Hashim rather than him, and the verse refuted that by saying God chooses whom He knows is suitable. This seems to imply suitability as a condition. However, the commentator then clarifies that this does not entail the necessitation (ījāb) claimed by the philosophers. God may still withhold prophethood even if the person is prepared. He gives an analogy: if you say to someone 'you know what you are doing,' it implies entrusting to his will, not that he is forced. So the verse is consistent with Ash'ari occasionalism.

Key Terms
الاستعداد
تفسير القاضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the occasion of revelation, what did the verse refute about prophethood?
A) That prophethood can be achieved by self-training
B) That prophethood is based on lineage or wealth
C) That prophethood requires a miracle
D) That prophethood is for all times

Key Takeaway
The verse subtly indicates that prophethood is given to those with spiritual suitability, as shown in the story of Abu Jahl.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself relying on your family background, social status, or wealth to feel spiritually secure? How would your life change if you truly believed that God only looks at your spiritual virtues?
?

?
If God chooses based on what He knows is suitable, are you actively cultivating the spiritual virtues that make you suitable for His blessings? What one virtue will you work on today because of this lesson?

Refuting Necessitation: God's Free Will in Prophethood
Refutation of the philosophers' claim that preparation necessitates prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the verse does not entail the philosophers' belief that preparation necessitates prophethood, because God may withhold it even if the person is prepared, affirming His free will.

Core Idea
God's will is free; preparation does not force His hand.

After establishing that the verse implies suitability, the commentator is careful to distance this from the philosophers' claim of necessary preparation (ījāb). In this lesson, we see how the Ash'ari position is preserved: God's will is not constrained by any condition, and the analogy of 'you know what you are doing' illustrates that entrusting to God's will means acknowledging His freedom.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation starting from 'Then, this does not entail...'
2
Understand the refutation
The commentator explicitly denies that implied preparation leads to necessitation. God may give or withhold prophethood even if the person is prepared.
This directly counters the philosophers' view.
3
Analyze the analogy
Consider the example of saying 'you know what you are doing' to someone. How does it show entrusting to will, not necessitation?
The analogy shows that entrusting implies freedom, not compulsion.

Source Text

The Ash'arite Definition of Prophethood as a Divine Gift
Al-Jurjani defines prophethood as a pure divine gift and interprets Quran 2:105.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Ash'arite view that prophethood is a pure divine gift, not earned through spiritual exercises or natural disposition, and how they interpret the Quranic verse to support this.

Core Idea
Prophethood is not earned; it is a gift of God's will alone.

Al-Jurjani begins by defining prophethood according to Ahl al-Haqq (the Ash'arites), contrasting it with the philosophers' conditions. He argues that no acquired attributes or natural preparedness are required; rather, God selects whom He wills. The passage then deals with a subtlety in the Quranic proof and responds to potential objections from philosophers.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the full Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the definition of prophethood and the mention of philosophers' conditions.
The passage includes a marginal comment integrated into the text.
2
Identify the Ash'arite position
What conditions does the Ash'arite position reject? What is the sole basis for prophethood according to Ahl al-Haqq?
Contrast with the philosophers who require 'istidad' (preparedness).
3
Analyze the subtlety in the verse
The passage notes that the verse 'He knows where to place His message' has a subtle indication. Consider how it was revealed in response to Abu Jahl and what it implies about the basis of prophethood.
The verse is used to refute the idea that lineage or wealth qualifies one for prophethood.
4
Consider the philosophical objection
The passage addresses whether this view leads to necessity (as philosophers claim). How does Al-Jurjani respond to that?
He emphasizes God's freedom: He can give or withhold prophethood even if the recipient is prepared.

The first observation - first objective: On the meaning of 'prophet' like 'and to all people' (or 'deliver from me' and similar expressions) that convey this meaning, such as 'I sent you' and 'inform them'. No condition is stipulated in the sending, of acquired attributes and states through spiritual exercises and struggles in seclusions and retreats. (And preparedness) is the essential purity of substance and sharpness of innate disposition, as the philosophers claim. Rather, God, glorified and exalted, 'chooses for His mercy whom He wills from His servants.' So prophethood is mercy and a gift pertaining only to His will. 'And He is most knowing of where to place His message.' In the indication of this verse for the desired point there is a kind of subtlety, as is not hidden. And this is what the people of truth held, based on the doctrine of the powerful chooser who does what He wills and chooses what He desires. He said: (A kind of subtlety, as is not hidden, because the desired point is that the determiner is mere will, and the verse only indicates that He knows the place where He places His message, and this is another matter. Rather, the indication of the noble verse on the condition of preparedness is more apparent, because it is narrated that Abu Jahl said: 'The Banu Abd Manaf competed with us until we became like two racehorses; they said: Among us is a prophet to whom revelation comes. By God, we will not be satisfied with him unless revelation comes to us as it comes to him.' Then the verse was revealed to refute them, that prophethood is not by lineage or wealth, but rather it is by spiritual virtues with which He chooses whom He wills from His servants, and He selects for His message whom He knows is suitable for it. Thus in the commentary of al-Qadi. Then this does not necessitate the necessitarianism that the philosophers claim, because He, if He wills, gives prophethood, and if He wills, withholds it, even if the place is prepared. And it may be said: When someone consults you, you say to him: 'You know what you are doing.' From this, according to custom, the delegation of his matter to his will is understood, and there is something in it.)
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage is a definition of prophethood according to Ahl al-Haqq (Ash'arites), combined with a proof from the Quranic verse and a refutation of the philosophers' conditions. The author first states that no acquired conditions (such as spiritual exercises or natural disposition) are required for prophethood. Instead, God chooses whom He wills from His servants, as indicated by the verse 'He chooses for His mercy whom He wills' (2:105). The author then notes that the verse's apparent meaning may not directly prove that the determiner is mere will, but a deeper interpretation shows it refutes the philosophers' claim of necessary preparedness. The marginal comment explains the subtlety: the verse is revealed to refute Abu Jahl's objection, showing that prophethood is not by lineage but by divine choice. The author emphasizes that this does not entail necessity (as philosophers hold) because God can give or withhold prophethood freely. The passage ends with an analogy: when a trustee says 'you know what you are doing', it delegates the matter to the other's will.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That prophethood can be earned through spiritual exercises or depends on natural disposition.
Correction: According to Ahl al-Haqq, prophethood is a pure gift from God, determined solely by His will, with no prerequisites of human effort or innate qualities.

Checkpoint
According to Ahl al-Haqq, what is the basis for God's selection of a prophet?
A) The prophet's spiritual exercises and struggles.
B) The prophet's natural purity and intelligence.
C) Merely God's will and mercy.
D) The prophet's lineage and wealth.

Key Takeaway
Prophethood is a mercy and gift from God, determined solely by His will, not by human effort or disposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever secretly believed that your own piety or knowledge made you more deserving of God's favor? How does this lesson's teaching that prophethood is a pure gift, not earned, challenge that assumption? What specific attitude do you need to repent of today?
?
When you see someone blessed with spiritual gifts or leadership, do you ever feel envious or think they don't deserve it? How does knowing that God chooses whom He wills for His mercy reshape your heart toward that person? What specific action can you take to align your thoughts with this truth?
?
If prophethood—the highest honor—is entirely a gift, then what about every other blessing in your life? Name one blessing you typically attribute to your own effort. How will you now consciously attribute it to God's will alone, and what will change in your daily gratitude or reliance?

The Philosophers' Argument for Prophetic Knowledge of the Unseen
Al-Jurjani presents the philosophers' argument for prophetic knowledge of the unseen.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical basis for the claim that prophets possess knowledge of the unseen, including the immaterial soul and its connection to higher intellects.

Core Idea
The soul's immaterial nature allows it to connect to higher intellects and gain hidden knowledge.

The philosophers argue that a prophet's knowledge of the unseen is rooted in the immateriality of the human soul. They propose that the soul can connect to celestial intellects that contain the forms of all events, allowing the prophet to see the unseen. This lesson unpacks their argument and the evidence they present.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the passage once without analysis, then the translation. Note the philosophical terminology used.
Focus on understanding the core idea before dissecting.
2
Identify the key components of the argument
The passage has three main parts: (1) the claim that the soul is immaterial, (2) its relation to higher intellects and the process of connection, (3) supporting evidence from variation in souls and examples of non-prophets.
3
Analyze the analogy of mirrors
The philosophers use the analogy of two facing mirrors to explain how the soul receives forms from the higher intellects. Consider why this analogy is chosen and what it implies about the nature of knowledge.
This analogy is central to their epistemology.
4
Reflect on the examples of exercisers, the sick, and sleepers
The philosophers support their argument by pointing to people who gain glimpses of the unseen through spiritual exercise, illness, or dreams. Ask yourself: does this prove the prophet's knowledge is of the same kind?
This sets up the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الفلاسفة فقالوا : هو ( أي النبي ( من اجتمع فيه خواص (ثلاث يمتاز بها عن غيره (إحداها ) أي إحدى الأمور المختصة به ( أن يكون له اطلاع على المغيبات ( الكائنة والماضية والآتية ) ولا يستنكر) هذا الاطلاع ( لأن النفوس الإنسانية مجردة ) في ذاتها عن المادة غير حالة فيها بل هي لامكانية ( ولها نسبة ) في التجرد ( إلى المجردات ) العالية والنفوس السماوية ( المنتقشة بصور ما يحدث في هذا (العالم العنصري الكائن الفاسد لكونها مبادئ له فقد تتصل) النفس الناطقة بها ) أي بتلك المجردات اتصالاً معنوياً وتنجذب إليها بواسطة الجنسية ) وتشاهد ما فيها من صور الحوادث ( فتحكيها ) أي يرتسم فيها تلك من الصور ما لا تستعد هي لارتسامه فيها كمرآة يحاذي بها مرآة أخرى فيها نقوش فينعكس منها إلى الأولى ما يقابلها ( ويؤيده ) أي يدل على جواز ما قلنا من أن تكون للنبي نفس قوية بهذه المرتبة ( ما ترى النفوس) أي رؤية النفوس البشرية (وما عليها من التفاوت ) في إدراك المعاني العقلية ( في طرفي الزيادة والنقصان ) تفاوتاً متصاعداً إلى النفوس القدسية التي تدرك النظريات الكثيرة بالحدس في أقرب زمان غير أن يعرض لها غلط ) ومتنازلاً إلى البليد الذي لا يكاد يفقه قولاً وكيف) يستنكر ذلك الاطلاع في حق النبي ( وقد يوجد ) ذلك ( فيمن قلت شواغله لرياضة ) بأنواع المجاهدات ) أو (مرض) صارف للنفس عن الاشتغال بالبدن واستعمال الآلة ) أو نوم ينقطع به إحساساته الظاهرة فإن هؤلاء قد يطلعون على مغيبات ويخبرون عنها كما يشهد به التسامع والتجارب بحيث لا يبقى فيه شبهة للمنصفين. (قلنا :) ما ذكرتم ( مردود) بوجوه ( إذ الاطلاع على جميع المغيبات لا يجب للنبي اتفاقاً) منا ومنكم، ولهذا قال سيد الأنبياء ولو كنت أعلم الغيب لاستكثرت من الخير وما مسني السوء. ( والبعض ) أي الاطلاع على البعض ) لا يختص به ) أي بالنبي (كما أقررتم به ) حيث جوزتموه للمرتاضين والمرضى والنائمين فلا يتميز به النبي عن غيره ( ثم) نقول : ( إحالة ذلك ) أي الاطلاع المختص بالنبي ) على اختلاف النفوس) في صفاء جوهرها وكدره وشدة قوتها على قطع التعلق والتوجه إلى جناب القدس والملاء الأعلى ) وتجردها مع اتحادها بالنوع) كما هو مذهبهم (مشكل) لأن المساواة في الماهية توجب الاشتراك في الأحكام والصفات وإسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن مبني على القول بالموجب بالذات ( و) نقول أيضاً (باقي المقدمات من الاتصال بالمباديء العالية بعلة الجنسبة وانتقاشها بما فيها من صور الحوادث كما في المرايا المتقابلة ( خطابية ) لا تفيد إلا ظناً ضعيفاً . قوله : ( المنتقشة بصور ما يحدث إلخ ) قد سبق تفصيله في عاشر مقاصد القدرة . قوله : ( حيث جوزتموه للمرتاضين إلخ قد يجاب عنه بأن لهم أن يقولوا : كونه بلا مرض ونوم ورياضة تختص به على أنه يجوز أن يكون الخاصة المطلقة مجموع الثلاثة ويكون كل واحد منها خاصة إضافية. قوله مبني قوله : ( مع اتحادها بالنوع مشكل إلخ) اعترض عليه بجواز الاستناد إلى المشخصات ثم على القول بالموجب مدفوع بجواز إسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن بطريق جري العادة . نعم مذهبهم الإيجاب لكن الكلام في لزوم القول به على تقدير إسناد الاختلاف إليها كما يفهم من كلامه والجواب : أن تشخص النفس باعتبار البدن عند الفلاسفة فيكون في المآل إسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن ثم إن الاختلاف بطريق جري العادة ينافي الشرطية التي كلامنا فيها .
As for the philosophers, they said: He (i.e., the prophet) is one in whom three special characteristics are combined, by which he is distinguished from others. The first of them (i.e., one of the things specific to him) is that he has knowledge of the unseen (past, present, and future). And this knowledge is not denied because human souls are immaterial in their essence, not subsisting in matter, but rather they are incorporeal. And they have a relationship, in their immateriality, with the higher immaterial substances and the celestial souls which are engraved with the forms of what occurs in this elemental world of generation and corruption, because they are its principles. Thus, the rational soul may connect to them (i.e., those immaterial substances) in a meaningful connection and be drawn to them by means of homogeneity, and witness what is in them of the forms of events, and then depict them (i.e., those forms become imprinted in it that which it is not prepared to receive by itself), like a mirror facing another mirror in which there are engravings, so that from the latter reflections appear in the former. And it is supported (i.e., it indicates the permissibility of what we have said that the prophet has a strong soul of this rank) by what is seen of souls (i.e., the vision of human souls) and the variation among them in perceiving intellectual meanings (in terms of increase and decrease), ascendingly to the holy souls that perceive many theories by intuition in the shortest time, without error befalling them, and descendingly to the dullard who hardly understands a saying. So how can this knowledge be denied in the case of the prophet, when it is found in those whose distractions are few due to spiritual exercise (by various struggles) or due to illness that diverts the soul from occupation with the body and use of the instrument, or sleep by which his external sensations are cut off? For such people may become aware of the unseen and inform of it, as attested by widespread reports and experiences, to the point that no doubt remains for the fair-minded. We say: What you have mentioned is refutable in many ways: (i) Knowledge of all the unseen is not necessary for the prophet, by agreement between us and you; hence the master of prophets said, 'If I knew the unseen, I would have increased the good and no evil would have touched me.' (ii) Knowledge of some of the unseen is not specific to the prophet, as you yourselves have admitted, since you allowed it for spiritual exercisers, the sick, and sleepers; so the prophet is not distinguished by it from others. (iii) Then we say: Attributing this (i.e., the prophet's specific knowledge) to the difference of souls (in purity and impurity of their substance, and the strength of their ability to cut attachment and turn to the court of holiness and the highest heavens) and their immateriality while being united in species, as is their doctrine, is problematic, because equality in essence necessitates commonness in judgments and attributes; and ascribing the difference to bodily states is based on the doctrine of natural necessity. (iv) We also say: The remaining premises, such as connection to the higher principles via homogeneity and their being engraved with the forms of events like facing mirrors, are rhetorical, yielding only weak conjecture. His saying: (The engraved with the forms of what occurs etc.) has already been detailed in the tenth objective of Power. His saying: (Where you allowed it for exercisers etc.) may be answered by saying they can say that it being without illness, sleep, or exercise distinguishes the prophet, and it is possible that the absolute specific quality is the sum of the three, and each one is an additional specific quality. His saying: (With their unity in species problematic etc.) is objected to by the possibility of ascription to individuators; then, on the doctrine of necessity, it is refuted by the possibility of ascribing the difference to bodily states by way of habitual occurrence. Yes, their doctrine is necessity, but the discussion is about the necessity of holding that given ascribing the difference to them, as understood from his words. The answer is that the individuation of the soul is in consideration of the body according to the philosophers, so ultimately it is ascribing the difference to bodily states. Then, the difference by way of habit contradicts the conditionality under discussion.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the philosophers' claim that one of the three special characteristics of a prophet is knowledge of the unseen. The passage begins with the philosophers' argument: the human soul is immaterial and can connect to higher immaterial intellects that contain the forms of all events, allowing the prophet to see the unseen. They support this by noting variation in human minds (from intuition to dullness) and by examples of spiritual exercisers, the ill, and sleepers who glimpse the unseen. The response (قلنا) gives four refutations: (1) Prophets do not know all the unseen (citing the Quranic verse). (2) Partial knowledge is not exclusive to prophets. (3) Ascribing this ability to differences in souls while souls share the same essence is problematic. (4) The premises of their argument are merely rhetorical, producing only weak conjecture. The marginal notes add further discussion, including a possible answer from the philosophers and a criticism of their position on necessity.

Key Terms

نفوس مجردة
اتصال معنوي
رياضة
مرض
نوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The philosophers' argument is based on empirical observation of prophets.
Correction: The argument is primarily metaphysical: it infers from the nature of the soul and the principle of homogeneity, not from direct evidence of prophetic experiences.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers, what enables the rational soul to know the unseen?
A) Divine revelation directly from God
B) Connection to higher immaterial intellects via homogeneity
C) Intensive spiritual exercises alone
D) Physical purification of the body through illness

Key Takeaway
The philosophers argue that a prophet's knowledge of the unseen stems from the soul's immateriality and its ability to connect with higher intellects, supported by the spectrum of human cognition and non-prophetic glimpses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that your soul is capable of knowing more than what your senses perceive? What distractions in your life are blocking that connection, and what will you do today to reduce at least one of them?
?
The philosophers say that even in sleep or illness, people glimpse the unseen. When was the last time you paid attention to a dream or a moment of clarity? Are you dismissing a genuine spiritual insight as coincidence, or are you willing to take it seriously enough to change something in your life?
?
The variation in souls shows that some are nearly incapable of understanding. How do you treat those who are slow to grasp religious truths? Do you blame them, or do you recognize that their soul's capacity is limited, and that you too are limited in some areas? What would it look like to humbly acknowledge your own limitations and rely on God's guidance?

Refutation of the Philosophers' Argument
Al-Jurjani refutes the philosophers' argument with four objections and additional marginal discussion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jurjani's four refutations of the philosophers' claim about prophetic knowledge of the unseen, including his critique of their premises and the marginal discussions.

Core Idea
The philosophers' argument fails on logical, scriptural, and epistemological grounds.

Al-Jurjani responds to the philosophers with a series of incisive objections. He argues that the philosophers' premises are insufficient, that prophetic knowledge is not unique, and that their metaphysical assumptions are problematic. This lesson examines each refutation and the subsequent discussion in the marginal notes.

Guided Path
1
Read the refutation passage
Read the full Arabic and translation, noting the four refutation points introduced by 'قلنا'.
Pay attention to the structure: each point is distinct.
2
Examine the first refutation: knowledge of all unseen is not necessary
Al-Jurjani argues that prophets do not know all the unseen, citing the Quranic verse. Understand why this undermines the philosophers' claim.
This is a scriptural and logical point.
3
Examine the second refutation: partial knowledge is not exclusive
Even if prophets know some unseen, this is also found in non-prophets (as admitted by the philosophers). Thus it cannot be a distinguishing characteristic.
4
Examine the third and fourth refutations: problematic premises
The third refutation challenges the philosophers' explanation of differences among souls, and the fourth dismisses their remaining premises as merely rhetorical.
These are more technical philosophical objections.
5
Review the marginal notes
The marginal notes discuss potential responses from the philosophers and further clarifications. Consider how these strengthen or complete the refutation.
These notes are part of the scholarly tradition.

وأما الفلاسفة فقالوا : هو ( أي النبي ( من اجتمع فيه خواص (ثلاث يمتاز بها عن غيره (إحداها ) أي إحدى الأمور المختصة به ( أن يكون له اطلاع على المغيبات ( الكائنة والماضية والآتية ) ولا يستنكر) هذا الاطلاع ( لأن النفوس الإنسانية مجردة ) في ذاتها عن المادة غير حالة فيها بل هي لامكانية ( ولها نسبة ) في التجرد ( إلى المجردات ) العالية والنفوس السماوية ( المنتقشة بصور ما يحدث في هذا (العالم العنصري الكائن الفاسد لكونها مبادئ له فقد تتصل) النفس الناطقة بها ) أي بتلك المجردات اتصالاً معنوياً وتنجذب إليها بواسطة الجنسية ) وتشاهد ما فيها من صور الحوادث ( فتحكيها ) أي يرتسم فيها تلك من الصور ما لا تستعد هي لارتسامه فيها كمرآة يحاذي بها مرآة أخرى فيها نقوش فينعكس منها إلى الأولى ما يقابلها ( ويؤيده ) أي يدل على جواز ما قلنا من أن تكون للنبي نفس قوية بهذه المرتبة ( ما ترى النفوس) أي رؤية النفوس البشرية (وما عليها من التفاوت ) في إدراك المعاني العقلية ( في طرفي الزيادة والنقصان ) تفاوتاً متصاعداً إلى النفوس القدسية التي تدرك النظريات الكثيرة بالحدس في أقرب زمان غير أن يعرض لها غلط ) ومتنازلاً إلى البليد الذي لا يكاد يفقه قولاً وكيف) يستنكر ذلك الاطلاع في حق النبي ( وقد يوجد ) ذلك ( فيمن قلت شواغله لرياضة ) بأنواع المجاهدات ) أو (مرض) صارف للنفس عن الاشتغال بالبدن واستعمال الآلة ) أو نوم ينقطع به إحساساته الظاهرة فإن هؤلاء قد يطلعون على مغيبات ويخبرون عنها كما يشهد به التسامع والتجارب بحيث لا يبقى فيه شبهة للمنصفين. (قلنا :) ما ذكرتم ( مردود) بوجوه ( إذ الاطلاع على جميع المغيبات لا يجب للنبي اتفاقاً) منا ومنكم، ولهذا قال سيد الأنبياء ولو كنت أعلم الغيب لاستكثرت من الخير وما مسني السوء. ( والبعض ) أي الاطلاع على البعض ) لا يختص به ) أي بالنبي (كما أقررتم به ) حيث جوزتموه للمرتاضين والمرضى والنائمين فلا يتميز به النبي عن غيره ( ثم) نقول : ( إحالة ذلك ) أي الاطلاع المختص بالنبي ) على اختلاف النفوس) في صفاء جوهرها وكدره وشدة قوتها على قطع التعلق والتوجه إلى جناب القدس والملاء الأعلى ) وتجردها مع اتحادها بالنوع) كما هو مذهبهم (مشكل) لأن المساواة في الماهية توجب الاشتراك في الأحكام والصفات وإسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن مبني على القول بالموجب بالذات ( و) نقول أيضاً (باقي المقدمات من الاتصال بالمباديء العالية بعلة الجنسبة وانتقاشها بما فيها من صور الحوادث كما في المرايا المتقابلة ( خطابية ) لا تفيد إلا ظناً ضعيفاً . قوله : ( المنتقشة بصور ما يحدث إلخ ) قد سبق تفصيله في عاشر مقاصد القدرة . قوله : ( حيث جوزتموه للمرتاضين إلخ قد يجاب عنه بأن لهم أن يقولوا : كونه بلا مرض ونوم ورياضة تختص به على أنه يجوز أن يكون الخاصة المطلقة مجموع الثلاثة ويكون كل واحد منها خاصة إضافية. قوله مبني قوله : ( مع اتحادها بالنوع مشكل إلخ) اعترض عليه بجواز الاستناد إلى المشخصات ثم على القول بالموجب مدفوع بجواز إسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن بطريق جري العادة . نعم مذهبهم الإيجاب لكن الكلام في لزوم القول به على تقدير إسناد الاختلاف إليها كما يفهم من كلامه والجواب : أن تشخص النفس باعتبار البدن عند الفلاسفة فيكون في المآل إسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن ثم إن الاختلاف بطريق جري العادة ينافي الشرطية التي كلامنا فيها .
As for the philosophers, they said: He (i.e., the prophet) is one in whom three special characteristics are combined, by which he is distinguished from others. The first of them (i.e., one of the things specific to him) is that he has knowledge of the unseen (past, present, and future). And this knowledge is not denied because human souls are immaterial in their essence, not subsisting in matter, but rather they are incorporeal. And they have a relationship, in their immateriality, with the higher immaterial substances and the celestial souls which are engraved with the forms of what occurs in this elemental world of generation and corruption, because they are its principles. Thus, the rational soul may connect to them (i.e., those immaterial substances) in a meaningful connection and be drawn to them by means of homogeneity, and witness what is in them of the forms of events, and then depict them (i.e., those forms become imprinted in it that which it is not prepared to receive by itself), like a mirror facing another mirror in which there are engravings, so that from the latter reflections appear in the former. And it is supported (i.e., it indicates the permissibility of what we have said that the prophet has a strong soul of this rank) by what is seen of souls (i.e., the vision of human souls) and the variation among them in perceiving intellectual meanings (in terms of increase and decrease), ascendingly to the holy souls that perceive many theories by intuition in the shortest time, without error befalling them, and descendingly to the dullard who hardly understands a saying. So how can this knowledge be denied in the case of the prophet, when it is found in those whose distractions are few due to spiritual exercise (by various struggles) or due to illness that diverts the soul from occupation with the body and use of the instrument, or sleep by which his external sensations are cut off? For such people may become aware of the unseen and inform of it, as attested by widespread reports and experiences, to the point that no doubt remains for the fair-minded. We say: What you have mentioned is refutable in many ways: (i) Knowledge of all the unseen is not necessary for the prophet, by agreement between us and you; hence the master of prophets said, 'If I knew the unseen, I would have increased the good and no evil would have touched me.' (ii) Knowledge of some of the unseen is not specific to the prophet, as you yourselves have admitted, since you allowed it for spiritual exercisers, the sick, and sleepers; so the prophet is not distinguished by it from others. (iii) Then we say: Attributing this (i.e., the prophet's specific knowledge) to the difference of souls (in purity and impurity of their substance, and the strength of their ability to cut attachment and turn to the court of holiness and the highest heavens) and their immateriality while being united in species, as is their doctrine, is problematic, because equality in essence necessitates commonness in judgments and attributes; and ascribing the difference to bodily states is based on the doctrine of natural necessity. (iv) We also say: The remaining premises, such as connection to the higher principles via homogeneity and their being engraved with the forms of events like facing mirrors, are rhetorical, yielding only weak conjecture. His saying: (The engraved with the forms of what occurs etc.) has already been detailed in the tenth objective of Power. His saying: (Where you allowed it for exercisers etc.) may be answered by saying they can say that it being without illness, sleep, or exercise distinguishes the prophet, and it is possible that the absolute specific quality is the sum of the three, and each one is an additional specific quality. His saying: (With their unity in species problematic etc.) is objected to by the possibility of ascription to individuators; then, on the doctrine of necessity, it is refuted by the possibility of ascribing the difference to bodily states by way of habitual occurrence. Yes, their doctrine is necessity, but the discussion is about the necessity of holding that given ascribing the difference to them, as understood from his words. The answer is that the individuation of the soul is in consideration of the body according to the philosophers, so ultimately it is ascribing the difference to bodily states. Then, the difference by way of habit contradicts the conditionality under discussion.
Source pages p. 242

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the philosophers' claim that one of the three special characteristics of a prophet is knowledge of the unseen. The passage begins with the philosophers' argument: the human soul is immaterial and can connect to higher immaterial intellects that contain the forms of all events, allowing the prophet to see the unseen. They support this by noting variation in human minds (from intuition to dullness) and by examples of spiritual exercisers, the ill, and sleepers who glimpse the unseen. The response (قلنا) gives four refutations: (1) Prophets do not know all the unseen (citing the Quranic verse). (2) Partial knowledge is not exclusive to prophets. (3) Ascribing this ability to differences in souls while souls share the same essence is problematic. (4) The premises of their argument are merely rhetorical, producing only weak conjecture. The marginal notes add further discussion, including a possible answer from the philosophers and a criticism of their position on necessity.

الاستناد إلى المشخصات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The refutation is based solely on traditional authority (the Quranic verse) and ignores logic.
Correction: The refutation combines scriptural, logical, and dialectical arguments. The third and fourth objections are purely philosophical.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the third refutation?
A) The soul is not immaterial
B) Differences in knowledge cannot be due to essence if souls share the same species
C) The Quranic verse proves prophets do not know the unseen
D) The mirror analogy is logically valid

Key Takeaway
Al-Jurjani refutes the philosophers by showing that prophetic knowledge is not comprehensive, not unique, and based on problematic metaphysical premises.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the Prophet's own statement, 'If I knew the unseen, I would have increased the good and no evil would have touched me,' change the way you view his humanity and your reliance on him for guidance in matters beyond the revealed texts? What practical steps can you take to follow his example in admitting limited knowledge?
?
Al-Jurjani argues that partial knowledge of the unseen is also found in non-prophets, such as spiritual exercisers and sleepers. If prophetic knowledge is not unique in this aspect, what truly distinguishes a prophet in your life? How does that distinction demand a response from you beyond just seeking knowledge?
?
The philosophers' argument relies on the idea that human souls can connect to higher principles. Al-Jurjani calls this 'rhetorical, yielding only weak conjecture.' In your own faith, are there beliefs you hold that are based on strong certainty (yaqīn) versus weak conjecture? Which areas of your religious practice might you be treating as certain when they are only conjectural?

The Philosophers' View on Miracles
Al-Jurjani explains the philosophers' second characteristic that a prophet performs miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' explanation of miracles as natural effects of the soul's power over matter.

Core Idea
Miracles are natural — the soul's conceptions shape matter.

The philosophers propose a naturalistic view of miracles: they are not divine interventions but manifestations of the soul's power over the physical world. In this passage, al-Jurjani explains how the prophet's soul can command the elemental matter just as the soul commands its own body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing, then read the English translation. Note the key terms.
The passage is dense; focus on the central claim.
2
Identify the analogy
The passage compares the soul's control over the body to the prophet's soul controlling the material world. Identify this analogy and how it supports the claim.
3
Understand the rationale
The philosophers argue that souls are not imprinted in bodies, so they can affect external matter. Notice the examples of blushing and fever as evidence.
These examples show the soul's influence on one's own body, extended to the world.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this explanation differs from a theological view that miracles are direct divine acts. How does the philosophers' view naturalize miracles?

And this submission is not denied because human souls are not imprinted in bodies, and they, by their conceptions, are effective in bodily matters, as is seen in blushing, pallor, and fever during shyness, fear, and anger.

Commentary
This passage presents the second characteristic that the philosophers claim distinguishes a prophet: the performance of miraculous acts (خوارق العادات). The explanation is that the physical material of the elemental world is obedient to the prophet's soul, just as his body obeys his soul in voluntary movements. The philosophers argue this is plausible because human souls are not imprinted in bodies and can influence physical matter through their conceptions (تصورات), as seen in emotional reactions like blushing or paleness. This is a naturalistic explanation of miracles, attributing them to the soul's power over matter.
