D) Because angels assist him.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain miracles as natural acts resulting from the soul's ability to influence matter, using the body-soul relationship as an analogy.

Are you comfortable with the idea that Allah's creation operates through causes that include the human soul? How does this affect your reliance on Allah versus reliance on your own soul's power?

Refuting Objections to Prophetic Knowledge of the Unseen
Refuting the claim that prophetic knowledge of the unseen is not unique.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational arguments used by theologians to defend the uniqueness of prophetic knowledge of the unseen against philosophical objections.

Core Idea
Prophetic knowledge of the unseen is unique, but not total—and rational arguments clear the confusion.

This passage addresses a key objection: if non-prophets can also gain knowledge of hidden things through spiritual exercises or sleep, then prophetic knowledge is not unique. The author, likely al-Jurjani, offers a multi-pronged rebuttal. You will see how theologians safeguard the prophetic miracle while acknowledging that partial knowledge can occur outside prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening lines of the passage carefully. They present the objector's claim: that ascetics, the sick, and dreamers can know hidden things, so prophetic knowledge is not exclusive.
The objection is from philosophers who deny the unique nature of prophethood.
2
Analyze the first two refutations
The author gives two quick responses: (1) prophets do not know all hidden things (agreed by all), so the objection is moot; (2) partial knowledge is not exclusive to prophets, as the objector himself admits.
These are based on common agreement and the objector's own premises.
3
Examine the third refutation
The author critiques the philosophical explanation that differences in soul purity cause special knowledge. He argues that souls of the same species should share properties; attributing differences to the body relies on a problematic notion of 'necessitation by essence'.
This targets the heart of the philosophical theory.
4
Consider the fourth refutation
The remaining premises about connection to higher principles and imprinting of forms are dismissed as merely rhetorical (khatabiyya), yielding only weak conjecture.
This shows the author's confidence that the philosophical model is not demonstrative.

Commentary
This passage is an objection and response concerning the first characteristic of prophets: knowledge of the unseen (al-ghayb). The objector, likely a philosopher, argues that non-prophets (ascetics, the sick, dreamers) also gain knowledge of hidden things, so it is not unique to prophets. The author responds with four points. First, prophetic knowledge is not necessarily comprehensive; even the Prophet Muhammad said he did not know the unseen. Second, partial knowledge is not exclusive to prophets, as the objector admits. Third, attributing prophetic knowledge to differences in souls is problematic because if souls are the same in essence, they should share the same properties; attributing differences to the body is based on the false doctrine of essential necessitation. Fourth, the remaining premises about connection to higher principles are merely rhetorical and yield only weak conjecture.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets have complete and exclusive knowledge of all unseen things.
Correction: Prophetic knowledge of the unseen is not necessarily total; even the Prophet said he did not know the unseen. Partial knowledge is not unique to prophets.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is it problematic to attribute prophetic knowledge of the unseen to differences in souls?

B) Because the soul is material and cannot differ.
C) Because prophets are physically superior.
D) Because such attribution is based on a hadith.

Key Takeaway
Prophetic knowledge of the unseen is unique in its divine origin, but not comprehensive in content; rational arguments show why philosophical objections fail.

Refuting Miracles as Proof of Prophethood
Refutation of the second prophetic characteristic: extraordinary acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the performance of extraordinary acts is not a valid distinctive characteristic of prophets according to Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
Extraordinary acts do not prove prophethood; correlation does not imply causation and such acts are not exclusive.

The second proposed characteristic of prophets is that they perform extraordinary acts (kharq al-ʿāda) because elemental matter is obedient to them. Proponents argue from everyday examples and the experiences of ascetics. In this passage, the author systematically refutes this claim, first by rejecting the underlying causal principle, then by questioning the inference from correlation, and finally by noting the lack of exclusivity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's argument unfold naturally.
The passage includes a marginal note; treat it as part of the discussion.
2
Identify the proponents' claim
Notice that the proponents base their argument on the soul's influence over the body, extending it to elemental matter. Find the examples they give.
They cite shame, fear, and anger as evidence of the soul's effect.
3
Trace the refutation points
The author gives three main refutations: 1) the entire doctrine of soul's influence is false, 2) correlation does not prove causation, it may be habit, 3) extraordinary acts are not exclusive to prophets.
Also note the marginal discussion about individuation and habit.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that miracles are not a distinguishing mark of prophethood because they are not unique and their cause is divine, not human.
This aligns with Ash'ari occasionalism.

على ترتيب من قوله : ( حيث جوزتموه للمرتاضين إلخ قد يجاب عنه بأن لهم أن يقولوا : كونه بلا مرض ونوم ورياضة تختص به على أنه يجوز أن يكون الخاصة المطلقة مجموع الثلاثة ويكون كل واحد منها خاصة إضافية. قوله مبني قوله : ( مع اتحادها بالنوع مشكل إلخ) اعترض عليه بجواز الاستناد إلى المشخصات ثم على القول بالموجب مدفوع بجواز إسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن بطريق جري العادة . نعم مذهبهم الإيجاب لكن الكلام في لزوم القول به على تقدير إسناد الاختلاف إليها كما يفهم من كلامه والجواب : أن تشخص النفس باعتبار البدن عند الفلاسفة فيكون في المآل إسناد الاختلاف إلى أحوال البدن ثم إن الاختلاف بطريق جري العادة ينافي الشرطية التي كلامنا فيها . اللف (و) كما نشاهد من السقوط من المواضع العالية القليلة العرض يتصور السقوط وإن كان ممشاه في غيرها ) أي في المواضع السالفة ( أقل عرضاً ) وإذا كانت إرادت النفس وتصوراتها مؤثرة في البدن مع عدم الانطباع فيه ( فلا يبعد أن تقوى نفس النبي ) بحيث تنقاد له الهيولى العنصرية فتؤثر فيها إرادته وتصوراته (حتى تحدث إرادته في الأرض رياحاً وزلازلاً وحرقاً وغرقاً وهلاك أشخاص ظالمة وخراب مدن فاسدة وبالجملة تتصرف نفسه في العنصريات خصوصاً في العنصر الذي تكون مناسبته لمزاجه أشد وأقوى بمجرد الإرادة والتصور من غير أن يستعمل آلة ( وكيف ) يستنكر حدوث هذه الأمور الخارقة العجيبة من النبي ونشاهد مثلها من أهل الرياضة والإخلاص ) على ما هو مشهور في كل عصر من الصلحاء . قلنا : هذا الذي ذكرتم من كون تصورات النفس وإرادتها مؤثرة في الأبدان (بناء على تأثير النفوس في الأجسام) وأحوالها وقد بينا بطلانه بما سلف من أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى ( والمقارنة ) بين التغيرات البدنية وبين التصورات والإرادات النفسية ( لا تعطيه ) أي لا تدل على كونها مؤثرة فيه لجواز أن يكون الدوران بطريق العادة ( مع أنه ( أي ظهور الأمور العجيبة الخارقة للعادة ) لا يختص بالنبي ) كما اعترفتم به فكيف تميزه عن غيره
(And the second of them) i.e., the second of those matters specific to the prophet (is that extraordinary acts appear from him due to the hylic matter of the elemental world being obedient to him, submissive to his dispositions, just as his body is submissive to his soul) in his movements and stillnesses in various ways and different modes according to his will (and that submission is not to be denied) because human souls are not impressed in bodies (and they, by their conceptions, are effective in bodily matters (as is observed: redness, paleness, and heating upon shame, fear, and anger. (This is a distribution in order from his saying: (Where you permitted it for ascetics etc., it may be answered that they can say: its being without sickness, sleep, and exercise is specific to him, and it is possible that the absolute specificity is the sum of the three, and each one is an additional specificity. His saying 'based on' his saying: ('while they are one in species is problematic etc.') It was objected to it by the permissibility of recourse to individuators; then the assertion of necessitation is repelled by the permissibility of attributing difference to states of the body by way of habitual occurrence. Yes, their doctrine is necessitation, but the discussion is about the necessity of holding it on the assumption of attributing difference to them, as understood from his words. And the answer: that the individuation of the soul by virtue of the body according to the philosophers, so in the final analysis it is attributing difference to states of the body. Then the difference by way of habitual occurrence contradicts the conditionality we are discussing. Alif (And) as we observe from falling from high places of narrow width, falling is imagined even if his walking in other places (i.e., in the aforementioned places) is narrower. And if the will of the soul and its conceptions are effective in the body without being impressed in it, (then it is not far that the prophet's soul becomes strong) so that the elemental hylic matter submits to him, so his will and conceptions affect it (so that his will produces in the earth winds, earthquakes, burning, drowning, destruction of unjust persons, and ruin of corrupt cities; in short, his soul disposes in the elementals, especially in the element whose suitability to his temperament is most intense and strong, by mere will and conception without using an instrument. (And how) is the occurrence of these wonderful extraordinary things from the prophet denied, while we observe the like from people of spiritual exercise and sincerity, as is famous in every age of the righteous. (We said:) This that you mentioned about the conceptions of the soul and its will being effective in bodies (is based on the influence of souls in bodies) and their states, and we have already shown its falsity by what has preceded: that there is no effecter in existence except Allah, Glorified and Exalted. (And the correlation) between bodily changes and psychological conceptions and wills (does not prove it) i.e., does not indicate that they are effective in it, because it is possible that the concomitance is by way of habit. (Moreover,) the appearance of wonderful extraordinary things (is not specific to the prophet) as you admitted, so how does it distinguish him from others?
Source pages p. 243

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second proposed characteristic of prophets: the performance of extraordinary acts (kharq al-ʿāda). The proponents argue that elemental matter is obedient to the prophet, just as the soul controls the body. They cite examples from everyday life and the experiences of ascetics. The author responds with several points. First, the entire doctrine of the soul's influence on bodies has already been refuted; only God is the true agent. Second, correlation between psychological states and bodily changes does not prove causation—it may be mere habit. Third, even if such extraordinary acts occur, they are not exclusive to prophets, as the opponents themselves admit, so they do not distinguish prophethood. The marginal note discusses a potential objection about individuation and habit, concluding that the philosophers' own principles lead to attributing difference to the body and that habitual occurrence contradicts the required conditionality.

Key Terms
أفعال خارقة للعادة
هيولى عنصرية
تأثير النفوس في الأجسام
دوران بطريق العادة
مقارنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are proofs of prophethood because they show the prophet's own power.
Correction: Miracles are created by Allah, not by the prophet's power. They do not prove prophethood because they are not exclusive and correlation does not imply divine favor.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the claim that the soul's conceptions cause bodily changes?
A) By denying that the soul exists.
B) By stating that correlation does not imply causation; it may be merely habitual.
C) By arguing that the body is inanimate.
D) By citing a hadith.

Key Takeaway
Extraordinary acts cannot be a distinguishing mark of prophethood because they are not exclusive to prophets and do not demonstrate human causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a time you assumed that because something happened after a person's action, that person caused it. How does the lesson's concept of 'habitual concomitance' challenge your default assumption? Will you now pause and attribute all true causation to Allah?
?
When you see an extraordinary event attributed to a pious person, does your heart incline to believe that person is special, or do you immediately remember that only Allah is the true agent? Where do you see this tendency in your own life?
?
If miracles are not exclusive to prophets, what does that mean for your reliance on external wonders versus internal conviction? How can you train yourself to look for the real distinguishing mark of truth?

Seeing Angels: The Philosophical Account Refuted
Refuting the philosophical explanation of prophetic angelic vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the philosophers' account of prophets seeing angels is inconsistent with their own metaphysical principles, and how the author exposes it as a deception.

Core Idea
The philosophers' 'angelic vision' is an imaginative projection, not a real encounter.

This passage tackles the third proposed characteristic of prophets: seeing angels and hearing their speech as revelation. The philosophers describe it as a quasi-dream state in wakefulness, but the author argues that this account is a deception because philosophers do not actually believe in sensible angels or real speech for incorporeal beings. Let's see how the argument unfolds.

Guided Path
1
Read the exposition of the third characteristic
Read the first part of the passage (up to 'بأدنى توجه') to understand how the philosophers explain the prophetic experience. Note the role of abstraction and the imaginative power.
The proponents compare it to dreaming, but in wakefulness.
2
Identify key elements of the philosophical account
Look for the soul's abstraction, connection to the world of holiness, and the function of the imaginative faculty in clothing intelligibles in sensible forms.
3
Read the author's refutation
From 'قلنا هذا الذي ذكروه' to the end, notice the accusation of deception (talbīs) and the reasons given: philosophers do not affirm sensible angels or real speech for incorporeals.
The author shows that the account contradicts the philosophers' own doctrines.
4
Understand the implications
Reflect on why the third characteristic is reduced to imagining something non-existent. Angels for philosophers are abstract souls or intellects, which have no form or speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وثالثها أن يرى الملائكة مصورة) بصورة محسوسة ( ويسمع كلامهم وحياً ) من الله إليه ( ولا يستنكر أن يحصل له في يقظته مثل ما يحصل للنائم في نومه من مشاهدة أشخاص يكلمونه بكلام منظوم دال على معان مطابقة للواقع وذلك لتجرد نفسه عن الشواغل البدنية وسهولة انجذابه إلى عالم القدس) فإِذا انجذبت إليه واتصلت به في يقظته شاهد المعقولات كمشاهدة المحسوسات فإن القوة المتخيلة تكسو المعقول المرتسم في النفس لباس المحسوس وتنقشه في الحس المشترك على نحو انتقاش المحسوسات فيه من خارج ( وربما صار ( الانجذاب والاتصال بعالم القدس ( ملكة ) أي صفة راسخة للنبي (و) حينئذ ( يحصل) له ذلك الانجذاب وما يترتب عليه من المشاهدة (بأدنى توجه) منه قلنا هذا الذي ذكروه لا يوافق مذهبهم واعتقادهم بل هو (تلبيس) على الناس في معتقدهم (وتستر) عن شناعته بعبارة لا يقولون بمعناها ) وذلك لأنهم لا يقولون بملائكة يرون بل الملائكة عندهم ) إما ( نفوس مجردة ) في ذواتها متعلقة بأجرام الأفلاك وتسمى ملائكة سماوية أو عقول مجردة ذاتاً وفعلاً وتسمى بالملا الأعلى ( ولا كلام لهم يسمع لأنه من خواص (الأجسام إذ الحرف والصوت عندهم من الأمور العارضة للهواء المتموج كما سلف فلا يتصور كلام حقيقي للمجرات ( ومآله ) أي مآل ما ذكروه في الخاصة الثالثة ) إلى تخيل ما لا وجود له في الحقيقة
(And the third of them is that he sees the angels in a form) in a sensible form (and hears their speech as revelation) from Allah to him (and it should not be denied that there occurs to him in his wakefulness the like of what occurs to the sleeper in his sleep: seeing persons who speak to him with composed speech indicating meanings corresponding to reality, and that is due to the abstraction of his soul from bodily preoccupations and the ease of its attraction to the world of holiness). For when it is attracted to it and connected to it in wakefulness, he beholds the intelligibles as he beholds sensibles, for the imaginative power clothes the intelligible impressed in the soul in the garment of the sensible and engraves it in the common sense in the manner of the engraving of sensibles in it from outside (and perhaps the attraction and connection to the world of holiness becomes a malaka) i.e., a firmly rooted attribute for the prophet (and) then (there occurs) to him that attraction and what follows from it of beholding (with minimal attention). (We said:) This that they mentioned does not agree with their doctrine and belief; rather, it is (a deception) upon people regarding their belief (and a concealment) of its ugliness by an expression whose meaning they do not affirm. That is because they do not affirm angels that are seen; rather, angels according to them are either (abstract souls) in their essences, attached to the celestial bodies, called celestial angels, or abstract intellects in essence and act, called the highest assembly (and they have no speech to be heard, because it is a property of bodies, since letters and sound according to them are among the accidents of undulating air, as has preceded, so no real speech can be conceived for the incorporeals). (And its outcome) i.e., the outcome of what they mentioned in the third characteristic (is to imagine something that has no existence in reality).
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the third proposed characteristic of prophets: seeing angels in sensible form and hearing their speech as revelation. The proponents explain this as an imaginative experience similar to dreams, resulting from the soul's abstraction and connection to the world of holiness. The author responds by calling this a deception (talbīs), because philosophers do not actually believe in angels that are seen or heard. Their angels are either abstract souls (attached to celestial bodies) or abstract intellects—both incorporeal. Speech, being composed of letters and sound, is a property of bodies, so incorporeal beings cannot have real speech. Thus, the third characteristic reduces to imagining something non-existent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the philosophical account of angelic communication is compatible with Islamic belief.
Correction: The author shows it contradicts their own metaphysics, as they do not affirm sensible angels or real speech for incorporeals.

Checkpoint
Why does the author call the third characteristic a deception (talbīs)?
A) Because prophets do not actually see angels.
B) Because philosophers do not believe in sensible angels or real speech for them.
C) Because the angel Jibril is not mentioned.
D) Because the prophets were mistaken.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers' account of prophets seeing angels is based on imagination and contradicts their own denial of sensible angels and speech for incorporeals.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The philosophers reduced prophetic visions to mere imagination, yet you claim to believe in the unseen reality of angels. When you recite the Qur'an or hear about Jibril (as), do you truly feel the weight of their reality, or do you subconsciously treat them as abstract symbols? What would change in your worship today if you lived with the certainty that angels are real beings who witness your actions? Ask Allah to grant you experiential faith, not mere intellectual assent.
?
The author calls the philosophers' account a 'deception'—a concealment of their true belief behind fine-sounding words. Are there areas in your own life where you have used religious language to mask doubt, compromise, or disobedience? For example, have you ever excused a sin by saying 'Allah is Most Merciful' without true repentance? Today, identify one such area and commit to honesty before Allah, even if it is painful.
?
If the prophetic experience were merely a product of imagination, then divine revelation would be reduced to human creativity. This lesson underscores that the Qur'an is not from the prophet's mind but from Allah. How does this truth demand that you approach the Qur'an with greater reverence and submission? Pledge to read a portion today as if you are hearing the direct speech of your Lord, and let it transform your actions.

First Property of a Prophet: Soul's Influence Refuted
Al-Iji states the first property and gives the Ash'ari response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the claimed first property of prophets—the soul's ability to affect elemental matter—and the Ash'ari refutation that only Allah is the true agent and that observed concomitance may be habit.

Core Idea
Correlation does not prove causation; only Allah creates effects.

In this passage, al-Iji presents the first of several properties that some philosophers claim distinguish a prophet: the ability to affect elemental matter through sheer will. The commentator then provides the Ash'ari response, which denies that any created being can be a true cause. Understanding this dialectic is crucial for grasping the Islamic theological stance on causality and prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the parentheses marking the matn-sharh distinction.
2
Identify the three parts of the argument
The passage has a clear dialectical structure: a claim, an objection, and an Ash'ari response. Find each part and note how they relate.
The claim begins with 'اللف (و) كما نشاهد...' and ends with 'بمجرد الإرادة والتصور من غير أن يستعمل آلة'. The objection is 'وكيف يستنكر...' and the response begins with 'قلنا: هذا الذي ذكرتم...'.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari response
The response first denies the premise that souls influence bodies, then explains that correlation does not imply causation—it could be habitual conjunction. Finally, it notes that extraordinary events are not unique to prophets.
The key phrases are 'لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله' and 'الدوران بطريق العادة'.
4
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this refutation is important for distinguishing a true prophet from a mere ascetic or magician. The Ash'ari position preserves divine uniqueness as the only true agent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اللف (و) كما نشاهد من السقوط من المواضع العالية القليلة العرض يتصور السقوط وإن كان ممشاه في غيرها ) أي في المواضع السالفة ( أقل عرضاً ) وإذا كانت إرادت النفس وتصوراتها مؤثرة في البدن مع عدم الانطباع فيه ( فلا يبعد أن تقوى نفس النبي ) بحيث تنقاد له الهيولى العنصرية فتؤثر فيها إرادته وتصوراته (حتى تحدث إرادته في الأرض رياحاً وزلازلاً وحرقاً وغرقاً وهلاك أشخاص ظالمة وخراب مدن فاسدة وبالجملة تتصرف نفسه في العنصريات خصوصاً في العنصر الذي تكون مناسبته لمزاجه أشد وأقوى بمجرد الإرادة والتصور من غير أن يستعمل آلة ( وكيف ) يستنكر حدوث هذه الأمور الخارقة العجيبة من النبي ونشاهد مثلها من أهل الرياضة والإخلاص ) على ما هو مشهور في كل عصر من الصلحاء . قلنا : هذا الذي ذكرتم من كون تصورات النفس وإرادتها مؤثرة في الأبدان (بناء على تأثير النفوس في الأجسام) وأحوالها وقد بينا بطلانه بما سلف من أن لا مؤثر في الوجود إلا الله سبحانه وتعالى ( والمقارنة ) بين التغيرات البدنية وبين التصورات والإرادات النفسية ( لا تعطيه ) أي لا تدل على كونها مؤثرة فيه لجواز أن يكون الدوران بطريق العادة ( مع أنه ( أي ظهور الأمور العجيبة الخارقة للعادة ) لا يختص بالنبي ) كما اعترفتم به فكيف تميزه عن غيره
The first (property): Just as we observe falling from high, narrow places, one can imagine falling even if one's walking path in other (places) is of [؟] less width, and since the soul's will and representations influence the body without being imprinted in it, it is not unlikely that the soul of the prophet becomes strong such that the elemental matter (hayula unsuriyya) becomes submissive to him, so his will and representations affect it, so that his will produces in the earth winds, earthquakes, burning, drowning, destruction of oppressive persons, ruin of corrupt cities, and in short, his soul acts upon elemental things, especially upon the element whose suitability to his temperament is strongest, solely by will and representation without using any instrument. And how can one deny the occurrence of these wonderful extraordinary things from the prophet, when we observe similar things from practitioners of spiritual discipline (riyada) and sincerity among the righteous (sulaha) in every age? We say: This which you have mentioned, that the soul's representations and will are effective in bodies, is based on the influence of souls upon bodies and their states, and we have already proven its falsehood with what has preceded, that there is no effect in existence except Allah, the Exalted. And the correlation between bodily changes and psychological representations and wills does not give (that they are causes), i.e., does not indicate their effectiveness therein, because it is possible that the concomitance is a matter of habit. Moreover, (the appearance of wonderful extraordinary things) is not specific to the prophet, as you yourselves have admitted, so how can you distinguish him from others?
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical argument. First, the philosopher (or disputant) describes the first property: since the soul already influences the body, it is plausible that a prophet's soul is so powerful that it can directly affect elemental matter, producing extraordinary events like earthquakes and destruction simply by willing them. Then an objection is raised: but we see similar wonders from ascetics and saints, so this is not unique to prophets. The commentator (al-Jurjani or the author of the Sharh) responds from the Ash'ari standpoint. He rejects the premise that souls influence bodies at all; only Allah is the true agent. The observed correlation between mental acts and bodily changes does not prove causation—it could be a habitual conjunction (ʿāda). Moreover, such extraordinary phenomena are not restricted to prophets, as the objector himself admitted. Thus the claim that this property distinguishes a prophet is unfounded.

Key Terms
نفس
هيولى عنصرية
إرادة
تأثير
عادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that correlation proves causation.
Correction: The Ash'ari response clarifies that habit (ʿāda) can explain the observed concomitance, so it does not entail causal influence.

Checkpoint
What is the main Ash'ari response to the argument that a prophet's soul influences elemental matter?
A) They agree it is possible but limit it to prophets.
B) They deny that souls influence bodies; only Allah is the true agent.
C) They accept the argument but say it requires additional conditions.
D) They argue that such events are impossible.

Key Takeaway
The claim that a prophet's soul directly influences elemental matter is refuted because only Allah is the true cause, and correlation may be due to habit, not causation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you see something good happen through your efforts, do you credit yourself or remember that Allah alone is the true cause? How can you make the shahada a reality in every moment of achievement?
?
The passage warns against mistaking correlation for causation. In your daily life, what patterns do you assume are caused by your own actions or others' without acknowledging that Allah creates them? What practical step can you take this week to redirect your attribution?
?
If you truly believed that no effect exists except from Allah, how would your anxiety about outcomes change? What would it look like to rely on Allah rather than the 'habits' you see in creation?

The Philosopher's Third Property: Seeing Angels and Hearing Speech
Al-Iji presents the philosophers' account of the third property of a prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosopher's explanation of how a prophet sees angels and hears revelation in waking life, by analogy with dreaming and through the mechanism of the imaginative faculty.

Core Idea
Prophetic vision is like dreaming but in waking: the soul disengages and the imaginative faculty clothes intelligibles in sensible forms.

The philosophers propose that prophets can see angels and hear their speech through a natural psychological process. This lesson presents their detailed account, which likens prophetic experience to a heightened form of dreaming where the soul connects with the holy realm and the imaginative faculty creates sensible representations of intelligible realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source and its translation. Notice how the philosopher describes the mechanism step by step.
2
Identify the key faculties
The passage mentions the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila) and common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak). Understand their roles.
The imaginative faculty clothes the intelligible in sensible garments.
3
Understand the analogy with dreaming
The philosopher uses the example of a sleeper seeing persons and hearing coherent speech. How does this relate to the prophet's waking experience?
4
Note the condition of becoming a fixed disposition
When the connection becomes a malaka, the prophet can witness at will with minimal effort.
This explains the ease of prophetic reception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثالثها أن يرى الملائكة مصورة بصورة محسوسة ويسمع كلامهم وحياً من الله إليه ولا يستنكر أن يحصل له في يقظته مثل ما يحصل للنائم في نومه من مشاهدة أشخاص يكلمونه بكلام منظوم دال على معان مطابقة للواقع وذلك لتجرد نفسه عن الشواغل البدنية وسهولة انجذابه إلى عالم القدس فإذا انجذبت إليه واتصلت به في يقظته شاهد المعقولات كمشاهدة المحسوسات فإن القوة المتخيلة تكسو المعقول المرتسم في النفس لباس المحسوس وتنقشه في الحس المشترك على نحو انتقاش المحسوسات فيه من خارج وربما صار الانجذاب والاتصال بعالم القدس ملكة أي صفة راسخة للنبي وحينئذ يحصل له ذلك الانجذاب وما يترتب عليه من المشاهدة بأدنى توجه منه قلنا هذا الذي ذكروه لا يوافق مذهبهم واعتقادهم بل هو تلبيس على الناس في معتقدهم وتستر عن شناعته بعبارة لا يقولون بمعناها وذلك لأنهم لا يقولون بملائكة يرون بل الملائكة عندهم إما نفوس مجردة في ذواتها متعلقة بأجرام الأفلاك وتسمى ملائكة سماوية أو عقول مجردة ذاتاً وفعلاً وتسمى بالملا الأعلى ولا كلام لهم يسمع لأنه من خواص الأجسام إذ الحرف والصوت عندهم من الأمور العارضة للهواء المتموج كما سلف فلا يتصور كلام حقيقي للمجرات ومآله أي مآل ما ذكروه في الخاصة الثالثة إلى تخيل ما لا وجود له في الحقيقة كما للمرضى والمجانين فإنهم يشاهدون ما لا وجود له في الخارج على ما صرحوا به وقرروا ما هو السبب فيه ولا شك أن ذلك إنما يكون على سبيل التخيل دون المشاهدة الحقيقة ولو كان أحدنا آمراً وناهياً من قبل نفسه بما يوافق المصلحة ويلائم العقل لم يكن نبياً باتفاق من العقلاء فكيف يكون نبياً من كان أمره ونهيه من قبيل ما يرجع إلى تخيلات لا أصل لها قطعاً أو ربما خالف ما دعا إليه المعقول أيضاً هذا كما مضى قوله : ( كما للمرضى والمجانين قد يجاب عنه بأن خلاصة كلامهم أن النفس الإنسانية إذا اتصلت بالمبادئ العالية يتمثل بقوته المتخيلة العقول المجردة والنفوس السماوية أشباحاً مصورة سيما العقل الفعال الذي له زيادة اختصاص بعالم العناصر فيخاطبه بكلام مسموع ويكون قبل الله تعالى وملائكته وفيه تخيل صورة لموجود وكم بينه وبين تخيل ما لا وجود له أصلاً كما للمرضى والمجانين
And third: That he sees the angels in a sensible form and hears their speech as revelation from Allah, and it is not objectionable that there occurs to him in his waking state something like what occurs to the sleeper in his sleep: seeing persons speaking to him with coherent speech indicating meanings that correspond to reality, and that is due to the disengagement of his soul from bodily distractions and the ease of its attraction to the holy realm (al-quds). So when it is attracted to it and connects with it in his waking state, he witnesses the intelligibles as one witnesses the sensibles; for the imaginative faculty (al-quwwa al-mutakhayyila) clothes the intelligible imprinted in the soul with the garment of the sensible and inscribes it in the common sense (al-hiss al-mushtarak) in the manner of the inscription of sensibles therein from outside. And perhaps this attraction and connection with the holy realm becomes a malaka (a fixed disposition) of the prophet, and then that attraction and the witnessing consequent upon it occurs to him with the least attention. We say: This which they have mentioned does not agree with their own school and belief; rather, it is a deception (talbis) of the people regarding their belief and a cover (tasattur) for its ugliness by an expression that does not convey their (real) meaning. That is because they do not affirm angels that can be seen; rather, angels in their view are either disembodied souls (nufus mujarrada) in themselves attached to the celestial spheres (called celestial angels) or intellects (ʿuqūl) disembodied in essence and act (called the highest council). And they have no speech that can be heard, because speech is a property of bodies, since letters and sound in their view are accidents of undulating air, as has preceded. So no real speech is conceivable for the separate entities. And the outcome of what they mentioned concerning the third property is a representation (takhyīl) of something that has no existence in reality, like the sick and the insane, for they witness what has no external existence, according to what they have stated and explained concerning its cause. There is no doubt that this is a matter of imagination, not true witnessing. And if one of us were to command and forbid from himself according to what agrees with expediency and suits reason, he would not be a prophet by consensus of the rational people. So how can the one whose command and prohibition are of the kind that revert to pure imaginings with no basis whatsoever be a prophet? Or perhaps he contradicts what he calls to, and reason also. This is as has passed. His statement [i.e., the objector's]: 'Like the sick and the insane'—it may be answered that the gist of their statement is that when the human soul connects with the high principles, the imaginative faculty represents the disembodied intellects and celestial souls as sensible forms, especially the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) which has special relation to the elemental world, and addresses him with audible speech, and that is from the presence of God and His angels, and in it is an imagining of an image of an existing thing. And how great is the difference between this and the imagining of something that has no existence at all, like the sick and the insane?
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed account of the philosopher's third property of a prophet: the ability to see angels in sensible form and hear their speech as revelation. The explanation parallels the experience of dreaming: the soul's disengagement from bodily concerns allows it to connect with the holy realm (ʿālam al-quds), and the imaginative faculty clothes intelligible realities in sensory forms. The commentator then launches a critical response. First, he argues that this claim contradicts the philosophers' own doctrines: they do not affirm visible angels with speech. For them, angels are either disembodied souls attached to celestial spheres or disembodied intellects; speech is a property of bodies, and they deny God's speech as composed of sounds. Thus, the supposed vision and hearing reduce to mere imagination (takhyīl) of non-existent things, akin to the delusions of the sick and insane. He then points out that if a prophet's commands were based on such groundless imaginings, he would not be considered a prophet by rational consensus. However, the passage ends with a possible answer (on behalf of the philosophers) introduced by 'qāla' (His statement): the response distinguishes the prophet's imagination from pathological delusion. The prophet's imagination depicts real entities (disembodied intellects and souls) and is from God, whereas the insane imagine non-existent things. So there is a fundamental difference.

Key Terms
قوة متخيلة
حس مشترك
عالم القدس
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the imaginative faculty do with the intelligible imprinted in the soul?
A) It creates a new intelligible.
B) It clothes it with the garment of the sensible and inscribes it in the common sense.
C) It removes it from the soul.
D) It transforms it into a dream.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers explain prophetic vision as a natural psychological process where the imaginative faculty renders intelligible realities in sensory form, analogous to dreaming.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage describes the prophet's soul disengaging from bodily distractions to connect with the holy realm. In light of this, examine your own life: what bodily distractions most prevent you from experiencing a deeper connection with Allah? What concrete step will you take today to minimize one of those distractions?
?
The philosophers say that the imaginative faculty clothes intelligible realities in sensible forms. Have you ever dismissed a profound spiritual insight because it came in a 'sensible' form that seemed too ordinary? How might you become more attentive to the 'garments' of divine truths in your daily experiences?
?
The prophet is said to have achieved a fixed disposition (malaka) such that witnessing occurred with minimal attention. Do you possess any spiritual habit that has become so ingrained that it requires no effort? If not, what is one small practice you can begin today to work towards that station?

Prophethood
intermediate

The Critique: Contradiction with Philosophical Doctrines
Al-Iji refutes the philosophers' third property as contradictory to their own system.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's argument that the philosopher's account of the third property is inconsistent with their own metaphysical doctrines regarding angels and speech.

Core Idea
The philosophers' explanation of prophetic vision contradicts their denial of sensible angels and divine speech.

Having presented the philosopher's account, the commentator immediately challenges its coherence. He argues that the very mechanism described contradicts the philosophers' own principles, particularly their rejection of angels that can be seen and heard. This lesson dissects that critique.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and translation carefully. Note the commentator's accusation of deception (talbis).
2
Identify the core inconsistency
The commentator says the philosophers do not affirm visible angels. What do they affirm instead?
Angels are either disembodied souls attached to spheres or disembodied intellects.
3
Understand the denial of divine speech
Speech is a property of bodies; separate entities have no speech. So how can the prophet hear angelic speech?
This contradicts the third property's requirement of hearing.
4
Grasp the accusation of imagination
The commentator concludes that the prophet's vision is mere imagination (takhyīl), like the delusions of the sick and insane, pointing to his analogy.
This is a serious charge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the philosophers offer a coherent naturalistic explanation of prophecy.
Correction: The commentator shows that their explanation contradicts their own metaphysical principles, thus it is not a genuine account.

Checkpoint
According to the commentator, what is the problem with the philosophers' claim that prophets see angels?
A) The philosophers deny the existence of prophecy.
B) The philosophers do not believe in angels that can be seen; their angels are disembodied intellects or souls without sensible forms.
C) The philosophers affirm angels but deny they can speak.
D) The philosophers claim angels are only seen in dreams.

Key Takeaway
The commentator argues that the philosopher's account of prophetic vision is internally inconsistent because their metaphysics preclude sensible angels and divine speech.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear an argument that sounds sophisticated but deep down you sense it is covering a flaw, do you have the courage to expose it—or do you let it pass? The commentator calls this 'deception' (talbis). Where in your own thinking might you be using fancy words to hide an inconsistency in your faith?
?
The philosophers claimed the prophet's vision was real, but their own system made it impossible. Are there beliefs you hold that, if you were honest, contradict each other? Will you examine them today, or keep the comfortable inconsistency?
?
Imagine you are the prophet receiving commands based on 'pure imaginings with no basis.' The commentator says such a person would not be a prophet. Does your life reflect that you follow a prophet whose message is rooted in truth and consistency, or do you sometimes follow your own imaginings? What will you change to align with the truth?

Imagination vs. Reality: The Objection and Its Answer
Al-Iji presents the philosophers' answer to the objection that prophetic vision is like delusion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the prophet's experience is akin to the delusions of the sick and the philosopher's response distinguishing between imagining what exists and imagining what does not.

Core Idea
The prophet's imagination depicts real entities from the holy realm, unlike the insane who imagine non-existent things.

The commentator previously compared the prophet's experience to the delusions of the sick and insane. Now, he presents a possible objection (from the philosophers) that distinguishes between the two: the prophet's imaginative faculty represents real entities (disembodied intellects and souls), whereas the insane imagine things with no external reality. This lesson examines that rebuttal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic source and translation. Notice the phrase 'qāla' which introduces the objector's statement.
2
Understand the objector's main point
The objector claims that the philosopher's explanation involves imagining an image (shabah) of something that actually exists, unlike the insane who imagine non-existent things.
3
Identify the specific example
The objector highlights the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) as having a special relation to the elemental world.
This reinforces the connection to reality.
4
Grasp the final contrast
The passage ends with a rhetorical question emphasizing the difference between imagining an existing thing and imagining nothing at all.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And they have no speech that can be heard, because speech is a property of bodies, since letters and sound in their view are accidents of undulating air, as has preceded. So no real speech is conceivable for the separate entities. And the outcome of what they mentioned concerning the third property is a representation (takhyīl) of something that has no existence in reality, like the sick and the insane, for they witness what has no external existence, according to what they have stated and explained concerning its cause. There is no doubt that this is a matter of imagination, not true witnessing. And if one of us were to command and forbid from himself according to what agrees with expediency and suits reason, he would not be a prophet by consensus of the rational people. So how can the one whose command and prohibition are of the kind that revert to pure imaginings with no basis whatsoever be a prophet? Or perhaps he contradicts what he calls to, and reason also. This is as has passed. His statement [i.e., the objector's]: 'Like the sick and the insane'—it may be answered that the gist of their statement is that when the human soul connects with the high principles, the imaginative faculty represents the disembodied intellects and celestial souls as sensible forms, especially the Active Intellect (al-ʿaql al-faʿʿāl) which has special relation to the elemental world, and addresses him with audible speech, and that is from the presence of God and His angels, and in it is an imagining of an image of an existing thing. And how great is the difference between this and the imagining of something that has no existence at all, like the sick and the insane?
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed account of the philosopher's third property of a prophet: the ability to see angels in sensible form and hear their speech as revelation. The explanation parallels the experience of dreaming: the soul's disengagement from bodily concerns allows it to connect with the holy realm (ʿālam al-quds), and the imaginative faculty clothes intelligible realities in sensory forms. The commentator then launches a critical response. First, he argues that this claim contradicts the philosophers' own doctrines: they do not affirm visible angels with speech. For them, angels are either disembodied souls attached to celestial spheres or disembodied intellects; speech is a property of bodies, and they deny God's speech as composed of sounds. Thus, the supposed vision and hearing reduce to mere imagination (takhyīl) of non-existent things, akin to the delusions of the sick and insane. He then points out that if a prophet's commands were based on such groundless imaginings, he would not be considered a prophet by rational consensus. However, the passage ends with a possible answer (on behalf of the philosophers) introduced by 'qāla' (His statement): the response distinguishes the prophet's imagination from pathological delusion. The prophet's imagination depicts real entities (disembodied intellects and souls) and is from God, whereas the insane imagine non-existent things. So there is a fundamental difference.

Key Terms
عقل فعال
مبادئ عالية
أشباح مصورة

Checkpoint
What is the key distinction the objector makes between the prophet's imagination and that of the sick?
A) The prophet's imagination is more vivid.
B) The prophet imagines only in sleep.
C) The prophet imagines an image of an existing thing, while the sick imagine non-existent things.
D) The sick imagine only demons.

Key Takeaway
The philosopher defends the third property by arguing that the prophet's imaginative vision is of real, existing entities from the holy realm, unlike groundless delusions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the prophet's imagination connects to the high principles and depicts real entities. What 'high principles' do you connect your imagination to? Are your daily thoughts and fantasies anchored in the reality of God, angels, and the unseen, or are they filled with illusions of this world? Will you take one concrete step today to redirect your imagination toward what is true and lasting?
?
The philosopher distinguishes between imagining something that exists and imagining something that has no existence at all. Think about the stories you tell yourself—about your identity, your failures, your future. Are they based on truth, or are they delusions that have no real basis? How can you actively confront and replace those false narratives with the reality of God's promises and your purpose?
?
The source says the prophet's experience is considered 'from the presence of God and His angels'. Do you live with a sense of that presence? Or have you reduced spiritual realities to abstract concepts that have no power over your choices? What would change in your life if you truly believed that the unseen realm is more real than what you see? Commit to one action that reflects that belief.

Prophethood

Social Submission and the Necessity of a Prophet
Al-Iji explains how the three properties lead to societal obedience and the need for a revealed law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the three properties of the prophet lead to societal submission, establish a just law, and why such a lawgiver is necessary for human civilization.

Core Idea
A prophet is necessary because human society naturally needs a just law to prevent chaos.

Al-Iji now presents the philosophers' conclusion: the three properties combine to command submission, which establishes a Shari'a. This Shari'a is essential for human cooperation, as humans are social by nature and cannot live without mutual transactions and a just law. Without it, conflict and disorder would prevail, making a divinely guided lawgiver an inevitable necessity.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the logical flow from properties to submission to law to necessity.
2
Identify the three properties and their effect
The passage begins by saying that whoever possesses the three properties (discussed earlier) gains submission of souls. Find this cause-and-effect relationship.
The submission is both outward and inward.
3
Understand the necessity of cooperation
The text explains why humans need to cooperate: no individual can survive alone. Look for the examples of baking, grinding, and farming.
This leads to the idea that humans are 'civic by nature' (مدني بالطبع).
4
Trace the argument to the necessity of a prophet
Follow how the need for a just law (Shari'a) arises from the necessity of cooperation, and how without it chaos would occur. Conclude with the divine care (عناية) argument that a prophet must exist.
This completes the philosophers' proof for prophethood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنهم قالوا : من اجتمعت فيه هذه الخواص الثلاث انقادت له النفوس البشرية المختلفة بطوعها مع ما جبلت عليه من الآباء من اختلاف من عمله عن الانقياد لبني نوعها وذلت له الهمم المتفاوتة على ما عليه الآراء فيصير ذلك الانقياد التام ظاهراً وباطناً سبباً لقرار أي ثبات الشريعة التي بها يتم التعاون الضروري لنوع الإنسان وإنما كان التعاون ضرروياً لهذا النوع من حيث أنه لا يستقل واحد منهم بما يحتاج إليه في معاشه من مأكله ومشربه وملبسه دون مشاركة من أبناء جنسه في المعاملات وهو أن يعمل كل واحد لآخر مثل ما يعمله الآخر له والمعاوضات وهي أن يعطي كل واحد صاحبه بإزاء ما يؤخذ منه من عمله ألا ترى أنه لو انفرد إنسان وحده لم يتيسر أو لم تحسن معيشته بل لا بد له من أن يكون معه آخرون من بني نوعه حتى يخبز هذا لذلك ويطحن ذاك لهذا ويزرع لهما ثالث وهكذا فإذا اجتمعوا على هذا الوجه صار أمرهم مكفياً ولذلك قيل الإنسان مدني بالطبع فإن التمدن هو هذا الاجتماع ولا بد لهم في التعاون من معاملة ومعاوضة يجريان بينهم ولا بد فيهما من قانون عدل يحافظ عليه دفعاً للظلم وإليه أشار بقوله : ( ولولا شريعة ينقاد لها الخاص والعالم لاشرأبت كل نفس أي مدت عنقها إلى ما يريده غيره وطمح أي ارتفع عين كل إلى ما الآخر فحصل بينهم التنازع وأدى ذلك التنازع إلى التواثب والتشاجر أي الاختلاف والتقاتل والتناحر وشمل الناس الهرج أي القتل والمرج أي الاختلاط واختل أمور المعاش والمعاد فوجب في الطبيعة وجود الموصوف بتلك الخواص لما علم من شمول العناية فيما أعطى كل حيوان من الآلات اللائقة به
Then they said: Whoever combines these three properties, the diverse human souls will submit to him willingly, despite the natural disposition towards stubbornness from their fathers [?] and the difference in their actions against submission to their own kind, and the varying ambitions will become humbled according to opinions. So that complete submission, outwardly and inwardly, becomes a cause for the establishment of the Shari'a (law) through which the necessary cooperation for the human species is achieved. And cooperation is necessary for this species, in that no single individual can independently obtain what he needs for his livelihood in terms of food, drink, and clothing without the participation of others of his kind in transactions (muʿāmalāt) and exchanges (muʿāwaḍāt). Transactions are that each person works for another like what the other works for him, and exchanges are that each gives to his counterpart in return for what he takes from his work. Do you not see that if a person were alone, his life would not be easy, nor would it be good? Rather, he must have others of his kind, so that one bakes for that one, another grinds for this one, a third cultivates for both, and so on. When they come together in this manner, their affair becomes sufficient. Therefore it is said: 'Human beings are civic (madani) by nature'; for civilization (tamaddun) is this social organization. And in cooperation, they must have transaction and exchange among themselves, and there must be a just law to preserve it, preventing oppression. He (the author) alludes to this by saying: 'Were it not for a law (sharīʿa) to which the elite and the commoner submit, every soul would stretch its neck (i.e., covet) what another desires, and each eye would gaze upon the property of another, so conflict would arise among them, and that conflict would lead to assault, quarrel, fighting, and mutual hatred, and people would be overwhelmed by chaos (harj) i.e., killing, and confusion (marj) i.e., disorder, and the affairs of this world and the Hereafter would be ruined. So it is necessary in nature that the one described by those properties exists, given the known comprehensiveness of divine care (ʿināya) in providing each animal with its suitable instruments.'
Source pages p. 244

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' conclusion: the three properties together result in the prophet commanding such authority that diverse human souls submit to him willingly, despite their natural independence. This submission establishes a stable Shari'a, which is necessary for human cooperation. The text then explains why cooperation is essential: humans cannot survive alone; they need food, clothing, etc., which require mutual transactions and exchanges. This leads to social organization (tamaddun), for which a just law is necessary to prevent oppression. Without such a law, humans would fall into conflict, killing, and disorder. Therefore, by the principle of divine care (ʿināya), a person possessing these properties (a prophet) must exist in nature to provide this law. The argument is teleological: a perfect world order requires a divinely guided lawgiver.

Key Terms

تعاون
معاملة
معاوضة
مدني بالطبع
تمدن
عناية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessity of a prophet is purely rational and abstract.
Correction: The text grounds it in observable social needs: humans require cooperation and a just law to avoid chaos, so a prophet is a practical necessity from divine care.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a prophet necessary for human society?
A) To perform miracles and demonstrate power.
B) To provide a just law (Shari'a) that enables cooperation and prevents conflict.
C) To teach abstract philosophy to the elites.
D) To establish a political dynasty.

Key Takeaway
The necessity of a prophet is not merely rational but grounded in the observable social order: without a just law, human society falls into chaos, so a divinely guided lawgiver is indispensable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The lesson says that complete submission must be outwardly and inwardly. In what area of your life is your submission only outward, while inwardly you resent God's limits? Will you examine that this week and repent?
?
If you truly believed that without a prophet's law human society would be destroyed, would you live differently with your neighbors? What is one transaction today where you can act justly as part of that divine law?

The Meaning of Prophet and the Three Characteristics
Al-Jurjani defines the prophet and lists three characteristics that cause human submission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Jurjani distinguishes true prophets from those who merely imagine things and identify the three key characteristics that define a prophet.

Core Idea
A true prophet is distinguished by three divine traits that command universal submission.

In this passage, al-Jurjani addresses a common objection that prophetic experiences are like the hallucinations of the sick or insane. He explains that prophetic visions have a real basis in connection to higher principles. He then presents the philosophers' definition of a prophet as one who possesses three specific characteristics: the willing submission of human souls, the humility of diverse aspirations, and the establishment of a divine law (shari'a).

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the opening lines and notice the analogy with the sick and insane. What is the objection being raised against prophets?
The objection is that prophetic visions are mere imagination, like hallucinations.
2
Understand the response
Al-Jurjani responds that prophetic imagination is different because it arises from connection to higher realities. Note the contrast between baseless imagination and true prophetic experience.
3
List the three characteristics
Find the three characteristics that the philosophers say define a prophet: (1) submission of souls, (2) humility of aspirations, (3) establishment of a law. Write them down.
These are mentioned in the sentence beginning with 'ثم إنهم قالوا'.
4
Reflect on the outcome
The passage states that these characteristics cause complete submission, which is the basis for the shari'a. Consider why this submission is important for human society.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في معنى النبي كما للمرضى والمجانين فإنهم يشاهدون ما لا وجود له في الخارج على ما صرحوا به وقرروا ما هو السبب فيه ولا شك أن ذلك إنما يكون على سبيل التخيل دون المشاهدة الحقيقة ولو كان أحدنا آمراً وناهياً من قبل نفسه بما يوافق المصلحة ويلائم العقل لم يكن نبياً باتفاق من العقلاء فكيف يكون نبياً من كان أمره ونهيه من قبيل ما يرجع إلى تخيلات لا أصل لها قطعاً أو ربما خالف ما دعا إليه المعقول أيضاً هذا كما مضى ثم إنهم قالوا من اجتمعت فيه هذه الخواص الثلاث انقادت له النفوس البشرية المختلفة بطوعها مع ما جبلت عليه من الآباء من اختلاف من عملة عن الانقياد لبني نوعها وذلت له شريعة ينقاد لها الخاص والعالم لاشرأبت كل نفس أي مدت عنقها إلى ما يريده غيره وطمح أي ارتفع عين كل إلى ما الآخر فحصل بينهم التنازع وأدى ذلك التنازع إلى التواثب والتشاجر أي الاختلاف والتقاتل والتناحر فتأمل
First Observatory - First Objective: On the meaning of prophet. Like the sick and insane, for they see what has no existence externally, according to what they have stated and they determined what is the cause of it, and there is no doubt that this only occurs by way of imagination without true observation. And if one of us were a commander and prohibitor from his own self in accordance with what agrees with benefit and suits reason, he would not be a prophet by consensus of the rational people. So how can one be a prophet whose command and prohibition are of the kind that return to imaginations with absolutely no basis, or perhaps contradicts what he calls to, which is also rational? This is as has passed. Then they said: 'He in whom these three characteristics are combined, the different human souls submit to him willingly, despite what they are naturally disposed to from their fathers of differing [natures], from [their] practice of refusing submission to their own kind, and the diverse aspirations are humbled to him according to what opinions are upon. So that complete submission, outwardly and inwardly, becomes a cause for the establishment – i.e., the firmness – of the sharī'a by which the necessary cooperation for the human species is accomplished. And cooperation is necessary for this species because not one of them is independent in what he needs for his livelihood of food, drink, and clothing without the participation of members of his kind in transactions – i.e., each one works for another like what the other works for him – and exchanges – i.e., each gives to his counterpart in return for what is taken from him of his work. Do you not see that if a person were alone by himself, his life would not be easy or good? Rather, he must have others of his kind so that this one bakes for that one, that one grinds for this one, and a third farms for both, and so on. When they gather in this manner, their affair becomes sufficient. Therefore it is said: 'Man is civil by nature,' for civilization is this gathering. And they must, in cooperation, have transactions and exchanges that occur between them, and in these there must be a just law preserved to repel injustice. And to this he alluded by his saying: 'And were it not for a sharī'a to which the elite and the common submit, every soul would stretch its neck – i.e., extend it – to what another desires, and the eye of each would rise – i.e., be elevated – toward what the other has, so conflict would occur between them, and that conflict would lead to leaping upon each other and wrangling – i.e., disagreement – and fighting and mutual hatred, and the people would be encompassed by harj – i.e., killing – and marj – i.e., confusion – and the affairs of this world and the Hereafter would become disordered. So it is necessary in nature that the one described with those characteristics exist, because it is known from the comprehensiveness of providence in that it gave every animal the instruments suitable to it. His saying: 'Like the sick and insane' may be answered by saying that the gist of their speech is that when the human soul connects to the high principles, its imaginative faculty represents to it the abstract intellects and celestial souls as formed apparitions, especially the active intellect which has a special connection to the world of elements, so it addresses him with audible speech and he becomes before God and His angels, and in it there is an imagination of the form of an existent. What a difference between this and the imagination of what has no existence at all, originally, as with the sick and insane. And He guided – i.e., each one of them to what contains its survival and by which its subsistence is established – for the human species, for providence concerning it in giving and guidance is greater, and it is the noblest of the animal species; He subjected to it whatever is other than it of those species. And this – i.e., the existence of one in whom the mentioned characteristics are combined – is among its greatest interests because of what appears in it of bringing great benefits and repelling severe harms. Do you think that nature would neglect that? No. The conclusion is that the existence of a prophet is a cause for order in this world and the Hereafter, so that is necessary in divine providence which requires the most perfect forms of order in His creatures. This is the method of proving prophethood according to the philosophers. His saying: 'Do you think nature [would neglect that]?' – 'nature' according to them is the existence of complete order, as stated in Sharh al-Maqāsid. And it appears that attributing non-neglect to nature is a rational metaphor, like 'my day fasted.' But if by nature its cause, i.e., providence, is meant, then consider.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of prophethood, presented on the method of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'). It begins by addressing an objection: the visions of the sick and insane are not genuine prophetic experiences but mere imaginations. The author then presents the philosophers' argument: one who possesses three specific characteristics—complete submission of human souls, humility of diverse aspirations, and establishment of a sharī'a—is a prophet. Human society requires cooperation, which in turn requires a just law to prevent conflict. This law must come from a prophet who has these characteristics. The argument then draws on the principle of divine providence: nature provides every animal with what it needs, so it would not neglect the greater need for a prophet to establish order. The passage ends with a gloss clarifying that 'nature' here means the complete order, and the attribution is metaphorical. The commentary explains each step: the objection, the three characteristics, the need for a law, the consequence of conflict without law, the argument from providence, and the final conclusion that prophethood is necessary.

نبوة
تخيل
خواص
انقياد
شريعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Prophets are just people who have hallucinations or visions like the insane.
Correction: Prophetic visions are not baseless; they come from the soul's connection to higher principles, and true prophets are validated by three objective characteristics that bring about social harmony.

Key Takeaway
A true prophet is not defined by visions alone but by three divine traits that cause universal submission and establish a just law.

Prophethood

The Necessity of Prophethood for Social Order
Al-Jurjani proves the necessity of prophethood from social necessity and divine providence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophical proof that human society requires a prophet, how divine providence guarantees this, and how the objection that prophetic visions are hallucinations is refuted.

Core Idea
Without a divinely appointed prophet, human cooperation collapses into chaos.

In this part, al-Jurjani expands on why a prophet is necessary for human society. He explains that humans are naturally social and require cooperation, which in turn requires a just law. Without a divine law (shari'a), conflict and disorder ensue. He then invokes the principle of divine providence: just as God provides for every creature's needs, He must also provide a prophet to order human life. The passage ends by addressing a remaining objection about the nature of prophetic vision.

Guided Path
1
Understand the need for cooperation
Read the opening sentences about human dependence on one another for food, drink, and clothing. Note that no one can live alone.
This establishes the natural basis for society.
2
Identify the requirement of a just law
The passage states that cooperation requires exchanges and transactions, which need a just law to prevent injustice. Highlight the quote about the consequences of lacking a shari'a.
The quote uses vivid imagery: 'every soul would stretch its neck'.
3
Follow the argument from providence
Al-Jurjani argues that since nature provides for all creatures, it must also provide a prophet for the most noble species. See how he concludes that the existence of a prophet is necessary in divine providence.
4
Examine the response to the hallucination objection
The final part addresses the earlier objection more fully, explaining that prophetic imagination is a representation of real higher beings, unlike the empty imaginations of the sick.
This reinforces the definition from the first lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الأول : في معنى النبي كما للمرضى والمجانين فإنهم يشاهدون ما لا وجود له في الخارج على ما صرحوا به وقرروا ما هو السبب فيه ولا شك أن ذلك إنما يكون على سبيل التخيل دون المشاهدة الحقيقة ولو كان أحدنا آمراً وناهياً من قبل نفسه بما يوافق المصلحة ويلائم العقل لم يكن نبياً باتفاق من العقلاء فكيف يكون نبياً من كان أمره ونهيه من قبيل ما يرجع إلى تخيلات لا أصل لها قطعاً أو ربما خالف ما دعا إليه المعقول أيضاً هذا كما مضى ثم إنهم قالوا من اجتمعت فيه هذه الخواص الثلاث انقادت له النفوس البشرية المختلفة بطوعها مع ما جبلت عليه من الآباء من اختلاف من عملة عن الانقياد لبني نوعها وذلت له الهمم المتفاوتة على ما عليه الآراء فيصير ذلك الانقياد التام ظاهراً وباطناً سبباً لقرار أي ثبات الشريعة التي بها يتم التعاون الضروري لنوع الإنسان وإنما كان التعاون ضرورياً لهذا النوع من حيث أنه لا يستقل واحد منهم بما يحتاج إليه في معاشه من مأكله ومشربه وملبسه دون مشاركة من أبناء جنسه في المعاملات وهو أن يعمل كل واحد لآخر مثل ما يعمله الآخر له والمعاوضات وهي أن يعطي كل واحد صاحبه بإزاء ما يؤخذ منه من عمله ألا ترى أنه لو انفرد إنسان وحده لم يتيسر أو لم تحسن معيشته بل لا بد له من أن يكون معه آخرون من بني نوعه حتى يخبز هذا لذلك ويطحن ذاك لهذا ويزرع لهما ثالث وهكذا فإذا اجتمعوا على هذا الوجه صار أمرهم مكفياً ولذلك قيل الإنسان مدني بالطبع فإن التمدن هو هذا الاجتماع ولا بد لهم في التعاون من معاملة ومعاوضة يجريان بينهم ولا بد فيهما من قانون عدل يحافظ عليه دفعاً للظلم وإليه أشار بقوله ولولا شريعة ينقاد لها الخاص والعالم لاشرأبت كل نفس أي مدت عنقها إلى ما يريده غيره وطمح أي ارتفع عين كل إلى ما الآخر فحصل بينهم التنازع وأدى ذلك التنازع إلى التواثب والتشاجر أي الاختلاف والتقاتل والتناحر وشمل الناس الهرج أي القتل والمرج أي الاختلاط واختل أمور المعاش والمعاد فوجب في الطبيعة وجود الموصوف بتلك الخواص لما علم من شمول العناية فيما أعطى كل حيوان من الآلات اللائقة به قوله كما للمرضى والمجانين قد يجاب عنه بأن خلاصة كلامهم أن النفس الإنسانية إذا اتصلت بالمبادئ العالية يتمثل بقوته المتخيلة العقول المجردة والنفوس السماوية أشباحاً مصورة سيما العقل الفعال الذي له زيادة اختصاص بعالم العناصر فيخاطبه بكلام مسموع ويكون قبل الله تعالى وملائكته وفيه تخيل صورة لموجود وكم بينه وبين تخيل ما لا وجود له ذلك من قبل أصلاً كما للمرضى والمجانين وهدى أي كل واحد منه إلى ما فيه بقاؤه وبه قوامه لما نوع الإنسان فإن العناية به في الإعطاء والهداية أكثر وهو أشرف الأنواع الحيوانية سخر له ما عداه من تلك الأنواع وهذا أي وجود من اجتمعت فيه الخواص المذكورة من أعظم مصالحه لما ظهر فيه من جلب المنافع الجليلة والدفع لمضار الشديدة أفترى الطبيعة تهمل ذلك كلا والحاصل أن وجود النبي سبب للنظام في المعاش والمعاد فيجب ذلك في العناية الإلهية المقتضية لأبلغ وجوه الانتظام في مخلوقاته فهذه طريقة إثبات النبوة على مذهب الحكماء قوله افترى الطبيعة الطبيعة عندهم وجود النظام الكامل صرح به في شرح المقاصد والظاهر أن إسناد عدم الإهمال إلى الطبيعة مجاز عقلي على نحو ما صام نهاري وإن أريد بالطبيعة سببها أعني العناية فتأمل
First Observatory - First Objective: On the meaning of prophet. Like the sick and insane, for they see what has no existence externally, according to what they have stated and they determined what is the cause of it, and there is no doubt that this only occurs by way of imagination without true observation. And if one of us were a commander and prohibitor from his own self in accordance with what agrees with benefit and suits reason, he would not be a prophet by consensus of the rational people. So how can one be a prophet whose command and prohibition are of the kind that return to imaginations with absolutely no basis, or perhaps contradicts what he calls to, which is also rational? This is as has passed. Then they said: 'He in whom these three characteristics are combined, the different human souls submit to him willingly, despite what they are naturally disposed to from their fathers of differing [natures], from [their] practice of refusing submission to their own kind, and the diverse aspirations are humbled to him according to what opinions are upon. So that complete submission, outwardly and inwardly, becomes a cause for the establishment – i.e., the firmness – of the sharī'a by which the necessary cooperation for the human species is accomplished. And cooperation is necessary for this species because not one of them is independent in what he needs for his livelihood of food, drink, and clothing without the participation of members of his kind in transactions – i.e., each one works for another like what the other works for him – and exchanges – i.e., each gives to his counterpart in return for what is taken from him of his work. Do you not see that if a person were alone by himself, his life would not be easy or good? Rather, he must have others of his kind so that this one bakes for that one, that one grinds for this one, and a third farms for both, and so on. When they gather in this manner, their affair becomes sufficient. Therefore it is said: 'Man is civil by nature,' for civilization is this gathering. And they must, in cooperation, have transactions and exchanges that occur between them, and in these there must be a just law preserved to repel injustice. And to this he alluded by his saying: 'And were it not for a sharī'a to which the elite and the common submit, every soul would stretch its neck – i.e., extend it – to what another desires, and the eye of each would rise – i.e., be elevated – toward what the other has, so conflict would occur between them, and that conflict would lead to leaping upon each other and wrangling – i.e., disagreement – and fighting and mutual hatred, and the people would be encompassed by harj – i.e., killing – and marj – i.e., confusion – and the affairs of this world and the Hereafter would become disordered. So it is necessary in nature that the one described with those characteristics exist, because it is known from the comprehensiveness of providence in that it gave every animal the instruments suitable to it. His saying: 'Like the sick and insane' may be answered by saying that the gist of their speech is that when the human soul connects to the high principles, its imaginative faculty represents to it the abstract intellects and celestial souls as formed apparitions, especially the active intellect which has a special connection to the world of elements, so it addresses him with audible speech and he becomes before God and His angels, and in it there is an imagination of the form of an existent. What a difference between this and the imagination of what has no existence at all, originally, as with the sick and insane. And He guided – i.e., each one of them to what contains its survival and by which its subsistence is established – for the human species, for providence concerning it in giving and guidance is greater, and it is the noblest of the animal species; He subjected to it whatever is other than it of those species. And this – i.e., the existence of one in whom the mentioned characteristics are combined – is among its greatest interests because of what appears in it of bringing great benefits and repelling severe harms. Do you think that nature would neglect that? No. The conclusion is that the existence of a prophet is a cause for order in this world and the Hereafter, so that is necessary in divine providence which requires the most perfect forms of order in His creatures. This is the method of proving prophethood according to the philosophers. His saying: 'Do you think nature [would neglect that]?' – 'nature' according to them is the existence of complete order, as stated in Sharh al-Maqāsid. And it appears that attributing non-neglect to nature is a rational metaphor, like 'my day fasted.' But if by nature its cause, i.e., providence, is meant, then consider.
Source pages p. 245

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the necessity of prophethood, presented on the method of the philosophers (al-ḥukamā'). It begins by addressing an objection: the visions of the sick and insane are not genuine prophetic experiences but mere imaginations. The author then presents the philosophers' argument: one who possesses three specific characteristics—complete submission of human souls, humility of diverse aspirations, and establishment of a sharī'a—is a prophet. Human society requires cooperation, which in turn requires a just law to prevent conflict. This law must come from a prophet who has these characteristics. The argument then draws on the principle of divine providence: nature provides every animal with what it needs, so it would not neglect the greater need for a prophet to establish order. The passage ends with a gloss clarifying that 'nature' here means the complete order, and the attribution is metaphorical. The commentary explains each step: the objection, the three characteristics, the need for a law, the consequence of conflict without law, the argument from providence, and the final conclusion that prophethood is necessary.

تعاون
معاملة
معاوضة
شريعة
عدل
عناية
طبيعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Human society can function properly without a divinely revealed law.
Correction: Without a divine law (shari'a) enforced by a prophet, human selfishness leads to conflict and chaos, as argued in the passage.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the direct consequence of lacking a just law (shari'a)?
A) People become more cooperative
B) People submit to a prophet
C) Conflict, killing, and confusion (harj and marj)
D) Everyone becomes wealthy

Key Takeaway
Human social nature requires a divinely guided prophet to establish a just law; divine providence guarantees such a prophet exists to maintain order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this argument, the prophet is essential to prevent people from selfishly grabbing what others desire. Consider your own interactions: when you ignore or circumvent the shari'a in your daily dealings (business, family, etc.), what small conflicts or injustices have you allowed, and how might you be contributing to the very chaos the passage warns about?
?
The passage claims that without a divine law, 'the affairs of this world and the Hereafter would become disordered.' Do you honestly treat the Prophet's teachings as the ultimate criterion for your social behavior, or do you rely more on your own judgment or societal norms? What would change if you fully submitted to the shari'a in areas where you now make exceptions?
?
Divine providence is said to provide for every animal, so surely it provides a prophet for humans. Yet many people deny the need for prophecy. Does your heart truly trust that God's guidance through the Prophet is the only path to true order, or do you sometimes think that human systems could suffice? What would it cost you to live as if this passage is absolute truth?
