Fourth Objection: Answering the Impossible Obligation Charge
Presents Ash'ari and Mu'tazila responses to the fourth objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Ash'ari and Mu'tazila theologians respond to the objection that obligating an act when ability is already present is impossible.

Core Idea
Obligating an act with concurrent ability is not demanding the impossible.

The fourth objection claims that if ability is concurrent with the act, then obligating the act is impossible—like demanding the already attained. This passage presents two classical answers: one from the Ash'ari school and one from the Mu'tazila. Each school shows why the objection fails using different reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the concise style and use of parentheses.
2
Identify the two answers
The passage contains two separate answers: first the Ash'ari response, then the Mu'tazila response. Identify the key move each school makes to show the obligation is not impossible.
The shift from 'عندنا' to 'عند المعتزلة' marks the transition.
3
Analyze the analogy to creation
The Mu'tazila draw an analogy between obligation and God's bringing about (إحداث) the act. Understand how this analogy turns the objection back on the objectors.
The analogy uses the same concepts of attainment of the already attained and combination of opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) جواب الرابع: عندنا أن القدرة مع (الفعل كما مر، والتكليف به في هذه الحالة ليس تكليفاً بالمحال الذي هو تحصيل الحاصل وإنما يكون كذلك إن لو أن كان الفعل حاصلا بتحصيل سابق على التحصيل الذي هو ملتبس به وما ذكر من لا فائدة فيه حينئذ لوجوبه فإنما يتم إذا وجب الغرض في أفعاله تعالى وهو باطل. ( و ) جواب الرابع : ( عند المعتزلة أن التكليف قبل الفعل ليس ذلك تكليفاً بما لا يطاق لأن التكليف ( في الحال إنما هو ( بالإيقاع في ثاني الحال لا بالإيقاع في الحال ليكون جمعاً بين الوجود والعدم (وذلك ) أي التكليف (كالإحداث) يعني ما أوردتموه علينا في التكليف يلزمكم في إحداث الفعل فيقال : إحداثه إما حال وجوده فيكون تحصيلاً للحاصل، وإما حال عدمه فيكون جمعاً بين النقيضين (وهو) أي الإحداث ( مما لا شك فيه فما هو جوابكم في الأحداث (فهو جوابنا) في التكليف .
And the answer to the fourth (objection): according to us, ability is with the act as previously mentioned, and obligation to it in this case is not an obligation of the impossible, which is the attainment of the already attained. It would only be so if the act were already present by a prior attainment before the attainment with which it is associated. And what was mentioned of there being no benefit then due to its necessity is only completed if purpose were necessary in His actions, may He be exalted, which is false. And the answer to the fourth (objection) according to the Mu'tazila: obligation before the act is not obligation of that which cannot be performed, because obligation in the present moment is only to bring about in the second moment, not to bring about in the present moment, which would be a combination of existence and non-existence. And that—i.e. obligation—is like bringing about (creation). That means what you have objected to us concerning obligation applies to you concerning the bringing about of the act, for it is said: its bringing about is either at the time of its existence, thus it would be attainment of the already attained, or at the time of its non-existence, thus it would be a combination of two opposites. And it—i.e. bringing about—is beyond doubt. So whatever your answer is concerning bringing about, it is our answer concerning obligation.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage gives two answers to the fourth objection about obligation when ability is concurrent with the act. According to the Ash'ari view ('indana), ability exists with the act, so obligating the act at that moment is not obligating the impossible, because it is not demanding the already realized. The objection of no benefit due to necessity is rejected because purpose is not necessary in God's actions. According to the Mu'tazila, obligation before the act is not impossible because obligation is to bring about the act in the next moment, not in the present, avoiding the problem of demanding the impossible. They draw an analogy to God's bringing about (creation) of the act: if asked, one would not say it is impossible, so similarly obligation is not impossible.

Key Terms
القدرة مع الفعل
تحصيل الحاصل
الجمع بين النقيضين
الإحداث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Obligating an act at the moment ability exists is impossible because it demands the already attained.
Correction: This passage shows that according to Ash'aris, ability is with the act so no impossibility; according to Mu'tazila, obligation is for the next moment. The analogy to creation further shows the objection is invalid.

Checkpoint
According to the Mu'tazila view presented, when does obligation apply to the act?
A) At the same moment the act is performed
B) To bring about the act in the second moment
C) Before the ability exists
D) Only after the act is completed

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because ability with the act does not entail impossibility; obligation can be for a future moment or the act is already possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel unable to fulfill a religious duty, do you ever think that Allah is demanding the impossible from you? How does the Ash'ari teaching that ability comes with the act, not before, challenge the excuses you make for delaying or avoiding obedience? What is one duty you have been avoiding because you think you lack ability, and how will you act on it today trusting that ability will come with the act?
?
The Mu'tazila say obligation is for a future moment. Have you ever postponed a good deed thinking you are not capable now, but planning to do it later? Does that excuse hold up when you know that the very command to do it now is actually a command to bring it about in the next moment? What is one action you will commit to performing within the next 24 hours because of this lesson?
?
The passage states that God's actions are not bound by purpose (الغرض) as we understand it. When you do not see the wisdom behind a command, do you still obey or do you question? How does this truth about Allah's transcendence confront your desire to understand everything before submitting? Will you commit to obeying one command that you have been struggling to accept, simply because it is from Him?

Reflection on God as the Greatest Purpose of Obligation
Fifth objection and answer: reflection on God as chief purpose.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that reflecting on knowledge of God, His attributes, and His actions is the primary purpose of religious obligation, and that all other obligations serve to support this end.

Core Idea
Reflection on God is the ultimate goal; all other obligations are its servants.

In this passage, the author addresses a key objection: is reflection on God merely one of many purposes of obligation, or is it something more? The answer reveals a profound hierarchy in Islamic theology. Understanding this hierarchy reshapes how we see the role of all other religious duties.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
Pay attention to the phrasing 'بل هو العمدة الكبرى'.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the author first says reflection is 'one of the purposes' but then emphasizes it is 'the greatest mainstay'. This is a rhetorical intensification.
The Arabic uses 'بل' for correction/emphasis.
3
Examine the supporting role of other obligations
The passage lists three functions: they assist reflection (معينة), call to it (داعية), and serve as a means to reforming worldly life.
Consider how each function contributes to the primary goal.
4
Trace the chain to purification
Reforming worldly life helps purify time from distractions, which in turn lightens the burden of other obligations, making reflection easier.
This shows a practical benefit beyond the spiritual.
5
Reflect on the implication
If reflection on God is the greatest purpose, then all obligations are ultimately means to that end, not ends in themselves.
This can change how one prioritizes religious duties.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) جواب الخامس : إن ذلك ) أي التفكر في معرفة الله وصفاته وأفعاله ( أحد أغراض التكليف ( بل هو العمدة الكبرى منها ) وسائر التكاليف معينة عليه) داعية إليه ( ووسيلة إلى صلاح المعاش المعين على صفاء الأوقات عن المشوشات التي يربي شغلها على شغل التكاليف).
And the answer to the fifth (objection): that—i.e. reflection on knowledge of God, His attributes, and His actions—is one of the purposes of obligation, indeed it is the greatest mainstay of them, and the rest of the obligations assist it, call to it, and are a means to reforming worldly life, which helps in purifying times from distractions whose occupation outweighs the occupation of obligations.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage presents the answer to the fifth objection. The author states that reflecting on knowledge of God, His attributes, and His actions is one of the purposes of obligation, indeed it is the greatest and most important. All other obligations assist this reflection and lead to it. They also serve as a means to reforming worldly life, which in turn helps to clear times from distractions that would otherwise preoccupy one more than the obligations themselves.

Key Terms

معرفة الله

Misconception: Reflection on God is just one obligation among many, separate from others.
Correction: The passage shows reflection is the greatest purpose, and all other obligations assist and call to it, making it central.

Checkpoint
What is said about reflection on God's knowledge in relation to other obligations?
A) It is less important than other obligations
B) It is the greatest purpose, and other obligations assist it
C) It is only for the elite, not for common people
D) It replaces the need for prophets

Key Takeaway
Reflection on God is the supreme purpose of obligation, supported by all other duties through assistance, calling, and facilitating worldly reform.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The author says all other obligations assist reflection on God. Which of your current religious duties do you perform as mere routines rather than as means to deepen your knowledge of God? What would change if you truly saw them as aids to reflection?
?
If reflection on God is the greatest purpose, why do you spend more mental energy on worldly concerns than on contemplating His attributes? What concrete step will you take today to reverse that priority?

The Skeptic Argument: Reason as Sufficient for Obligations
Introduction of the third group and their rationalist argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the position of those who claimed reason alone is sufficient for moral knowledge and their argument, and learn the specific groups that held this view.

Core Idea
Some groups argue reason can judge good and evil, making prophethood unnecessary.

The third group in the discussion on prophethood is composed of those who deny the need for divine messengers, claiming human reason is enough to know obligations. This includes the Brahmans, Sabians, and reincarnationists, though some accept limited prophethood. Let's examine their argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the groups mentioned
Read the passage and list the groups that are said to hold the view that reason suffices. Note the variations among them.
They are not monolithic; some accept certain prophets.
2
Understand their core argument
The argument is that reason judges actions as good or evil, so actions are done or left accordingly. For actions not judged, they are done when needed and left when not.
3
Reflect on the logical structure
Notice that they classify actions into three categories based on reason's judgment, and then apply a rule to each.
This is a simplistic approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة الثالثة : من قال في العقل مندوحة عن البعثة ) إذ هو كاف في معرفة التكاليف فلا فائدة فيها ( وهم البراهمة والصابئة والتناسخية، غير أن من البراهمة من قال بنبوة آدم فقط ومنهم من قال بنبوة إبراهيم فقط ومن الصابئة من قال بنبوة شيث وإدريس فقط و هؤلاء كلهم (احتجوا بأن ما حكم العقل بحسنه ) من الأفعال ( يفعل وما حكم بقبحه يترك وما لم يحكم فيه بحسن ولا قبح يفعل عند الحاجة ) إليه ) لأن الحاجة ناجزة ) حاضرة فيجب اعتبارها دفعاً لمضرة فواتها ) ولا يعارضها مجرد الاحتمال ) أي احتمال المضرة بتقدير ) ويترك عند عدمها للاحتياط في دفع المضرة المتوهمة ( والجواب : بعد تسليم حكم العقل بالحسن والقبح ( أن الشرع ) المستفاد من البعثة (فائدته تفصيل ما أعطاه العقل إجمالاً من مراتب الحسن والقبح والمنفعة والمضرة ( وبيان ما يقصر عنه العقل ) ابتداء ) فإن القائلين بحكم العقل لا ينكرون أن من الأفعال ما لا يحكم) العقل ( فيه ) بشيء وذلك ) كوظائف العبادات وتعيين الحدود) ومقاديرها ( وتعليم ينفع وما يضر من الأفعال وذلك أي النبي الشارع ) كالطبيب ) الحاذق ( يعرف الأدوية وطبائعها وخواصها مما لو أمكن معرفتها للعامة بالتجربة ففي دهر طويل ) يجربون فيه ) أي في ذلك الدهر الطويل ( من فوائدها ( لعدم حصول العلم بها بعد ( ويقعون في المهالك قبل استكمالها أي قبل استكمال مدة التجربة إذ ربما يستعملون من الأدوية في تلك المدة ما يكون مهلكاً ولا يعلمون ذلك فيهلكهم ( مع أن اشتغالهم بذلك ) أي بتحصيل العلم بأحوال الأدوية بطريق التجربة ( يوجب إتعاب النفس وتعطل الصناعات ( الضرورية ( والشغل عن المصالح المعاش فإذا تسلموه من الطبيب خفت المؤونة وانتفعوا به وسلموا من تلك المضار ولا يقال في إمكانه معرفته ) أي معرفة ما ذكر ( غنى عن الطبيب ) فكذا لا يقال في إمكان معرفة التكاليف وأحوال الأفعال بتأمل العقل فيها غنى عن المبعوث ( كيف والنبي لا يعلم ما يعلم إلا من جهة (الله بخلاف الطبيب إذ يمكن التوصل إلى جميع ما يعلمه بمجرد الفكر والتجربة فإذا لم يكن هو مستغنى عنه كان النبي بذلك أولى ( وفيما تقدم من تقرير مذهب (الحكماء وهو أن الإنسان مدني بالطبع فلا بد له من قانون عدل محتاج إلى واضع يمتاز عن بني نوعه بما يدل على أن ما أتى به من عند ربه تتمة لهذا الكلام فإنه يدل على وجوب وجود النبي في العناية الأزلية المقتضية للنظام الأبلغ.
The third group: those who say that in reason there is sufficiency from the mission, because it is sufficient for knowing obligations, so there is no benefit in it. They are the Brahmans, Sabians, and reincarnationists. However, among the Brahmans some say only the prophethood of Adam, and among them some say only the prophethood of Abraham, and among the Sabians some say only the prophethood of Seth and Idris. All of these argued: that what reason judges as good among actions is done, what it judges as evil is left, and what it does not judge as good or evil is done when there is need for it, because the need is present, so it must be considered in order to repel the harm of losing it, and it is not countered by mere possibility, i.e. the possibility of harm in estimation, and it is left when there is no need out of caution in repelling supposed harm. And the answer, after acknowledging reason's judgment of good and evil: that the law derived from the mission its benefit is the detailing of what reason gave in general of the ranks of good and evil, benefit and harm, and the explanation of what reason falls short of initially. For those who affirm reason's judgment do not deny that among actions there are some about which reason judges nothing, such as the functions of worship, the specification of legal punishments (ḥudūd) and their amounts, and teaching what benefits and harms among actions. And that—i.e. the prophetic lawgiver—is like a skilled physician who knows medicines, their natures and properties, such that if common people could know them through experience, it would take a long time experimenting to obtain their benefits, and they would fall into destruction before completing it, because perhaps they would use some medicines in that period that are deadly without knowing it, and so it would destroy them. Moreover, their preoccupation with that, i.e. acquiring knowledge of the states of medicines through the method of experience, would cause weariness of the soul, suspension of necessary industries, and distraction from the interests of livelihood. So if they receive it from the physician, the burden is lightened, they benefit, and they are saved from those harms. And it is not said that in the possibility of knowing it, i.e. knowledge of what was mentioned, there is independence from the physician. Similarly, it cannot be said that in the possibility of knowing obligations and the states of actions through reason's reflection on them there is independence from the messenger. How, while the prophet only knows what he knows from God, unlike the physician, since everything he knows can be reached through mere thought and experience. So if he is not independent of him, then the prophet is more deserving of being indispensable. And in what preceded of the setting forth of the philosophers' doctrine, which is that man is by nature civil, so he inevitably needs a just law, requiring a legislator distinguished from his species by what indicates that what he brought is from his Lord, is a completion of this speech, for it indicates the necessity of the existence of a prophet in the eternal providence that requires the most perfect order.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage introduces the third group who believe reason is sufficient for knowing obligations, thus prophethood has no benefit. They include Brahmans, Sabians, and reincarnationists, though some of them accept limited prophethood. Their argument is that reason judges good and evil, so actions are done or left accordingly; for actions not judged, they are done when needed and left when not. The refutation acknowledges reason's capability but argues that divine law from prophethood details what reason grasps in general and explains what reason cannot reach, such as specific acts of worship and legal rulings. The author uses the analogy of a skilled physician: people would benefit from the physician's knowledge rather than discovering everything through lengthy and dangerous experience. Even if some knowledge could be obtained through reason, the risk and effort make the prophet necessary. Moreover, the prophet's knowledge comes directly from God, unlike a physician's knowledge which can be obtained through thought and experience, so the need for the prophet is even clearer. The passage concludes by referencing the philosophical argument that humans need a just law and a distinguished legislator, which is part of the eternal providence requiring the most perfect order.

Key Terms
البراهمة
الصابئة
التناسخية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All groups that deny prophethood reject all prophets.
Correction: Some of them accept limited prophethood (Adam, Abraham, Seth, Idris).

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT mentioned as a group holding the view that reason suffices?
A) Brahmans
B) Sabians
C) Reincarnationists
D) Atheists

Key Takeaway
The third group’s argument rests on reason’s ability to judge good, evil, and indifferent actions, making prophethood seem unnecessary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life have you trusted your own reasoning so completely that you feel no need for divine guidance? What would it cost you to submit that area to revelation?
?
When you face a moral decision that feels 'indifferent' to your reason, do you act simply out of convenience, or do you pause to seek God's command? Will you commit to finding a scriptural basis for one such decision this week?
?
The Brahmans and Sabians acknowledged some prophets but still denied the full mission. Is there a part of you that selectively accepts certain divine commands while dismissing others as unnecessary? Which command are you most tempted to rationalize away today?

The Refutation: Reason’s Insufficiency and the Role of Prophethood
Refutation of the rationalist argument and proof of the need for prophethood.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why reason alone is insufficient and how divine law through prophethood provides necessary detail and guidance, as illustrated by the physician analogy.

Core Idea
Prophethood details what reason grasps in general and explains what reason cannot reach.

Having presented the skeptic argument, the author now refutes it. The refutation does not deny reason's capacity entirely but shows its limitations. Divine law from prophethood provides the detailed knowledge of worship, punishments, and beneficial actions that reason cannot independently discover. The author uses the powerful analogy of a skilled physician whose expertise saves people from harmful experimentation.

Guided Path
1
Understand the refutation's starting point
The refutation acknowledges reason's judgment of good and evil but highlights that the law (sharʿ) details what reason only knows in general.
So reason is not useless, but incomplete.
2
Examine the physician analogy
Read the analogy of the physician. Why is the physician necessary? What happens if people try to learn medicine through experience?
3
Compare the prophet to the physician
Note the key difference: the prophet’s knowledge comes from God, while the physician’s comes from thought and experience. This makes the prophet even more necessary.
4
Connect to the broader argument about human nature
The final part references the philosophical view that humans are civil and need a just law from a distinguished lawgiver. This shows prophethood is part of divine providence.

The Fourth Group: Denial of Miracles as Sophistry
Presents the fourth group's argument against miracles.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument of those who deny the possibility of miracles and their claim that permitting miracles leads to sophistry.

Core Idea
Denying miracles leads to absurdity, or so the fourth group claims.

The fourth group in the debate on prophethood rejects miracles altogether, arguing that allowing the breaking of natural law opens the door to irrational skepticism. This lesson presents their objection, which is a key challenge to the traditional understanding of prophethood.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note that the passage is incomplete, ending mid-sentence.
Pay attention to the examples they give.
2
Identify the core claim
Find the phrase where they state that permitting the breaking of custom is sophistry. Understand that this is their main reason against miracles.
Sophistry here means a kind of skepticism that accepts absurdities.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الطائفة الرابعة : من قال : بامتناع المعجزة ) فلا يثبت النبوة أصلاً ( لأن . تجويز خرق العادة سفسطة ولو جوزناه لجاز انقلاب الجبل ذهباً وماء البحر دماً ودهناً وأواني البيت رجالاً كملاً ) وتولد هذا الشيخ دفعة بلا أب وأم وكون من ظهرت
The fourth group: those who say by the impossibility of miracles, so prophethood is not established at all, because permitting the breaking of custom is sophistry. If we permitted it, it would be permissible for the mountain to turn into gold, the sea water into blood and oil, the household vessels into complete men, and the birth of this elder suddenly without father and mother, and the existence of someone who appeared...
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage begins the discussion of the fourth group who deny the possibility of miracles, thereby rejecting prophethood entirely. Their argument is that allowing the breaking of natural custom (khariq al-‘ada) leads to sophistry (safsata) and opens the door to absurd possibilities such as mountains turning to gold, water to blood, and spontaneous generation of humans. The passage is incomplete, breaking off in the middle of a sentence, so the full argument and refutation are not present.

Key Terms
المعجزة
خرق العادة
سفسطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Miracles are impossible because they break natural law.
Correction: The fourth group's claim is not that miracles are impossible per se, but that permitting them leads to irrationality. The Islamic response is that miracles are possible as signs of prophethood.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument of the fourth group against miracles?
A) Miracles are rationally necessary
B) Permitting the breaking of custom leads to sophistry
C) Prophets never performed miracles
D) Miracles are only for saints

Key Takeaway
The fourth group argues that accepting miracles leads to sophistry, thus rejecting prophethood.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear about a miracle, do you feel a tug of skepticism because it seems 'absurd'? How does that internal reaction mirror the fourth group's reasoning? What does this reveal about your trust in God's unrestricted power over His creation?
?
The fourth group feared that accepting one miracle would open the door to any absurdity. Where in your own faith might you be setting rational limits on what Allah can do, out of fear of irrationality? Will you repent of that fear and submit to His absolute omnipotence today?
?
Have you ever avoided a difficult command from Allah because you feared where obedience might lead? What is the root of that fear? Do you trust that His guidance coheres perfectly, even when it breaks your expectations? Commit to one area where you will stop rationalizing and simply obey.

The Sabian Argument for Limiting Prophethood
Sabian argument based on rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sabian argument that rational judgments of good and evil suffice to guide human actions, thereby limiting the need for multiple prophets.

Core Idea
The Sabians argued that reason alone can judge actions as good, evil, or neutral.

The Sabians, an ancient religious group, accepted only two prophets: Sheth (Seth) and Idris (Enoch). Their reasoning was based on a rigorous rationalism: actions are divided by reason into good, evil, and neutral. This lesson presents their argument, which forms the backdrop for the Sunni response.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully.
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: the Sabian position is stated first, then their argument.
The passage uses a concise style typical of philosophical theology.
2
Identify the three categories of actions.
The Sabians claim that intellect judges some actions as good (حسن), some as evil (قبح), and some as neither. They then prescribe what to do in each case.
This classification is central to their argument.
3
Understand the implication for prophethood.
If reason suffices to guide all actions, why would multiple prophets be needed? The Sabians conclude that only those prophets who brought knowledge beyond reason are necessary, which they limit to Sheth and Idris.
This leads to the refutation in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن الصابئة من قال بنبوة شيث وإدريس فقط وهؤلاء كلهم احتجوا بأن ما حكم العقل بحسنه من الأفعال يفعل وما حكم بقبحه يترك وما لم يحكم فيه بحسن ولا قبح يفعل عند الحاجة إليه لأن الحاجة ناجزة حاضرة فيجب اعتبارها دفعاً لمضرة فواتها ولا يعارضها مجرد الاحتمال أي احتمال المضرة بتقدير ويترك عند عدمها للاحتياط في دفع المضرة المتوهمة. والجواب: بعد تسليم حكم العقل بالحسن والقبح أن الشرع المستفاد من البعثة فائدته تفصيل ما أعطاه العقل إجمالاً من مراتب الحسن والقبح والمنفعة والمضرة وبيان ما يقصر عنه العقل ابتداء فإن القائلين بحكم العقل لا ينكرون أن من الأفعال ما لا يحكم العقل فيه بشيء وذلك كوظائف العبادات وتعيين الحدود ومقاديرها وتعليم ينفع وما يضر من الأفعال وذلك أي النبي الشارع كالطبيب الحاذق يعرف الأدوية وطبائعها وخواصها مما لو أمكن معرفتها للعامة بالتجربة ففي دهر طويل يجربون فيه أي في ذلك الدهر الطويل من فوائدها لعدم حصول العلم بها بعد ويقعون في المهالك قبل استكمالها أي قبل استكمال مدة التجربة إذ ربما يستعملون من الأدوية في تلك المدة ما يكون مهلكاً ولا يعلمون ذلك فيهلكهم مع أن اشتغالهم بذلك أي بتحصيل العلم بأحوال الأدوية بطريق التجربة يوجب إتعاب النفس وتعطل الصناعات الضرورية والشغل عن المصالح المعاش فإذا تسلموه من الطبيب خفت المؤونة وانتفعوا به وسلموا من تلك المضار ولا يقال في إمكانه معرفته أي معرفة ما ذكر غنى عن الطبيب فكذا لا يقال في إمكان معرفة التكاليف وأحوال الأفعال بتأمل العقل فيها غنى عن المبعوث كيف والنبي لا يعلم ما يعلم إلا من جهة الله بخلاف الطبيب إذ يمكن التوصل إلى جميع ما يعلمه بمجرد الفكر والتجربة فإذا لم يكن هو مستغنى عنه كان النبي بذلك أولى وفيما تقدم من تقرير مذهب الحكماء وهو أن الإنسان مدني بالطبع فلا بد له من قانون عدل محتاج إلى واضع يمتاز عن بني نوعه بما يدل على أن ما أتى به من عند ربه تتمة لهذا الكلام فإنه يدل على وجوب وجود النبي في العناية الأزلية المقتضية للنظام الأبلغ. قوله: (وهؤلاء كلهم احتجوا إلخ لا يخفى انتقاض دليلهم ببعثة ما اعترفوا ببعثته من الأنبياء المذكورين.
And among the Sabians are those who said only Sheth and Idris were prophets, and these all argued that whatever the intellect judges as good of actions is done, and whatever it judges as evil is left, and whatever it does not judge as good or evil is done when needed because the need is present, so it must be considered to avoid the harm of its loss, and mere probability (i.e., probability of harm by supposition) does not oppose it, and it is left when there is no need as a precaution against imagined harm. The answer: after conceding the intellect's judgment of good and evil, the law (shar‘) derived from sending (ba‘tha) has the benefit of detailing what the intellect gave in general of the levels of good, evil, benefit, and harm, and clarifying what the intellect falls short of initially. For those who affirm the intellect's judgment do not deny that among actions there are some about which the intellect judges nothing, such as the functions of worship, the specification of limits and their amounts, and teaching what benefits and harms among actions. And that is, the prophet-lawgiver is like a skilled physician who knows medicines, their natures, and properties, such that if the common people could know them through experience, they would experiment over a long time—i.e., in that long time—for their benefits, because knowledge of them is not yet attained, and they fall into perils before completing it, i.e., before completing the period of experience, for perhaps they use from the medicines in that period what is lethal and do not know it, and it kills them. Moreover, their occupation with that—i.e., acquiring knowledge of the conditions of medicines by way of experience—causes exhaustion of the soul, neglect of essential crafts, and distraction from worldly interests. So when they receive it from the physician, the burden is lightened, they benefit from it, and they are safe from those harms. And it is not said concerning the possibility of knowing it—i.e., knowing what was mentioned—that there is no need for the physician; likewise, it is not said concerning the possibility of knowing the obligations and the conditions of actions by reflection of the intellect that there is no need for the sent one. How so, while the prophet does not know what he knows except from God, unlike the physician, for it is possible to arrive at all that he knows by mere thought and experience. So if he is not independent of him, the prophet is more deserving of that. And what preceded from the exposition of the philosophers' doctrine—that man is by nature civil, so he must have a just law requiring a legislator who is distinguished from his kind by what indicates that what he brought is from his Lord—is a completion of this speech, for it indicates the necessity of the prophet's existence in eternal providence that necessitates the most perfect order. His saying: (And these all argued etc.) It is not hidden that their argument is refuted by the sending of those prophets whose sending they acknowledged.
Source pages p. 259

Commentary
This passage presents an argument from the Sabians, who accept only Sheth and Idris as prophets, and then provides a refutation. The Sabians reason that actions are judged by the intellect: good actions are done, evil ones avoided, and neutral actions may be done when needed to prevent harm, with mere probability of harm not outweighing the present need. The reply, while conceding the intellect's judgments of good and evil, argues that the law (shar‘) brought by prophets serves to detail what the intellect grasps only in general, such as the specific degrees of good and evil or rituals whose rationale the intellect cannot fully determine. The analogy of a physician is used: people could theoretically learn medicine through long experience, but it would be exhausting, dangerous, and detrimental to society. Similarly, even if the intellect could eventually grasp some obligations, relying on revelation saves effort and prevents harm. Moreover, the prophet knows from God, unlike a physician who relies on human reason and experience, making the need for a prophet even greater. The passage concludes by referencing the philosophical argument that humans are social by nature and require a just law from a distinguished legislator, reinforcing the necessity of prophethood. A marginal note then points out that the Sabians' own acceptance of some prophets undermines their denial of others.

Key Terms
عقل
حسن
قبح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sabian argument, what is done with actions that the intellect does not judge as good or evil?
A) They are always avoided.
B) They are done when needed.
C) They are left when needed.
D) They are never considered.

Key Takeaway
The Sabians argued that rational judgments of good and evil are sufficient to govern actions, thereby limiting the need for prophets to only those who brought unique knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Sabians believed their intellect could judge all actions. When faced with a moral dilemma, do you instinctively trust your own reasoning more than the teachings of revelation? Recall a specific instance and consider which guided you.
?
If you were to encounter a situation where your intellect tells you something is good but revelation says it is harmful, which would you follow? What does your answer say about your reliance on reason versus divine guidance?
?
The Sabians limited prophethood because they felt reason was sufficient. Are there areas in your life where you have effectively 'limited prophethood' by ignoring or rationalizing away the commands of the prophets? Confront that now.

The Reply: Revelation Details Reason's General Knowledge
Reply emphasizing revelation's role in detailing rational knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how revelation complements reason by providing detailed moral and ritual knowledge that reason cannot fully determine on its own.

Core Idea
Revelation details the general rational knowledge and supplies what reason falls short of.

The reply to the Sabians begins by conceding that reason can judge good and evil in general terms, but argues that revelation has a distinct role: it specifies the precise degrees of good, evil, benefit, and harm, and it clarifies matters that reason finds indeterminate, such as the specifics of worship. The answer uses the analogy of a physician to illustrate how a prophet saves humanity from hardship and danger. This lesson unpacks that response.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage.
The reply starts by conceding the intellect's judgment of good and evil, then explains the role of revelation.
Notice the structure: concession, then positive role.
2
Identify what revelation details.
The passage says revelation details what the intellect gave in general: the levels of good/evil, benefit/harm, and it clarifies what the intellect falls short of.
This includes the specific rules of worship and legal limits.
3
Analyze the physician analogy.
The prophet is compared to a skilled physician who knows medicines and their properties. The common people could potentially learn through experience, but it would take a long time and involve danger and neglect of other duties.
This analogy highlights the practical benefit of revelation.
4
Understand the concluding arguments.
The passage also mentions that the prophet's knowledge comes from God, unlike the physician's which is empirical. Moreover, the necessity of a lawgiver is supported by the philosophical argument that humans are social by nature and need a just law.
The final note points out that the Sabians' own acceptance of some prophets refutes their argument.

The Objection of the Similar Person and the Answer on Miracle Possibility
Objection and answer about miracle possibility with a similar person

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Muslim theologians defend the possibility of miracles against the objection that a similar person could appear, using the concepts of habit as a source of knowledge and the analogy with creation.

Core Idea
Habit is a reliable source of knowledge, but it does not negate the possibility of miracles.

This passage presents a clever objection against the evidentiary value of miracles: what if a miraculous event occurs on the hand of a person who merely looks like the prophet? The objector argues this would lead to chaos. The author responds by distinguishing between certainty from habit and logical possibility, and by pointing to the greater wonder of creation itself. Let's examine the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the first part of the passage where the objector presents the scenario of a similar person and the resulting absurdities.
Note the objector’s concern about replacing the true prophet.
2
Identify the answer’s starting point
Notice that the answer begins by comparing miracle to creation – a move that shifts the burden of wonder.
The author argues that creation is more wondrous, so miracles are not uniquely improbable.
3
Understand the role of habit
The author explains that habit is a way of knowledge like sense perception, and that certainty from habit does not make the opposite impossible.
This is crucial: certainty and possibility are different categories.
4
See the conclusion on continuous habit
The passage ends by noting that miracles themselves become a continuous habit over time, making them undeniable.
Also note the definition of miracle: intended to verify the claimant.

المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : في إمكان البعثة
المعجزة على يد غير من ادعى النبوة بأن يعدم المدعي عقيب دعواه بلا مهلة ( ويوجد مثله ) في آن إعدامه فيكون ظهور المعجزة على يد المثل ( ولا يخفى ما فيه. أي في تجويز خرق العادة ) من الخبط والإخلال بالقواعد ) المتعلقة بالنبوة وغيرها إذ يجوز حينئذ أن يكون الآتي بالأحكام الشرعية في الأوقات المتفرقة أشخاصاً مماثلة للذي ثبتت نبوته بالمعجزة وأن يكون الشخص الذي تتقاضاه غير الذي كان عليه دينك إلى غير ذلك من المفاسد التي تنافي نظام المعاش والمعاد. ) والجواب أن خرق العادات ليس أعجب من أول خلق السموات والأرض وما بينهما ومن انعدامها الذي نقول نحن (به والجزم بعدم وقوع بعضها) كما في الأمثلة المذكورة ( لاينافي إمكانها ) في أنفسها ) وذلك كما في المحسوسات فإنا نجزم بأن حصول الجسم المعين في الحيز المعين لا يمتنع فرض عدمه بدله مع الجزم به ) جزماً مطابقاً للواقع ثابتاً لا تتطرق إليه شبهة ( للحس ( الشاهد به شهادة موثوقاً بها ( والعادة أحد طرق العلم (كالحس فجاز أن نجزم ذلك الجزم بشيء من جهة العادة مع إمكان نقيضه في نفسه ( ثم إن خرق العادة إعجازاً ) لنبي ( وكرامة ) لولي ( عادة مستمرة ) توجد في كل عصر وأوان فلا يمكن للعاقل المنصف إنكاره أو فلا يكون حينئذ خرقاً للعادة بل أمراً عادياً . والمعجزة عندنا ما يقصد به تصديق مدعي الرسالة وإن لم يكن خارقا للعادة الطائقة
The First Observatory - The Third Objective: On the possibility of sending. A miracle upon the hand of other than the one who claimed prophethood, by the claimant being made non-existent immediately after his claim without delay, and a similar person exists at the moment of his non-existence, so the miracle appears on the hand of the similar one. And it is not hidden what is in it (i.e., in permitting the breaking of habit) of confusion and violation of the rules related to prophethood and others, for then it would be permissible that the one who brings the legal rulings in different times be persons similar to the one whose prophethood was established by the miracle, and that the person whom you are following be other than the one upon whom your religion was based, and other such corruptions that contradict the order of worldly life and the afterlife. And the answer is that the breaking of habits is not more wondrous than the first creation of the heavens and the earth and what is between them, and than their non-existence which we affirm. And the certainty of the non-occurrence of some of them, as in the mentioned examples, does not contradict their possibility in themselves. And that is as in perceptible things, for we are certain that the occurrence of a specific body in a specific place does not prevent the supposition of its non-existence, with our certainty of it, a certainty conforming to reality, established, no doubt reaching it, due to sense which bears witness with trusted testimony. And habit is one of the ways of knowledge, like sense, so it is possible for us to have that certainty about something from the aspect of habit while its opposite is possible in itself. Then, the breaking of habit as a miracle for a prophet and as a marvel for a saint is a continuous habit that exists in every age and time, so a fair-minded rational person cannot deny it, or then it is not a breaking of habit but a customary matter. And the miracle according to us is what is intended to verify the claimant of messengership, even if it is not a breaking of the prevailing habit.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer unit. The objector argues that if a miracle could appear on the hand of a person similar to the claimant of prophethood (e.g., the claimant is destroyed and a look-alike appears), then many absurd consequences follow: the true prophet might be replaced by an impostor, and the religion could be based on the wrong person. This would undermine both worldly order and salvation. The answer begins by asserting that the breaking of habits (miracles) is no more wondrous than the original creation of the heavens and earth or their annihilation, which Muslims affirm. The impossibility of some miraculous events does not negate their possibility in themselves. The author uses the analogy of sense perception: we are certain that a particular body exists in a particular place, but it is not impossible that it could be elsewhere; our certainty from sense or habit does not prevent the logical possibility of the opposite. Furthermore, miracles and saintly marvels have occurred continuously in every age, so they become a 'continuous habit' themselves—a rational person cannot deny them. The passage concludes by defining a miracle as that which is intended to confirm a claimant's truthfulness, even if it is not strictly beyond habitual occurrence (i.e., some miracles may be of a type that recurs).

العادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the certainty from habit excludes the possibility of its opposite.
Correction: The passage clarifies that habit is a source of knowledge but does not negate the logical possibility of different events. Certainty from habit does not mean the opposite is impossible.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument the author uses to answer the objection about the similar person?
A) Miracles are impossible and cannot occur.
B) The breaking of habit is less wondrous than original creation, and habit is a source of knowledge that does not negate possibility.
C) The similar person does not exist, so the objection is invalid.
D) The objection only applies to saints, not prophets.

Key Takeaway
Habit is a source of knowledge but does not make the opposite impossible; miracles are rationally possible and their continuous occurrence makes them undeniable.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you rely on the regularity of your daily life—such as the sun rising, your health, or your provision—do you recognize that these are habits established by God, and that He could interrupt them at any moment? How does that awareness affect your reliance on Him versus on the habits themselves?
?
The passage teaches that habit is a way of knowledge but its opposite is still possible. In your personal acts of worship—prayer, fasting, charity—have these become mere habits that you perform without conscious intention? What would it take to transform them into acts of deep conviction and presence before God?
?
Miracles are said to be a continuous habit across ages. Look at your own life: what seemingly ordinary events—a meeting, a rescue, a provision—have you dismissed as 'just the way things are' that might actually be divine interventions? How can you train your heart to see God's hand in the everyday?

The Fifth Objection: Alternative Explanations for Miracles
The author lists possibilities that could explain miracles without proving prophethood, focusing on sorcery and other causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the multiple alternative explanations for miracles proposed as objections to their evidential value, including differences in essence, temperament, sorcery, talismans, and properties of compounds.

Core Idea
The possibility of miracles arising from natural or human causes undermines their necessary link to divine confirmation.

In this passage, the author presents the fifth objection to the evidential value of miracles. The objector argues that extraordinary events could be explained by several alternative causes unrelated to divine validation. By examining these possibilities—ranging from innate human capacities to sorcery and natural properties—the objection challenges the claim that miracles must be from God. The passage also asserts the reality of sorcery, citing scriptural and consensual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical structure: the objection is introduced, then a series of possibilities are listed.
The text uses 'or' (أو) to connect each sub-possibility.
2
Identify the five sub-possibilities
Under the first possibility ('it being from his own action'), the author lists five specific sub-possibilities: different essence, special temperament, sorcery, talismans, and properties of compounds. Find each one.
The sorcery sub-possibility includes a defense of its reality with Quranic, sunnic, and consensual evidence.
3
Analyse the sorcery defense
The objector affirms the reality of sorcery by citing Quran 2:102, the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam, and juristic consensus. Understand why this strengthens the objection.
This shows the objection does not deny sorcery but uses its accepted reality.
4
Reflect on the cumulative force
Consider how each sub-possibility provides a plausible alternative explanation for a miracle, thereby weakening the argument that miracles necessarily confirm prophethood.
The objection does not prove any of these are true, only that they are possible.

الخامسة من قال : ظهور المعجز لا يدل على الصدق في دعوى النبوة لاحتمالات : الأول : كونه من فعله لا من فعل الله فلا يكون نازلاً منزلة التصديق له الله من وإنما جاز كون المعجز فعلاً له مع كون غيره عاجزاً . إما لمخالفة نفسه لسائر النفوس البشرية في الماهية كما ذهب إليه جماعة فيجوز حينئذ أن يصدر عن بعضها ما لا يقدر عليه بعض آخر منها أو لمزاج خاص في بدنه هو أقوى من أمزجة أقرانه فيقوى به على فعل يعجز عنه غيره وإن توافقا في الماهية أو لكونه ساحراً ماهراً في السحر وقد أجمعتم على حقيقته أي على كون السحر مؤثراً في أمور غريبة كما دل عليه الكتاب كقوله تعالى : فيتعلمون منهما ما يفرقون به بين المرء وزوجه [البقرة:102] والسنة كقصبة لبيد بن الأعصم مع النبي عليه السلام ومن أنكره من القدرية فقد خالف كتاب الله وسنة نبيه وإجماع الأمة أيضاً إذ ما من عصر من عهد الصحابة إلى ظهور المخالفين إلا وكان الناس يتقاوضون فيه في أمر السحر وتأثيراته حتى اختلف الفقهاء في حكم الساحر ، فقال بعضهم: يجب قتله، وقال آخرون: هو كافر وقال الشافعي: إذا اعترف الساحر بأن قتله شخصاً بسحره مما يقتل غالباً وجب عليه القود ولم ينكره أحد فكان إجماعاً، هكذا ذكره الآمدي ( أو لطلسم اختص هو بمعرفته ) يعني إن سلم امتناع السحر في نفسه فلا مجال لإنكار الطلسمات الغريبة التي تؤثر تأثيرات عجيبة فجاز أن يمتاز ذلك الشخص بمعرفة نوع منها لا يعرفه غيره فإذا أتى به ترتب عليه أمر غريب يعجز من هو في عصره . وقالوا : الطلسم عبارة عن تمزيج القوى السماوية الفعالة بالقوى الأرضية المنفعلة وذلك أن القوى السماوية أسباب لحدوث الكائنات العنصرية ولحدوثها شرائط مخصوصة بها يتم استعداد القابل فمن عرف أحوال الفاعل والقابل وقدر على الجمع بينهما عرف ظهور آثار مخصوصة غريبة (أو لخاصية بعض المركبات إذ لا شك أن المركبات العنصرية تستتبع آثاراً عجيبة ( كالمغناطيس الجاذب للحديد ) والكهرباء التي تجذب التبن وكالحجر الباغض للخل فإنه إذا أرسل على إناء فيه خل لم ينزل بل ينحرف عنه حتى يسقط خارجاً عن الإناء وكالحجر الجالب للمطر وهو مشهور فيما بين الأتراك فجاز أن يكون ذلك الخارق الذي ظهر على يد المدعي تابعاً لخاصية بعض المركبات ويكون هو عالماً به
The fifth: Whoever said: The appearance of a miracle does not indicate truthfulness in the claim of prophethood, due to possibilities: The first: It being from his own action, not from the action of God, so it is not in the position of God's affirmation of him. And it is only possible that the miracle be his action while others are incapable of it, either due to his essence being different from all other human souls in quiddity, as a group held, so then it is possible that some of them can do what another cannot; or due to a special temperament in his body that is stronger than the temperaments of his peers, so he is strong enough to perform an action that others are incapable of, even if they agree in quiddity; or due to him being a skilled sorcerer in sorcery, and you have agreed upon its reality—that is, upon sorcery being effective in strange matters—as the Book indicates, such as His statement: 'they learn from them that by which they cause separation between a man and his wife' [Quran 2:102], and the Sunnah, like the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet, peace be upon him. And whoever denied it among the Qadariyya has contradicted the Book of God, the Sunnah of His Prophet, and the consensus of the ummah. Indeed, there was no age from the time of the Companions until the emergence of the opponents but that people exchanged talks about the matter of sorcery and its effects, until the jurists differed regarding the ruling of the sorcerer; some said: He must be killed, others said: He is an unbeliever, and al-Shafi'i said: If the sorcerer confesses that he killed a person by his sorcery in a manner that usually kills, blood-revenge is obligatory upon him, and no one denied it, so it was consensus. Thus al-Amidi mentioned it. Or due to a talisman that he alone knows, meaning if the impossibility of sorcery in itself is conceded, there is no room to deny the strange talismans that produce wondrous effects, so it is possible that this person excels in knowing a type of them that others do not know, so if he performs it, a strange matter results that his contemporaries are incapable of. They said: The talisman is an expression of the mixing of effective celestial powers with receptive earthly powers, and that the celestial powers are causes for the occurrence of elemental beings, and their occurrence has specific conditions by which the preparedness of the receiver is completed, so whoever knows the states of the agent and the receiver and is able to combine them knows the appearance of specific strange effects. Or due to a property of some compounds, for there is no doubt that elemental compounds entail wondrous effects, like the magnet attracting iron, and electricity that attracts straw, and the stone that hates vinegar, for if it is thrown into a vessel containing vinegar, it does not descend but deviates away until it falls outside the vessel, and the stone that brings rain, which is well-known among the Turks. So it is possible that the extraordinary thing that appeared on the hand of the claimant is consequent upon a property of some compounds and he is knowledgeable of it.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth objection to the evidential value of miracles. The objector argues that the occurrence of an extraordinary event on the hand of a claimant of prophethood does not necessarily prove his truthfulness, because there are several alternative explanations. The first possibility is that the miracle is from the person himself, not from God. This could be due to three sub-reasons: (a) a difference in the very essence of his soul that makes him capable of acts others cannot do; (b) a special temperament in his body that gives him unusual strength; or (c) his being a skilled sorcerer. The author then defends the reality of sorcery, citing the Quran (the story of Harut and Marut), the Sunnah (the incident of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet), and the consensus of the community, mentioning differing juridical opinions on the sorcerer's punishment, including a report from al-Amidi. The objector then adds further possibilities: (d) talismans—which combine celestial and earthly forces—if one concedes the reality of sorcery, and (e) special properties of compounds, illustrated by examples like magnetism, electricity, and stones with peculiar behaviors. The overall argument is that miracles could be explained by natural or human causes, so they do not uniquely attest to divine commission. The commentary does not here provide a direct refutation but rather lays out the objection and affirms the reality of the alternative causes.

Correction: The passage actually affirms the reality of sorcery, citing the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus, and treats talismans as plausible.

Checkpoint
What is the first sub-reason given under the first possibility that a miracle could be from the person himself?
A) The person has a special stone.
B) The person's essence is different from other human souls in quiddity.
C) The person uses a talisman.
D) The person is a prophet.

Key Takeaway
The objection to miracles as proof of prophethood rests on a proliferation of possible explanations—essence, temperament, sorcery, talismans, and natural properties—each grounded in accepted phenomena.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read this passage, do you find yourself instinctively defending the miracles of the Prophet (peace be upon him) against these objections? Or do you feel a flicker of doubt? What does that reaction reveal about the strength of your conviction in his prophethood? This week, when you say the shahada, pause and ask yourself: Do I truly believe that no human could have produced the Quran or split the moon except by Allah's permission?
?
The objector here uses sorcery and natural properties to explain away miracles. In your own life, have you ever attributed a clear answer to your du'a to coincidence or natural causes rather than to Allah's direct intervention? If so, what would it take for you to recognize a miracle in your own experience? Commit tonight to write down one event you previously dismissed and reconsider it as a possible sign from Allah.
?
This lesson shows that skeptics can always find an alternative explanation. Are you someone who looks for reasons to disbelieve in Allah's signs, or reasons to believe? Examine your heart: When you hear about a miracle of the Prophet, do you immediately try to rationalize it away, or do you accept it with tasleem (submission)? If you struggle, pray for the ability to see Allah's hand in His creation and in His revelation.

The Reality of Magic and Its Proofs from Revelation and Consensus
Sanusi cites scriptural and communal proofs for magic's reality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Quran, Sunnah, and consensus of the Ummah establish the reality of magic and its effects.

Core Idea
Magic is real, proven by scripture and the agreement of the Muslim community.

Sanusi begins by grounding the reality of magic in divine revelation and historical consensus. He cites the Quranic verse about the two angels (2:102) and the prophetic tradition of Labid ibn al-A'sam, then notes that the Ummah has always affirmed magic's effects, with jurists differing only on its ruling. This lesson extracts the foundational proofs that magic is not mere illusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice how Sanusi piles up evidence: Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the three proofs
Find the Quranic citation, the reference to the Prophet's story, and the statement about consensus. These form the backbone of the argument.
3
Understand the juristic differences
Sanusi mentions that jurists disagreed on the ruling of a sorcerer: execution, unbelief, or retaliation. This shows that the reality of magic was not in dispute, only its legal consequences.

And that his magic separates a man from his wife [Quran 2:102], and the Sunnah like the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet, peace be upon him. So it is possible that the extraordinary event that appeared at the hand of the claimant follows a property of some compounds, and he is knowledgeable about it. His statement: (like the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet, peace be upon him). He narrated that he bewitched the Prophet, peace be upon him, in eleven knots and buried it in a well, so he became ill, and the two protectors (al-Mu'awwidhatayn) were revealed, and Gabriel, peace be upon him, informed him of the location of the magic. So he sent Ali, may God honor his face, and he brought it, and he recited them over it, and whenever he recited a verse, a knot was untied and he felt some lightness. If you say: does this not necessitate that the unbelievers are truthful about him being bewitched? I say: no, because they meant by it that he is mad due to magic, and he is far above that. His statement: (and whoever denies it from the Qadariyya). The Qadariyya said: magic is showing something that has no reality, like sleight of hand, which is caused by the quickness of the hand and concealment of the trick. They argued with the statement of God in the story of Moses, peace be upon him: 'It seemed to him from their magic that they were moving' [Quran 20:20]. It was answered by the possibility that their magic was to cause that illusion, and it occurred. And even if it is granted, then its effect in that form being illusion does not indicate that it has no reality. His statement: (and others said: he is an unbeliever). The obligation to kill does not necessitate unbelief, so there is no problem in the contrast between his statement: 'and others said' etc. to his statement: 'some said: he must be killed', because what is meant is that he must be killed as a prescribed punishment, even if he is not an unbeliever. Reflect.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses the reality of magic and its effects, refuting those who deny it, and then examines talismans and special properties as alternative explanations for extraordinary events. The author begins by citing the Quranic verse about the two angels teaching magic (Quran 2:102) and the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam's magic against the Prophet, establishing that magic has real effects. He then notes that the Qadariyya denied magic, contradicting Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. The discussion then moves to disagreements among jurists about the ruling on a sorcerer: some say execution, others say he is an unbeliever; al-Shafi'i holds that if sorcery causes death, retaliation is due, and this view was not denied, hence consensus. Then the author explains that talismans are a combination of celestial and terrestrial powers, and that special properties of certain compounds (like magnet attracting iron, electricity attracting straw, the stone that repels vinegar, and the rain-bringing stone) produce strange effects, so a prophet's miracle could be attributed to such properties. Finally, he addresses the Qadariyya's claim that magic is mere illusion, responding that even if the effect is perceptual deception, it does not negate the reality of the magical act itself. He also clarifies that the disagreement about whether a sorcerer is killed as an unbeliever or as a punishment does not imply a contradiction.

Key Terms
سحر
إجماع
قدرية
لبيد بن الأعصم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Magic is merely superstition or illusion with no real effect.
Correction: The Quran, Sunnah, and consensus affirm that magic has real effects, such as separating spouses or causing harm.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary purpose of citing the Quranic verse about the two angels?
A) To prove that magic is forbidden
B) To establish that magic is real and taught by angels
C) To show that Moses was more powerful than magicians
D) To argue that magic is a form of disbelief

Key Takeaway
Magic is a real phenomenon confirmed by the Quran, the Prophet's experience, and the unanimous agreement of the Muslim community.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The Prophet himself was affected by magic. If the best of creation was not immune, how can you afford to be careless about spiritual protection? What specific ruqya or du'a will you commit to reciting daily starting today?
?
The Ummah across centuries has affirmed the reality of magic. Where in your life have you let modern skepticism erode your belief in the unseen? Name one matter of the unseen you have rationalized away, and resolve to accept it fully as revelation teaches.
?
Magic can even sever the bond between spouses. Examine your closest relationship right now. Are there any 'invisible' influences—jealousy, envy, neglect—that you have been ignoring? What concrete action will you take this week to strengthen that bond against all forms of harm?

Talismans: Celestial and Terrestrial Forces Combined
Sanusi defines talismans and explains their mechanism of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of a talisman and how it operates through the combination of celestial and terrestrial powers.

Core Idea
Talismans harness celestial and terrestrial forces to produce extraordinary effects.

Having established the reality of magic, Sanusi turns to talismans as another category of extraordinary phenomena. He explains that even if one were to deny magic as a category, one cannot deny the existence of talismans, which are based on a different principle: the mixing of active celestial powers with passive terrestrial elements. This lesson unpacks that mechanism.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of talisman
Read the Arabic passage. Notice that the talisman is defined as a combination of forces. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the components
The talisman involves two elements: the celestial (active) and the terrestrial (passive). The celestial forces are causes for elemental beings, and their effects depend on proper conditions and readiness of the receiver.
3
Grasp the implications for miracles
Sanusi suggests that a miracle-worker could be knowledgeable in talismans, producing wonders through such knowledge. This provides an alternative explanation for some extraordinary events.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الأول - المقصد الثالث : في إمكان البعثة وبأن سحره يفرقون به بين المرء وزوجه [البقرة: 102]، والسنة كقصة لبيد بن الأعصم مع النبي عليه السلام ومن أنكره من القدرية فقد خالف كتاب الله وسنة نبيه وإجماع الأمة أيضاً إذ ما من عصر من عهد الصحابة إلى ظهور المخالفين إلا وكان الناس يتقاوضون فيه في أمر السحر وتأثيراته حتى اختلف الفقهاء في حكم الساحر، فقال بعضهم: يجب قتله، وقال آخرون : هو كافر وقال الشافعي : إذا اعترف الساحر بأن قتله شخصاً بسحره مما يقتل غالباً وجب عليه القود ولم ينكره أحد فكان إجماعاً، هكذا ذكره الآمدي ( أو لطلسم اختص) هو (بمعرفته ) يعني إن سلم امتناع السحر في نفسه فلا مجال لإنكار الطلسمات الغريبة التي تؤثر تأثيرات عجيبة فجاز أن يمتاز ذلك الشخص بمعرفة نوع منها لا يعرفه غيره فإذا أتى به ترتب عليه أمر غريب يعجز من هو في عصره . وقالوا : الطلسم عبارة عن تمزيج القوى السماوية الفعالة بالقوى الأرضية المنفعلة وذلك أن القوى السماوية أسباب لحدوث الكائنات العنصرية ولحدوثها شرائط مخصوصة بها يتم استعداد القابل فمن عرف أحوال الفاعل والقابل وقدر على الجمع بينهما عرف ظهور آثار مخصوصة غريبة (أو لخاصية بعض المركبات إذ لا شك أن المركبات العنصرية تستتبع آثاراً عجيبة ( كالمغناطيس) الجاذب للحديد ) والكهرباء ) التي تجذب التبن وكالحجر الباغض للخل فإنه إذا أرسل على إناء فيه خل لم ينزل بل ينحرف عنه حتى يسقط خارجاً عن الإناء وكالحجر الجالب للمطر وهو مشهور فيما بين الأتراك فجاز أن يكون ذلك الخارق الذي ظهر على يد المدعي تابعاً لخاصية بعض المركبات ويكون هو عالماً عن مثله قوله : (كقصة لبيد بن الأعصم مع النبي عليه السلام روى أنه سحر النبي عليه السلام في إحدى عشر عقدة وتردسه في بئر فمرض عليه السلام ونزل المعوذتان وأخبره جبريل عليه السلام بموضع السحر، فأرسل عليا كرم الله وجهه فجاء به فقرأهما عليه وكان كلما قرأ آية انحلت عقدة ووجد بعض الخفة فإن قلت : هل لا يلزم على هذا صدق الكفرة في أنه مسحور، قلت : لا لأنهم أرادوا به أنه مجنون بواسطة السحر وحاشاه عن ذلك . قوله : ( ومن أنكره من القدرية ) قال القدرية : السحر إراءة ما لا حقيقة له كالشعبذة التي سببها خفة اليد وإخفاء وجه الحيلة واحتجوا بقوله تعالى في قصة موسى عليه السلام يخيل إليه من سحرهم أنها تسعى [طه: 20]، أجيب بجواز أن يكون سحرهم إيقاع ذلك التخيل وقد وقع ولو سلم فكون أثره في تلك الصورة هو التخيل لا يدل على أن لا حقيقة له . قوله: (وقال آخرون : هو كافر وجوب القتل لا يستلزم الكفر فلا إشكال في تقابل قوله : وقال آخرون إلخ . لقوله : فقال بعضهم يجب قتله لأن المراد به أنه يجب قتله حداً، وإن لم يكن كافرا، تأمل .
The First Observatory - Third Objective: Concerning the possibility of the mission. And that his magic separates a man from his wife [Quran 2:102], and the Sunnah like the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet, peace be upon him. And whoever denies it from the Qadariyya has contradicted the Book of God, the Sunnah of His Prophet, and the consensus of the Ummah as well, for there is no era from the time of the Companions to the appearance of the opponents except that people were discussing the matter of magic and its effects until the jurists differed concerning the ruling on the sorcerer. Some said: he must be killed. Others said: he is an unbeliever. And al-Shafi'i said: if the sorcerer confesses that he killed a person by his magic in a way that usually kills, then retaliation is required, and no one denied it, so it was a consensus. Thus al-Amidi mentioned it. (Or a talisman specifically) he (by his knowledge of it) means: if the impossibility of magic in itself is granted, then there is no room to deny the strange talismans that produce wondrous effects. So it is possible that that person is distinguished by knowing a type of them that no one else knows, and when he produces it, a strange matter results that incapacitates those of his time. And they said: the talisman is an expression of mixing the effective celestial forces with the passive terrestrial forces, because the celestial forces are causes for the occurrence of elemental beings, and for their occurrence there are specific conditions by which the preparedness of the receiver is completed. So whoever knows the states of the agent and the receiver and is able to combine them knows the appearance of specific strange effects. (Or due to a property of some compounds), for there is no doubt that elemental compounds entail wondrous effects (like the magnet) that attracts iron, and (electricity) that attracts straw, and the stone that hates vinegar, for if it is thrown into a vessel containing vinegar, it does not descend but deviates from it until it falls outside the vessel, and the stone that brings rain, which is well-known among the Turks. So it is possible that the extraordinary event that appeared at the hand of the claimant follows a property of some compounds, and he is knowledgeable about it. His statement: (like the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet, peace be upon him). He narrated that he bewitched the Prophet, peace be upon him, in eleven knots and buried it in a well, so he became ill, and the two protectors (al-Mu'awwidhatayn) were revealed, and Gabriel, peace be upon him, informed him of the location of the magic. So he sent Ali, may God honor his face, and he brought it, and he recited them over it, and whenever he recited a verse, a knot was untied and he felt some lightness. If you say: does this not necessitate that the unbelievers are truthful about him being bewitched? I say: no, because they meant by it that he is mad due to magic, and he is far above that. His statement: (and whoever denies it from the Qadariyya). The Qadariyya said: magic is showing something that has no reality, like sleight of hand, which is caused by the quickness of the hand and concealment of the trick. They argued with the statement of God in the story of Moses, peace be upon him: 'It seemed to him from their magic that they were moving' [Quran 20:20]. It was answered by the possibility that their magic was to cause that illusion, and it occurred. And even if it is granted, then its effect in that form being illusion does not indicate that it has no reality. His statement: (and others said: he is an unbeliever). The obligation to kill does not necessitate unbelief, so there is no problem in the contrast between his statement: 'and others said' etc. to his statement: 'some said: he must be killed', because what is meant is that he must be killed as a prescribed punishment, even if he is not an unbeliever. Reflect.

Special Properties of Compounds: Natural Explanations for Wonders
Sanusi lists examples of special properties in compounds to illustrate natural wonders.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the special properties of elemental compounds can explain extraordinary events, providing an alternative basis for miracles.

Core Idea
Ordinary compounds have extraordinary properties that can produce seemingly miraculous effects.

Sanusi continues his exploration of alternative explanations for miracles by examining the special properties (khawass) inherent in certain compounds. He gives vivid examples like the magnet attracting iron, electricity attracting straw, and stones that repel vinegar or bring rain. These natural properties show that what appears miraculous could simply be a rare but natural phenomenon known to the miracle-worker.

Guided Path
1
Read the examples of properties
Read the Arabic passage and notice the concrete examples: magnet, electricity, vinegar-repelling stone, rain-stone. These are meant to be familiar or plausible.
2
Understand the implication
Sanusi argues that if such properties exist naturally, then a miracle could be a form of applied knowledge of these properties, not necessarily a suspension of natural laws.
3
Reflect on the apologetic purpose
This argument is used to counter those who deny miracles by reducing them to natural phenomena. Sanusi is not denying miracles but showing that they could be consistent with natural properties.

Source Text

D) Stone that brings rain

Key Takeaway
Extraordinary events can arise from little-known but natural properties of compounds, providing a natural basis for some miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever dismiss unusual events as impossible without considering that nature itself might hold secrets you haven't learned? How can you cultivate humility about what you don't know?
?
If a person performs an extraordinary feat using hidden natural properties, would you be quick to label it a miracle or dismiss it as trickery? What does your reaction reveal about your trust in God's creation?
?
When you encounter something amazing, do you immediately seek a natural explanation or feel threatened that it might undermine your faith? How can you open your heart to see God's power in both natural wonders and supernatural signs?

The Story of Labid and the Qadariyya Refutation
Sanusi recounts the Labid story and refutes Qadariyya denial of magic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam's magic against the Prophet and how it refutes the Qadariyya denial of magic's reality.

Core Idea
The Prophet's experience with magic proves its reality and refutes those who claim magic is mere illusion.

Sanusi now returns to the concrete historical evidence: the well-known story of Labid ibn al-A'sam bewitching the Prophet. He narrates the detailed account of the eleven knots, the revelation of the Mu'awwidhatayn, and the Prophet's recovery. He then addresses the objection that this might imply the Prophet was under the control of magic, and refutes the Qadariyya who argue that magic is only deception.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Labid
Read the Arabic passage. Follow the sequence: the knotting, the burial, the illness, the revelation, the recovery. Then read the translation.
2
Understand the objection and response
The objection: does this prove the disbelievers' claim that the Prophet was bewitched (meaning mad)? The response: no, because they meant he was mad, but the Prophet was only affected physically, not mentally.
3
Examine the Qadariyya argument
The Qadariyya claim magic is mere illusion (sleight of hand). Sanusi responds that even if the effect is illusion, the act of magic itself is real and has an effect (the illusion). The Quranic story of Moses supports this.
4
Clarify the juristic point
Sanusi concludes with a note that the disagreement about whether a sorcerer is killed as a punishment or as an unbeliever does not create a contradiction, as they differ in legal grounds.

(Or a talisman specifically) he (by his knowledge of it) means: if the impossibility of magic in itself is granted, then there is no room to deny the strange talismans that produce wondrous effects. So it is possible that that person is distinguished by knowing a type of them that no one else knows, and when he produces it, a strange matter results that incapacitates those of his time. And they said: the talisman is an expression of mixing the effective celestial forces with the passive terrestrial forces, because the celestial forces are causes for the occurrence of elemental beings, and for their occurrence there are specific conditions by which the preparedness of the receiver is completed. So whoever knows the states of the agent and the receiver and is able to combine them knows the appearance of specific strange effects. (Or due to a property of some compounds), for there is no doubt that elemental compounds entail wondrous effects (like the magnet) that attracts iron, and (electricity) that attracts straw, and the stone that hates vinegar, for if it is thrown into a vessel containing vinegar, it does not descend but deviates from it until it falls outside the vessel, and the stone that brings rain, which is well-known among the Turks. So it is possible that the extraordinary event that appeared at the hand of the claimant follows a property of some compounds, and he is knowledgeable about it. His statement: (like the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam with the Prophet, peace be upon him). He narrated that he bewitched the Prophet, peace be upon him, in eleven knots and buried it in a well, so he became ill, and the two protectors (al-Mu'awwidhatayn) were revealed, and Gabriel, peace be upon him, informed him of the location of the magic. So he sent Ali, may God honor his face, and he brought it, and he recited them over it, and whenever he recited a verse, a knot was untied and he felt some lightness. If you say: does this not necessitate that the unbelievers are truthful about him being bewitched? I say: no, because they meant by it that he is mad due to magic, and he is far above that. His statement: (and whoever denies it from the Qadariyya). The Qadariyya said: magic is showing something that has no reality, like sleight of hand, which is caused by the quickness of the hand and concealment of the trick. They argued with the statement of God in the story of Moses, peace be upon him: 'It seemed to him from their magic that they were moving' [Quran 20:20]. It was answered by the possibility that their magic was to cause that illusion, and it occurred. And even if it is granted, then its effect in that form being illusion does not indicate that it has no reality. His statement: (and others said: he is an unbeliever). The obligation to kill does not necessitate unbelief, so there is no problem in the contrast between his statement: 'and others said' etc. to his statement: 'some said: he must be killed', because what is meant is that he must be killed as a prescribed punishment, even if he is not an unbeliever. Reflect.
Source pages p. 261

Commentary
This passage discusses the reality of magic and its effects, refuting those who deny it, and then examines talismans and special properties as alternative explanations for extraordinary events. The author begins by citing the Quranic verse about the two angels teaching magic (Quran 2:102) and the story of Labid ibn al-A'sam's magic against the Prophet, establishing that magic has real effects. He then notes that the Qadariyya denied magic, contradicting Quran, Sunnah, and consensus. The discussion then moves to disagreements among jurists about the ruling on a sorcerer: some say execution, others say he is an unbeliever; al-Shafi'i holds that if sorcery causes death, retaliation is due, and this view was not denied, hence consensus. Then the author explains that talismans are a combination of celestial and terrestrial powers, and that special properties of certain compounds (like magnet attracting iron, electricity attracting straw, the stone that repels vinegar, and the rain-bringing stone) produce strange effects, so a prophet's miracle could be attributed to such properties. Finally, he addresses the Qadariyya's claim that magic is mere illusion, responding that even if the effect is perceptual deception, it does not negate the reality of the magical act itself. He also clarifies that the disagreement about whether a sorcerer is killed as an unbeliever or as a punishment does not imply a contradiction.

Misconception: The Prophet was under the mental control of magic (i.e., he was mad).
Correction: Magic affected his physical health, not his prophetic intellect or character.
